
--- Page 1 ---

Effects of premixed ﬂames on turbulence and turbulent scalar transport
A.N. Lipatnikov*, J. Chomiak
Department of Applied Mechanics, Chalmers University of Technology, 412 75 Go¨teborg, Sweden
a r t i c l e i n f o
Article history:
Received 30 January 2009
Accepted 3 July 2009
Available online 4 November 2009
Keywords:
Premixed turbulent combustion
Flame-generated turbulence
Countergradient transport
Flame instabilities
a b s t r a c t
Experimental data and results of direct numerical simulations are reviewed in order to show that
premixed combustion can change the basic characteristics of a ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁeld (the so-called
ﬂame-generated turbulence) and the direction of scalar ﬂuxes (the so-called countergradient or pres-
sure-driven transport) in a turbulent ﬂow. Various approaches to modeling these phenomena are
discussed and the lack of a well-elaborated and widely validated predictive approach is emphasized.
Relevant basic issues (the transition from gradient to countergradient scalar transport, the role played
by ﬂame-generated turbulence in the combustion rate, the characterization of turbulence in premixed
ﬂames, etc.) are critically considered and certain widely accepted concepts are disputed. Despite the
substantial progress made in understanding the discussed effects over the past decades, these basic
issues strongly need further research.
 2009 Elsevier Ltd. All rights reserved.

1. Introduction
The problem
of
the propagation of
premixed
ﬂames in
a turbulent gas can be divided into two major subproblems; the
effects of the turbulence on ﬂame speed, thickness, and structure,
and the effects of the ﬂame on turbulence. Starting with the pio-
neering work of Damko¨hler [1] and Shchelkin [2], the focus of
research into premixed turbulent combustion has been placed on
the former subproblem, and the substantial progress made in
understanding these former effects has already been reviewed
in the two volumes of ‘‘Turbulent Reacting Flows’’ [3,4]; in the
textbooks by Lewis and Von Elbe [5], Shchetinkov [6], Williams [7],
Chomiak [8], Kuznetsov and Sabel’nikov [9], Warnatz et al. [10],
Peters [11], Poinsot and Veynante [12], and others; in the review
papers by Chomiak [13], Borghi [14,15], Clavin [16–18], Libby [19],
Pope [20–22], Williams [23,24], Peters [25], Daneshyar and Hill
[26], Law [27], Gu¨ lder [28], Bradley [29,30], Ashurst [31], Heywood
[32], Bray [33,34], Poinsot et al. [35], Poinsot [36], Candel et al. [37],
Klimenko and Bilger [38], Bilger [39], Law and Sung [40], Renard
et al. [41], Kerstein [42], Lipatnikov and Chomiak [43,44], Veynante
and Vervisch [45], Dinkelacker [46], Bilger et al. [47], Kadowaki and
Hasegawa [48], Janicka and Sadiki [49], Westbrook et al. [50],

Pitsch [51], Driscoll [52], and in many other papers of a narrower
scope.
Despite that the importance of the effects of combustion on
turbulence was recognized more than half a century ago [53–56],
and a number of contributions considering these effects have been
made over the past 50 years, this issue has drawn much less atten-
tion in the review literature. The above books and review papers
eitherdo notaddress these effects at all, oraddress themvery brieﬂy,
or restrict discussion to a particular issue (e.g. the balance of
turbulent
kinetic
energy
in
a
ﬂame
is
often
considered
[3,4,12,15,19,33,35,45]), or a particular approach. In 1983, Gu¨nther
[57] reviewed various measurements of turbulence in ﬂames, but
the results of experimental research into the effects of combustion
on turbulence, reported overthe two past decades, have not yet been
reviewed. Turbulent transport in ﬂames, numerical modeling of
turbulence in ﬂames, the characterization of turbulence in ﬂames
are among other issues that have been studied over the past two
decades, but have not yet been the main subject of a review paper.

--- Page 4 ---

The general goal of the present paper is to make up for the
deﬁciency discussed above and to review progress in experimental
investigations, numerical modeling, and physical understanding of
the behavior of ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁeld and turbulent scalar
transport in premixed ﬂames by considering all standpoints
(sometimes contradictory) and approaches the authors are aware
of.
When writing this review paper, we have arrived at the
conclusion that modeling of the effects of a premixed ﬂame on
turbulence is still a major challenge to the combustion community.
Despite the abundance of particular results, the general under-
standing of these effects is poor in comparison with the general
understanding of the effects of turbulence on ﬂames. Many
important issues relevant to the former effects are on the periphery
of scientiﬁc discussions and some basic problems (e.g. what
quantities should be used to characterize turbulence in a premixed
ﬂame?) are not discussed in contemporary literature.
Accordingly, the present paper pursues two speciﬁc goals and
can be divided into two parts. The ﬁrst, particular goal, which is
typical for a review paper, is to introduce the reader to the subject
by considering various methods of investigations, experimental
and numerical data, theoretical concepts, and physical hypothesis
relevant to it. In the corresponding part of the paper (Sections 2–4),
already published results are summarized. This part is more rele-
vant to researchers interested in applications of existing basic
knowledge.
The second, far-reaching goal is to draw attention to some
important issues that strongly need further study and wider
discussion by the combustion community. The corresponding part
of the paper (Sections 5 and 7) is written in a speculative manner by
orchestrating a collision between opposing hypotheses and raising
questions. This part is intended for scientists interested in resolving
basic problems. We hope that it will help the reader to (i) realize
that in spite of the avalanche of scientiﬁc contributions to the
subject, a number of basic issues strongly need further study and
(ii) deﬁne future research areas.
To introduce the problem to the reader, to brieﬂy explain the
basic concepts and terms, to outline relevant issues, and to
provide links between subsequent sections, we will begin with
a background
section that
reports
relevant
analytical tools,
deﬁnes some basic quantities, contains a brief historical over-
view, and summarizes physical mechanisms. Experimental and
numerical data on the effects of premixed ﬂames on ﬂuctuating
velocity ﬁeld and turbulent scalar transport, obtained over the
past 30 years, are reviewed in Section 3. Available models and
important unresolved issues of the subject of this review will be
discussed in Sections 4 and 5, respectively. The paper closes
with
a
physical
appraisal
of
ﬂame-generated
hydrodynamic
effects.
2. Background
We will start this section with a list of balance equations
developed for modeling turbulent ﬂames, because further discus-
sion of the effects of combustion on turbulence requires references
to certain terms in these equations. The reader interested in
a detailed discussion of them is referred to books [3,4,12] and the
recent review paper [45].
2.1. Basic quantities and balance equations
A general set of balance equations that model reacting ﬂows is
discussed in detail elsewhere [7]. Here, we will restrict ourselves to
a less general set of equations that is commonly used to model
premixed combustion. The latter may be obtained from the former
by assuming that [4]
 the Mach number is much less than unity;
 there is no body force;
 the Soret and Dufour effects, pressure-gradient diffusion, and
bulk viscosity are negligible;
 the molecular mass and heat ﬂuxes are approximated by Fick’s
and Fourier’s laws, respectively; and
 the mixture is an ideal gas, i.e.,
pM ¼ pR0T;
(1)
where p, r, and T are the pressure, density, and temperature,
respectively, M is the molecular weight of the mixture, and R0 is the
universal gas constant.
2.1.1. General balance equations
Under the above assumptions, premixed combustion is modeled
by the following balance equations.

The physical meaning of various terms in Eqs. (21)–(23) is
further discussed in Section 4.

2.1.5. Comments
First, modeling of turbulent ﬂames is not reduced solely to
a study of the balance equations summarized above. For instance,
a number of important results have been obtained by using other
methods, e.g. by considering a transport equation for a probability
density function (PDF) [9,20–22], or by running stochastic simu-
lations [31,42], or by developing a conditional moment closure
(CMC) approach [38], etc. The fact that the present paper begins
with an equation list does not mean that the following discussion
will deal only with these equations. In fact, only Section 4.1 will
focus on discussing the above equations, whereas most of the paper
will be devoted to considering other issues such as experimental
data, physical mechanisms, etc. The equations were placed in the
beginning of the paper solely because the discussion of other issues
sometimes requires references to certain terms in these equations.
Second,
to
apply
Eqs.
(18)–(22)
to
premixed
turbulent
combustion, terms B, C (this term is commonly neglected at high
Reynolds numbers), ii–vii, and II–V should be closed. Modeling the
effects of turbulence on ﬂame propagation is associated either with
closing term B or with evaluating the turbulent burning velocity

where the functions F 1 and F 2 are different for different models.
The simplest examples are [3]: F 1 ¼ 1 and F 2 ¼ ~cð1  ~cÞ.
Modeling the effects of combustion on turbulence is associated
with closing terms ii–vii in Eq. (21) and II–V in Eqs. (22) or (23).
The difference in the number of unclosed terms associated with the
effects of turbulence on combustion (a single term B) and with the
effects of combustion on turbulence (10 terms ii–vii and II–V)
clearly shows the overwhelming complexity of the latter problem.
Third, even if models for evaluating terms ii–vii and II–V were
well elaborated and the function F 2 were known, Eqs. (16)–(22)
would not yet be closed. The point is that a typical model that
results in Eq. (39) addresses a hypothetical case of a statistically
planar, 1D, fully developed ﬂame that propagates in a statistically
stationary, uniform, and isotropic turbulence not affected by
combustion. The rms velocity u0
t that characterizes the inﬂuence of
such turbulence on a ﬂame may differ substantially from rms
velocities calculated solving Eqs. (22) or (23), e.g. ðru00
1u00
1=rÞ1=2 or
ð2~k=3Þ1=2. For instance, not only the unburned but also the burned
mixture contributes to the values of the above second moments
within a ﬂame brush, whereas the effects of turbulence on ﬂame
propagation are often associated solely with the upstream turbu-
lence just ahead of the ﬂame front. To stress this difference, we use
subscript t when considering the rms turbulent velocity that
controls the mean burning rate W and turbulent burning velocity
Ut or turbulent ﬂame speed St. Symbols u0 and v0 designate
ðru002=rÞ1=2 and ðrv002=rÞ1=2, respectively. The difference in u0
t and u0
or v0 is further discussed in Section 5.4.
Fourth, the effects of a premixed ﬂame on turbulence can be
divided into indirect and direct ones. The former effects are asso-
ciated with the ﬂame-induced changes in the magnitudes (with the
preservation of the same sign) of some terms in Eqs. (21)–(23). Such
effects are not speciﬁc to combustion and similar phenomena exist
in non-reacting ﬂows. For instance, turbulence decays due to
viscous dissipation [terms III and III0 in Eqs. (23) and (29), respec-
tively] both in ﬂames and non-reactive ﬂows, but combustion can
accelerate this process by increasing the viscosity of the mixture.
The latter, direct, effects are speciﬁc to ﬂames and are associ-
ated, e.g. (i) with terms in Eqs. (21)–(23), which do not appear in
Eqs. (27)–(29) [e.g., term vii in Eq. (21)], or (ii) with different signs
of the same term in reacting and non-reacting ﬂows (e.g. the
phenomenon of countergradient scalar transport discussed in
Sections 3.3 and 5.1).
Fifth, the effects of a premixed ﬂame on turbulence are mainly
associated with the heat release in the ﬂame, whereas chemical
reactions themselves can affect turbulence only by changing the
density and viscosity of the mixture [e.g. there is no reaction term
in Eq. (29)]. Due to heat release, gas density decreases within the
instantaneous ﬂame front and the variations in density affect ﬂow
velocity by virtue of mass conservation. Note that such effects are
not restricted to the ﬂame and products, but may also be
pronounced ahead of the ﬂame, because steady Navier–Stokes
equations are elliptic. For instance, perturbations propagate at an
inﬁnite speed in an incompressible ﬂow [62] so that the pertur-
bation of such a ﬂow at a ﬂame front instantly disturbs the ﬂow far
ahead of the ﬂame.
If the present paper were solely limited to turbulence itself, it
would appear to be more correct to discuss the effects of heat
release on turbulence, rather than the effects of a ﬂame (or
combustion) on it. However, the paper also addresses turbulent
scalar transport, which can be affected by chemical reactions even
in the constant-density case [63], see term vii in Eq. (21). Moreover,
the heat release in a typical ﬂame is conﬁned to very thin zones
[52], the thickness of which may be markedly less than the length
scale of the smallest turbulent eddies. This peculiarity of premixed
combustion makes the problem of the inﬂuence of heat release in
a ﬂame on turbulence much more difﬁcult than the problem of the
effects of distributed heating on turbulence. For the above reasons,
we will consider the effects of a ﬂame (or combustion) on turbu-
lence in the following, bearing in mind that these effects are
controlled by heat release.
2.2. Historical overview
2.2.1. Flame-generated turbulence
Initially, the problem of ﬂame-generated turbulence attracted
attention due to the hypothetical possibility of the self-acceleration
of a ﬂame caused by the feedback effect of turbulence. The ability of
a premixed ﬂame to self-accelerate by changing the ﬂow velocity in
the unburned mixture was theoretically shown by Darrieus [64]
and Landau [65] who discovered the so-called hydrodynamic or
Darrieus–Landau (DL) instability of premixed laminar ﬂames. These
scientists considered the following simple 2D model problem: The
ﬂame is reduced to an inﬁnitely thin surface which propagates at
a constant speed SL and separates unburned and burned mixtures.
The ﬂow outside the ﬂame is laminar, incompressible, and is gov-
erned by non-reacting Euler equations with a density equal to
either ru or rb, ahead or behind the ﬂame, respectively. Jump
conditions on the ﬂame surface are used to close the model. The
linear analysis of the stability of such a planar ﬂame with respect to
inﬁnitesimally velocity perturbations, reproduced in many text-
books [8,66,67], shows that the ﬂame is unconditionally unstable to
perturbations of any wavenumber K, i.e. both the perturbations and
the amplitude of the ﬂame surface disturbance caused by them
grow exponentially with time

normal and tangential, respectively, to the mean ﬂame surface
x ¼ 0, the y-axis is parallel to the mean ﬂame surface, j(t, y) is the
x-coordinate of the disturbed instantaneous ﬂame surface, uz is the
z-th component of the vorticity vector, and a is the amplitude of
the ﬂow velocity perturbation.
Note that the ﬂame generates vorticity in the burned gas,
whereas the ﬂow in the unburned mixture is irrotational. More-
over, the ﬂame generates not only vorticity but also a potential ﬂow.
Indeed, the RHS of the above expressions for ub and vb may be
decomposed as follows: ub ¼ ub1 þ ub2 and vb ¼ vb1 þ vb2, where
subscripts 1 and 2 designate the ﬁrst and second terms on the RHS
of Eq. (40). Then, it can easily be shown that maxjub1(x ¼ 0)j>maxj
uu(x ¼ 0)j, maxj vb1(x ¼ 0)j>maxj vu(x ¼ 0)j, and uzb1 ¼0, i.e. the
ﬂame generates the potential ﬂow ub1 and the rotational ﬂow ub2.
The physical mechanism of the DL instability consists of the
following (see Fig. 1). Due to the ﬂame-induced convergence
(divergence) of the unburned mixture ﬂow upstream of concave
(convex) ﬂame fronts, the unburned ﬂow velocity at the ﬂame
surface, uu,f increases (decreases), whereas the ﬂame speed is
constant. As a result, both convex (toward the unburned gas) and
concave bulges, characterized by uu,f < SL and uu,f > SL, respectively,
grow, i.e. the amplitude of the disturbance increases and causes
further increase in the velocity perturbation.
Although Darrieus [64] and Landau [65] considered laminar ﬂows,
their seminal result is of importance for turbulent combustion too, as
highlightedbyKuznetsovandSabel’nikov[9]anddiscussedinSection
5.3. Nevertheless, the ﬁrst studies of the effects of combustion on
turbulence [53–56] addressed other physical mechanisms.
To the best of the authors’ knowledge, Scurlock [53,54] was the
ﬁrst who put forward a hypothesis of ﬂame-generated turbulence.
By analyzing results of experimental investigations of conﬁned
ﬂames stabilized behind bluff bodies, he assumed that large mean
velocity gradients caused by the acceleration of hot combustion
products
generated
additional
turbulence,
which
markedly
exceeded the approach stream turbulence. The turbulence gener-
ation caused by the mean velocity gradients was well known even
for constant-density shear ﬂows, see term I0 in Eq. (29), and the
effect of the ﬂame was reduced by Scurlock to the change in the
mean velocity ﬁeld. Scurlock also assumed that such a ﬂame-
generated turbulence could increase turbulent ﬂame speed St
similar to the approach stream turbulence and, hence, the sum of
the ﬂame-generated, u0
fg, and approach stream, u0
0, rms turbulent
velocities should be substituted into Eq. (39) instead of u0
t.
Ina subsequentpaper, Scurlock and Grover [56]proposeda simple
expression for estimating u0
fg and a more complicated expression for
evaluating St. The model reasonably well described the early experi-
mental data on turbulent ﬂame speeds, reported by Williams and
Bollinger [68] and by Karlovitz et al. [55], with a substantial contri-
bution by ﬂame-generated turbulence to the calculated St.
Three years after the publication of the ﬁrst aforementioned
Scurlock’s paper [53], Karlovitz et al. [55] reported results of
measurements of the speeds of Bunsen ﬂames stabilized in fully
developed turbulent pipe ﬂows. The ﬂames were enveloped by
a coaxial air ﬂow of comparable velocity to reduce turbulence
generation in the shear layer. The measured ﬂame speeds were
much higher than the values of St yielded by the models developed
early by Damko¨hler [1], Shchelkin [2], and Karlovitz.
To
explain
this
quantitative
difference,
Karlovitz
(i)
put
forward a modiﬁed hypothesis of ﬂame-generated turbulence
which accelerated ﬂame propagation and (ii) associated ﬂame-
generated turbulence with the following physical mechanism: A
turbulent ﬂame consists of thin, wrinkled reacting fronts that
separate unburned reactants from burned products and are
randomly convected by turbulent eddies, thus, forming a turbu-
lent ﬂame brush [1,2]. The ﬂow velocity normal to an instanta-
neous ﬂame front increases from SL to SL(1 þ s) (in the coordinate
framework attached to the ﬂame front) due to mass conservation.
Since the ﬂame front is wrinkled, the orientation of the ‘‘ﬂame-
introduced’’ velocity sSL ﬂuctuates so that it may be decomposed
to average and random ‘‘ﬂame-introduced’’ velocities, with the
latter quantity being ﬂame-generated rms turbulent velocity u0
fg.
The ﬂame-generated turbulence ‘‘diffuses against the ﬂow before
the ﬂame front’’ [55], increases ﬂame front surface distortions
and, thus, accelerates ﬂame propagation. Karlovitz estimated u0
fg
to be much higher than the rms turbulent velocity of the
approach
ﬂow
under
the
conditions
of
his
measurements.
Fig. 1. Schematic showing the mechanism of the DL instability.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102

Substitution
of
u0
t ¼ u0
0 þ u0
fg
into
an
expression
for
St(ut0),
developed by Karlovitz in the same paper, allowed him to explain
his own experimental data.
As noted already by Sokolik [69], the rationale for introducing the
concept of ﬂame-generated turbulence was the disagreement
between St calculated using a model not yet validated and St
measured using a method which was later put into question. For
instance, Richmond et al. [70] applied a burner identical to that used
by Karlovitz et al. [55], but improved the method of measuring
turbulent ﬂame speed. Richmond et al. [70] showed that Karlovitz’s
method substantially overestimated St under the conditions of the
measurements, because the cylindrical curvature of the burner and
the divergence of the mean ﬂow in the unburned mixture were not
taken into account by Karlovitz et al. [55]. Richmond et al. [70]
concluded that the results they obtained were ‘‘inconsistent with
existing concepts of ﬂame-generated turbulence.’’ Many other
measurements of turbulent ﬂame speeds performed in the ﬁfties
and sixties yielded values of St comparablewith u0
0 [71–74] and, thus,
did not imply that ﬂame-generated turbulence played a substantial
role in the rate of burning. However, a few researchers, e.g. Koza-
chenko [75], invoked the hypothesis of ﬂame-generated turbulence
in order to explain high ﬂame speeds that they had measured.
Although (i) Scurlock discussed ﬂame-generated turbulence
before Karlovitz and (ii) the experimental foundation of Karlovitz’s
concept was later put into question, Karlovitz is commonly
considered to be the founding father of the concept of ﬂame-
generated turbulence. Indeed, he was the ﬁrst who discussed
a possible mechanism of turbulence generation that was speciﬁc to
combustion, whereas Scurlock considered a ﬂame to be an ampli-
ﬁer of a mechanism well-known in constant-density ﬂows.
Scurlock’s and Karlovitz’s hypotheses called for experimental
investigations of turbulence in premixed ﬂames. Measurements
that used hot-wire anemometers were restricted to unburned
gas. Gross [76] did not observe a signiﬁcant increase in the
turbulent intensity ahead of an open V-shaped ﬂame. Jensen [77]
investigated conﬁned ﬂames stabilized behind a rod and reported
an increase in velocity ﬂuctuations in the upstream ﬂow in the
combustion
case.
He
associated
this
increase with
acoustic
phenomena.
The ﬁrst measurements of turbulence within ﬂames were based
on Taylor’s theory [78,79] of turbulent diffusion. Westenberg [80]
injected helium into a conﬁned turbulent ﬂame stabilized behind
a ﬂame-holder and measured the standard deviation ðy2Þ1=2 of the
transverse distribution of the helium concentration at distance Z
from the source, sufﬁciently short in order for ðy2Þ1=2 to be equal to
Zv0/U. It is worth recalling that the theory [78,79] predicts such
a linear dependence of ðy2Þ1=2 on Z if the distance Z is substantially
less than UsL. Here, U is the mean ﬂow velocity, v0 is the rms
turbulent velocity in the direction perpendicular to the mean ﬂow,
i.e. almost normal to the mean ﬂame brush in the measurements,
and sL is the Lagrangian time scale of turbulence. Initially [80], the
measurements were performed in combustion products far away
from the ﬂame-holder and showed that ﬂame-generated turbu-
lence (if any) was much weaker than expected. Although the
increase in v0 in weakly turbulent ﬂames was twice than in cold
ﬂows, with turbulence characteristics at the ﬂame-holder being the
same in both cases, this difference was in part associated with ‘‘the
total elapsed time during which the turbulence can undergo decay
as the ﬂow moves from the position of the ﬂame-holder to the
point of measurement is considerably less with the ﬂame present
than without due to the acceleration during combustion’’ [80].
When cold ﬂow turbulence was increased from 2.4 to 4.8 m/s, the
effect of combustion on v0 was very weak.
In a subsequent paper, Westenberg and Rice [81] extended
measurement goals and evaluated v0 not only in the products, but
also in the reactants and within the ﬂame brush. In the products,
combustion increased v0 by a factor of 1.25–1.5 at certain distance Z,
but strongly reduced v0 farther downstream. Within the ﬂame
brush, combustion increased v0 by a factor of 1.3–2. No effect of
combustion on turbulence was found in the reactants upstream of
the ﬂame zone.
Denniston et al.2 [82] and Richmond et al. [70] applied Taylor’s
theory to evaluate v0/U by measuring the rms amplitude of random
ﬂuctuations of the instantaneous ﬂame front within ﬂames iden-
tical to those investigated early by Karlovitz et al. [55]. The ﬂame
front was detected using an electronic probe. In high-speed ﬂows
(the Reynolds number Re h Ud/nu ¼ 50,000 and 100,000, where d is
the burner diameter), the values of v0 obtained from the ﬂames
[70,82] were approximately equal to the values of v0 measured
using a hot-wire anemometer in the corresponding cold ﬂows.
Richmond et al. [70] concluded that this result did not ‘‘admit the
possibility of appreciable ﬂame-generated turbulence.’’ Note that
Karlovitz [55] invoked a ratio of u0
fg=u0
0 as large as 7 in order to
obtain agreement between the measured and calculated St at
Re ¼ 50,000 (see Fig. 12, data for natural gas, in the cited paper). At
lower Re ¼ 10,000 and 25,000, the electronic probe indicated ‘‘an
apparent increase in turbulent intensity in the ﬂame over that of
the approach ﬂow’’ [70].
Prudnikov [83] (i) investigated premixed ﬂames stabilized
behind either a bluff body or a grid in a channel, (ii) made photo-
graphs of the diffusion wake of luminous vapors (NaCl) behind
a point source placed in the ﬂow at the exit of the channel, and (iii)
measured the blackening of the photographic plates in order to
evaluate the standard deviation ðy2Þ1=2 of the transverse distribu-
tion of the particles. Then, a ratio of v0/U was calculated invoking
Taylor’s theory [78,79]. In the products, the measured values of v0/U
(v0 was almost normal to the mean ﬂame brush in these experiment
also) were either equal to or slightly larger than v0/U in the corre-
sponding cold ﬂow, depending on whether or not the ﬂame brush
reached the walls before the exit of the channel. Since the mean ﬂow
velocity in the products was higher than in the corresponding cold
ﬂows, v0 in the products was markedly higher than in the cold ﬂow
in all the studied cases. Within the ﬂame brush, the ratio of v0/U
depended on the density ratio. Combustion reduced v0/U at s ¼ 1–3,
but increased v0/U at s > 3. Certain experimental results by Prud-
nikov [83] were explained by Frost [84] by applying the rapid
distortion theory [85].
In a subsequent study, Prudnikov [86] processed data reported
by six scientiﬁc groups leaded by Bespalov, Khramtsov, Lushpa,
Shcherbina, Solntsev, and Vlasov in Russia. Based on the reported
dependencies of the mean ﬂame brush thickness dt on distance Z
from the ﬂame-holder,3 Prudnikov estimated v0 invoking Taylor’s
theory [78,79]. The obtained values were roughly equal to the
rms turbulent velocities measured in the corresponding cold
ﬂows. This result indicates that even if the investigated ﬂames
generated turbulence, the turbulence did not affect the thickness
dt. Moreover, turbulent ﬂame speeds evaluated by Prudnikov [86]
by processing the results of the six above experimental studies,
and also of the measurements by Talantov’s group [87] were of
the order of v0 in the cold ﬂows in all the cases, thus, indicating
that ﬂame-generated turbulence played a minor role in the
propagation of these ﬂames.
2 Karlovitz was a co-author.
3 In the 50s and 60s, it was widely accepted [5,6,55,56,70,82,86] that the growth
of the thickness dt was controlled by turbulent diffusion and, accordingly, the
dependence of dt(Z) followed Taylor’s turbulent diffusion law. Recent data that
support this hypothesis are reviewed elsewhere [43].
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
9


--- Page 10 ---

Howe et al. [88] investigated conﬁned stoichiometric propane–
air ﬂames stabilized behind bluff bodies and evaluated diffusion
coefﬁcients D by seeding a radioactive tracer in the upstream ﬂow.
Since the values of D obtained from the ﬂames were too high to be
explained by weak upstream turbulence, Howe et al. [88] claimed
that combustion generated turbulence due to the mean velocity
shear caused by the ﬂame [Scurlock’s mechanism, see terms I in
Eqs. (22) and (23)].
Parker
and
Guillon
[89]
developed
an
iridium
hot-wire
anemometer in order to measure the characteristics of the ﬂow of
the unburned mixture upstream of an open V-shaped stoichio-
metric methane–air ﬂame with turbulence being generated by
a perforated plate-screen combination placed in the fresh mixture
ﬂow. No evidence of turbulence generated by the ﬂame in the
reactants was found.
Dashchuk [90] investigated stoichiometric methane, ethane,
and propane–air Bunsen ﬂames stabilized by a narrow annular
pilot ﬂame. Both laminar and turbulent ﬂames were investigated,
with turbulence being generated by grids. When the ﬂow at the
exit of the burner was laminar, very tall (20 or more burner
diameters) laminar ﬂames were obtained, despite that the cor-
responding cold ﬂow generated turbulence at 20 burner-diame-
ters downstream from the burner exit. Dashchuk considered this
observation to imply that diffusion of turbulence through the
ﬂame front into the approach ﬂow was negligible and, thus, put
Karlovitz’s
hypothesis
into question. To
further dispute
the
hypothesis,
Dashchuk
performed
similar
experiments
using
a coaxial air jet to generate more turbulence behind the ﬂame.
Schlieren photographs provided by him showed no effect of the
jet on the laminar ﬂame. This result was claimed ‘‘to be powerful
evidence against the assumption of the theory of ﬂame-generated
turbulence that part of this turbulence diffuses back into the
approach ﬂow’’ [90].
In the same work, Dashchuk processed direct time-exposure
photographs of turbulent ﬂames using a recording microdensi-
tometer. The results showed that the width of the ﬂames increased
linearly with distance Z from the burner exit, in line with early
experimental data [70,82]. Dashchuk considered this observation
(i) to imply the independence of v0 on Z, and (ii) to be inconsistent
with Karlovitz’s hypothesis.
Furthermore, Dashchuk [90] was the ﬁrst to apply a laser
Doppler anemometer (LDA) to premixed turbulent ﬂames, to the
best of the authors’ knowledge. These ﬁrst measurements were
restricted to the ﬂow upstream of the ﬂame brush. No ﬂame-
generated turbulence was found there.
After the work by Dashchuk [90], applications of LDA to pre-
mixed ﬂames were rapidly developed [91–94]. The use of lasers
revolutionized experimental research into turbulent combustion
and a number of valuable data on ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁeld in ﬂames
have been obtained over the past 30 years. These data will be
analyzed in Section 3.
Similar to the use of lasers in experiments, the use of computers
to numerically solve Reynolds averaged Navier–Stokes (RANS)
equations revolutionized computations of turbulent combustion
almost at the same time. The pioneering work by Spalding [95]
triggered an avalanche of numerical simulations of turbulent
ﬂames in simple laboratory burners and complicated industrial
cases.
Initially, such simulations dealt with balance equations for mean
quantities, e.g. Eqs. (16)–(18).
To the best of the authors’ knowledge, the ﬁrst attempt to close
a balance equation for the second moment in a chemically reacting
ﬂow was undertaken by Williams [96], who considered a constant-
density case and restricted himself to the 1D Eq. (21). Bray et al.
[97–101] and Librovich and Lisitzyn [102] began studying the
closure problem for Eq. (23) in a variable-density case. Eq. (21) with
variable density was ﬁrst analyzed by Borghi and Dutoya [103] and
by Bray et al. [104,105]. The closure problem for the two equations
is further discussed in Section 4. Note that the pressure ﬂuctuation
terms (v and IV) in the two equations are now considered to play
a very important role, whereas these terms were neglected in the
studies [98–105]. The importance of such a term in Eq. (23) was ﬁrst
shown theoretically by Kuznetsov [106].
2.2.2. Unburned–burned intermittency
The phenomenon of intermittency in shear ﬂows was discov-
ered by Corrsin [107] and Townsend [108] who documented two
different types of signals from a hot-wire probe placed near the
outer edge of a free ﬂow (a jet in Ref. [107] and a wake in Ref. [108]).
The signals showed either sharply or smoothly varying velocity
ﬂuctuations. Corrsin and Kistler [109] and Townsend [110] further
investigated this phenomenon and argued that, in free turbulent
ﬂows, there is a thin wrinkled interface (called the entrainment
interface or viscous superlayer) that separates the turbulent ﬂow
characterized by a large vorticity and the ambient, essentially
irrotational ﬂow. The two types of signals were recorded in the two
types of ﬂows, respectively. Due to the viscous diffusion of vorticity,
see term 20 in Eq. (15), the irrotational ﬂow in the vicinity of the
interface acquires vorticity, which is then ampliﬁed by the straining
caused by the turbulent ﬂow (term 10). As a result, the interface
propagates at a speed of the order of the Kolmogorov speed
uhwðn3Þ1=4 and has a thickness of the order of the Kolmogorov
length scale h ¼ n3=431=4.
Parallel to the above phenomenon, another, internal intermit-
tency of turbulent ﬂows was discovered by Batchelor and Town-
send [111] who documented that the instantaneous dissipation rate
2nsijsij intermittently attained values much larger than the mean
dissipation rate 3. The interested reader is referred to books
[9,61,112] and recent review papers [113,114] for further discussion
of intermittency in incompressible ﬂows.
Premixed turbulent combustion is an example of one more
intermittency, i.e. the intermittency of unburned and burned
mixture separated by a thin, wrinkled instantaneous ﬂame front,
the thickness df of which is much less than the thickness dt of
a turbulent ﬂame brush. The latter thickness is controlled by the
random motion of the front around its mean position under the
inﬂuence of turbulent eddies. The ﬂame front is often assumed to
have the same local structure as a stretched laminar ﬂame.
Accordingly, the front is called ﬂamelet [25]. In the following, we
will use the term ‘‘ﬂamelet’’ exactly in this sense, i.e. we will use
this term to identify stretched laminar ﬂames in a turbulent ﬂow.
Otherwise, we will use a more general term ‘‘ﬂame front’’ (the
world ‘‘instantaneous’’ will often be skipped for the sake of brevity)
bearing in mind that its structure may or may not be reduced to the
structure of a stretched laminar ﬂame.
To the best of the authors’ knowledge, the ﬁrst model of pre-
mixed turbulent combustion that straightforwardly addressed the
unburned–burned intermittency was developed by Prudnikov
[86,115,116] in the 60s. The work by Prudnikov will further be
discussed in the next subsection.
In contemporary literature, the unburned–burned intermit-
tency is commonly described using the approach developed by
Bray, Moss, and Libby (BML) [99,100]. They assumed that the
probability g(t, x) of ﬁnding intermediate states of a gas mixture is
much less than unity at any point x at any instant t. Based on this
assumption, they introduced the bi-modal approximation

2.2.3. Countergradient turbulent scalar transport and negative
turbulent viscosity
In constant-density, non-reacting ﬂows, the turbulent ﬂux of
a scalar q is commonly modeled invoking turbulent diffusion
approximation [61,122]
ru00
i q00 ¼ rDt
v~q
vxi
;
(53)
with the turbulent diffusivity Dt being controlled by turbulence
characteristics. For instance, Dtfk2=3 > 0 within the framework of
the well-known k–3 model [122]. In ﬂames, the signs of ru00
i q00 and
v~q=vxi may be the same, i.e. the transport of the scalar q may occur
in the direction of an increasing ~q. The early, simplest explanation
of this phenomenon is as follows [104]. Since pressure decreases
from the reactant to the product side of a free ﬂame, the pressure
gradient causes a stronger acceleration of the hot, low-density
products than of the cold, high-density reactants. The differential
acceleration results in the preferential motion of the hot eddies
towards the product side, and, thus, to countergradient transport,
which is sometimes called pressure-driven transport in order to
highlight the above physical mechanism.
The discovery of the phenomenon of countergradient scalar
transport in premixed turbulent ﬂames is often associated with the

Prudnikov in Russian in 1960.

countergradient scalar transport today.
Unfortunately, Prudnikov himself did not further discuss this
phenomenon in the sixties, probably because he was mainly

framework of which the second term on the RHS of Eq. (58) (i.e. the
countergradient transport term) did not affect Ut and dt and was of
minor importance. Nevertheless, as before, Prudnikov wrote (see

gradient and countergradient scalar transport, as discussed in
Section 5.1. The role played by countergradient scalar transport in
ﬂame propagation and structure is addressed in Section 5.1.5.
In the late seventies, Clavin and Williams [123] developed
a theory of a wrinkled laminar ﬂame front in a large-scale turbulent
ﬂow ﬁeld and found that, if dt (i.e. the amplitude of the front oscil-
lations in the case studied) does not grow with time, turbulent scalar
ﬂux should be countergradient in part of the turbulent ﬂame. They
have concluded that ‘‘gradient approximations for turbulent trans-
port of reacting species in ﬂames do not have sufﬁcient fundamental
justiﬁcation and must be considered to be of doubtful validity’’ [123].
Later on, Libby and Bray [104,124] analyzed Eq. (47), applied it to
a statistically planar, 1D ﬂame propagating from right to left (i.e.
v~c=vx  0), and noted that ‘‘a small pressure drop across the ﬂame
can lead to’’ ub > uu and ru00c00  0, contrary to gradient transport
approximation. This hypothesis was further supported by closing
and numerically solving the stationary 1D balance equations for
ru00c00 and ru002 [104].
Almost at the same time, Moss6 [125] and Yanagi and Mimura7
[126] experimentally conﬁrmed countergradient scalar transport in
turbulent Bunsen ﬂames.
Theoretical study by Libby and Bray [104] and the measure-
ments by Moss [125] and by Yanagi and Mimura [126] triggered
intensive experimental and numerical research into counter-
gradient scalar transport, results of which are discussed in Sections
3.3 and 4, respectively.
In combustion literature, countergradient scalar transport is
sometimes called countergradient diffusion (CGD). For the sake of
brevity, we will use this abbreviation and also GD (gradient diffu-
sion) in the following. However, it is worth emphasizing that the
physical mechanisms that cause countergradient scalar transport
are totally different from the physical mechanism of turbulent
diffusion and, thus, CGD does not seem to be the proper term from
a basic point of view.
Bray et al. [33,127,128] suggested a simple criterion for esti-
mating whether GD or CGD dominated in a premixed ﬂame.
According to the criterion, GD dominates if
NBh sSL
2Fu0  1;
(60)
where the function F ¼ FðL=dLÞ depends on a ratio of an integral
length scale L of turbulence to the laminar ﬂame thickness dL. The
number NB is called the Bray number.
The ﬁrst evidence of the transition from CGD to GD with an
increasing ratio of u0/SL was obtained in direct numerical simula-
tions (DNS) by Veynante et al. [128] and in experiments by Frank,
Kalt, and Bilger [129,130]. Veynante and Poinsot [131] documented
the substantial inﬂuence of an external pressure gradient on the
transition from CGD to GD, and modiﬁed the Bray number to allow
for this effect. The modiﬁcation was supported by the experimental
data obtained by Kalt et al. [132]. Zimont et al. [133] and Lipatnikov
and Chomiak [134] have stressed that the direction of turbulent
scalar ﬂux changes as the ﬂame develops. This hypothesis is sup-
ported by DNS and experimental data reported by Veynante et al.
[128,135]. The transition from CGD to GD and vice versa is further
discussed in Section 5.1.

2.3. Physical mechanisms of the effects of combustion on turbulence
To conclude this background section, it is worth summarizing
the physical mechanisms relevant to the effects of combustion on
turbulence. Such physical mechanisms can be considered using two
different methods which elucidate different phenomena.
One method consists of studying a simple problem as a physical
model of the effects referred to. The following physical mechanisms
have been introduced using such an approach.
1. Pressure perturbations by a laminar premixed ﬂame may
enhance velocity perturbations in the approach ﬂow (the DL
model [64,65]) and generate vorticity in the burned mixture,
see Eq. (40). However, the ﬂow of the unburned mixture
remains irrotational, i.e. laminar, within the framework of this
model.
2. The difference in the instantaneous normal ﬂow velocities
immediately ahead and behind a ﬂame front contributes to the
turbulence in burned mixture after averaging over random
orientations of the ﬂame fronts. Then, the ﬂame-generated
turbulence diffuses to unburned mixture (the Karlovitz model
[55]).
3. Intermittency and preferential acceleration of the burned
mixture by the mean pressure gradient within a free turbulent
ﬂame (the BML model [104]) can cause CGD, see Eq. (47), and
increase the apparent energy ~khru00
ku00
k=ð2rÞ within the ﬂame if
the difference in ui;b and ui;u is sufﬁciently large, see Eq. (50).
This mechanism appears to contain the Karlovitz mechanism,
but is more general, as ui;b  ui;u is affected not only by the
averaged local velocity jump across the ﬂame fronts, but also by
the different acceleration of unburned and burned mixtures
caused by the mean pressure gradient.
Another method is based on considering different terms on the
RHS of Eq. (23). This method was ﬁrst used by Scurlock [53] to
highlight turbulence production due to large mean velocity gradi-
ents in ﬂames, see term I in Eq. (23).
However, this term may be negative in ﬂames. First, Eq. (49)
shows that the signs of ru00
j u00
k and v~uj=vxk may be the same due to
the intermittency and, therefore, term I may be negative.
Second, this term contains  P3
j¼1 ru002
j ðv~uj=vxjÞ, which vanishes
in the case of a constant-density and isotropic turbulence, but may
reduce the ATKE due to dilatation in ﬂames. For instance, term I is
equal to ru002ðv~u=vxÞ and is negative in a statistically planar 1D
ﬂame. This mechanism of the degradation of ATKE was pointed out
by Bray [97–99].
The transport term II redistributes ATKE within the ﬂame brush,
but does not seem to contribute markedly to the net ATKE in the
ﬂame. Strictly speaking, the integration of term II from the leading
to the trailing edge of ﬂame brush yields ðru0kÞ0  ðru0kÞ1 in the
statistically planar 1D case, where subscripts 0 and 1 designate the
leading and trailing edge, respectively. However, both terms vanish
in isotropic incompressible turbulence commonly addressed in the
theory.
The dissipation term III reduces the ATKE in a ﬂame more than
in the corresponding constant-density ﬂow, because molecular
viscosity increases with temperature. However, if the Reynolds
number Ret ¼ u0L/nu is sufﬁciently high, then the increase in
viscosity increases the scale of the smallest turbulent eddies, but
does not affect the dissipation term III, which is controlled by the
rate of energy withdrawal from the mean ﬂow by large-scale
turbulent eddies [112].
The ﬂuctuating pressure term IV is most difﬁcult to model, as
discussed in Section 4.1.4, but plays an important role in premixed
ﬂames, as hypothesized by Kuznetsov [106] and shown in the DNS
discussed in Section 4.1.1.
The mean pressure gradient term V can either generate or
reduce the ATKE, depending on the signs of u00
i and Vp. For instance,
in a free statistically stationary, planar, 1D ﬂame propagating from
right to left, vp=vx < 0 and term V is positive if ru00
i c00 > 0, see Eq.
(48), i.e. if scalar transport is countergradient. Such a mechanism of
turbulence generation was highlighted by Bilger [137] for diffusion
ﬂames and by Libby and Bray [104] for premixed ﬂames.
As pointed out by a reviewer, the mean pressure gradient could
cause transformation of the internal energy of a variable-density
gas to mechanical energy (the kinetic energy of both the mean and
turbulent ﬂows) under some conditions. Such an effect seems to be
similar to the well-known Rayleigh–Taylor instability of an inter-
face between two ﬂuids of different densities under the inﬂuence of
gravity or acceleration. We are not aware of any study of the issue
raised by the reviewer.
The behavior of various terms in Eqs. (21)–(23) in premixed
ﬂames will further be considered in Section 4.1.1. Here, it is worth
discussing another issue; What terms on the RHSs of Eqs. (22) and
(23) allow us to model the simple physical mechanisms listed in the
beginning of this subsection?
The mechanism hypothesized by Karlovitz et al. [55] highlights
the instantaneous velocity jump across ﬂame fronts. Since such
a jump is caused by the local pressure gradient, this mechanism is
mainly associated with the ﬂame-front contribution [i.e. the
contribution of the last term on the RHS of Eq. (41)] to term IV, but
the mean pressure gradient term V may also contribute to the
velocity jump under certain conditions. For instance, if we assume
that the mean pressure gradient across the turbulent ﬂame brush
and the pressure gradient across the ﬂame front scale as sruu02/L
and sruSL
2/dL, respectively, then the two gradients are of the same
order if Da(SL/u0) is of the unity order. Here, Da ¼ st/sc is the Dam-
ko¨hler number, st ¼ L/u0 and sc ¼ dL/SL are the turbulence and
chemical time scales, respectively.
The ﬂame-front contribution to the ﬂuctuating pressure term IV
appears to model the DL mechanism also. To support this claim, let
us decompose the DL solution given by Eq. (40) into (i) a mean part
by integrating along the y-axis, qðx; tÞ ¼ R yþL
y
qðx; z; tÞdz=L, where
L ¼ 2p=K is the wavenumber of the perturbation, and (ii) an
oscillating part q0ðx; y; tÞ ¼ qðx; y; tÞ  qðx; tÞ. Then, the 1D Eq. (23)
holds, and terms I, II, and V in it can be calculated straightforwardly
using Eqs. (40) and (42), as well as the non-reactive contribution
[i.e. the contribution of the ﬁrst and second terms on the RHS of Eq.
(41)] to term IV. Term III vanishes, because the DL model does not
allow for molecular viscosity. Normalized terms I and V and the
normalized non-reactive contribution to term IV are shown in
Fig. 2. The transport term II is not plotted, because it cannot affect
the net turbulent kinetic energy. Note that these calculations have
been performed for a small amplitude of velocity oscillations to be
consistent with the linear theory.
Although, all three plotted terms are of the same order in the
‘‘ﬂame brush’’ (i.e. the x-range of the ﬂame oscillations at instant t),
the mean pressure gradient term V and the non-reactive contri-
bution to the ﬂuctuating pressure term IV almost cancel one
another (see solid line) so that the net balance of the energy of
velocity oscillations is controlled by term I, which is controlled by
the dilatation part ru002v~u=vx and, thus, is negative. If the ﬂame-
front contribution to the ﬂuctuating pressure term IV was of minor
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
13


--- Page 14 ---

importance, then the velocity oscillations would decay, contrary to
the DL prediction. Eq. (23) can only be made consistent with this
prediction by assuming that the sole term in this equation, which
cannot be evaluated using Eq. (40), i.e. the ﬂame-front contribution
to term IV, plays a key role and serves to increase the amplitude of
velocity oscillations. This simple example allows us to associate the
DL mechanism with the ﬂuctuating pressure term IV. Certainly, this
simple example does not mean that the linear DL theory is sufﬁ-
cient to close term IV in a general case.
On the face of it, the third physical mechanism mentioned above
(the intermittency and preferential acceleration) is associated
mainly with term V in Eq. (23). However, the difference in the
conditioned velocities uu and ub is affected not only by the pref-
erential acceleration of the burned mixture caused by the mean
pressure gradient, but also by the ﬂuctuating pressure gradient.
Indeed, the local velocity jump across the ﬂame front is addressed
by term IV.
Furthermore, let us consider the following simple statistically
1D problem: turbulent mixing of two gases which have the same
densities, zero molecular diffusivities, and occupy half-spaces x < 0
and x > 0 at t ¼ 0. Moreover, u ¼ 0 and the turbulence is isotropic
and uniform, i.e. u02 does not depend on x. On the one hand, in this
particular case, Eqs. (18) and (27) read

For the simple problem considered, gradient diffusion approxi-
mation is acceptable and, hence, ub < uu if vc=vx > 0, i.e. the ﬂuc-
tuating pressure term v0 serves to reduce the ﬂux u0c0 in this
nonreacting case. In ﬂames this term and the mean pressure gradient
term iv can act in the same direction, as shown in Section 4. 1. 1.
All in all, the above simple example shows that the condi-
tioned velocities are affected not only by the mean pressure
gradient but also by the ﬂuctuating pressure term. As far as
higher values of ui;b than ui;u are concerned, the contributions of
the mean and ﬂuctuating pressure gradients are mainly associ-
ated with the preferential acceleration of low-density burned
mixture and the instantaneous velocity jump across the thin
ﬂame front, respectively.
Thus, all three simple physical mechanisms enumerated above
are, at least in part, associated with the pressure gradient terms, e.g.
terms IV and V in Eq. (22). The crucial role played by the pressure
gradient in generating turbulence in premixed ﬂames is also
elucidated by the vorticity Eq. (13). Terms 2 and 3 in this equation
generally serve to reduce vorticity, whereas term 4 can increase it
provided that the directions of the gradients of density and pres-
sure differ from one another. This is not so in a planar laminar
ﬂame, where term 4 vanishes. However, it does not vanish and even
overwhelms the dilatation term 3 in a weakly stretched laminar
ﬂame, where it increases the tangential component of the vorticity
vector in the burned mixture (see Eq. (6.15) in Ref. [141]). The DL
solution also shows that a perturbed laminar ﬂame generates
vorticity in the tangential direction of the mean ﬂame surface, see
Eq. (40). Term 4 is the sole term in Eq. (13) that serves to generate
vorticity in this case, because term 1 vanishes in the equation for
the z-component of the vorticity vector as the velocity vector lies in
the xy-plane.
Within a turbulent ﬂame, the directions of the instantaneous
pressure and density gradients can also be different due to the
random motion of the ﬂame front within the ﬂame brush and term
4 can serve to generate turbulence. For instance, in the DNS by
Treurniet et al. [142], isobars (see thin lines in Fig. 3a) were nearly
parallel to one another and did not follow the deformations of the
instantaneous ﬂame front (bold line). Accordingly, the baroclinic
term 4 in Eq. (13) served to produce either a positive (solid lines in
Fig. 3b) or negative (dashed lines) y-component uy of the vorticity
vector, depending on the angle between the gradients of density
and pressure.
In summary, if a premixed ﬂame generates turbulence in the
absence of substantial shear stresses, then the pressure gradient
terms IV and V should play an important role in Eqs. (22) and (23).
Moreover, shear stresses induced by combustion can also generate
turbulence within premixed ﬂames, see term I, a mechanism
highlighted by Scurlock [53]. However, the same term I can reduce
turbulence due to dilatation and ‘‘negative turbulent viscosity.’’
What terms are of more importance and whether or not a premixed
ﬂame generates turbulence depends on the particular ﬂame
conﬁguration, mixture and turbulence characteristics, etc. Both
Fig. 2. Various terms in Eq. (23) computed for the Darrieus–Landau problem at SL/

turbulence generation by ﬂames and turbulence dampening by
ﬂames have been documented under different conditions, as dis-
cussed in the next section.
It is worth emphasizing one more physical mechanism of the
interaction between premixed combustion and turbulence. In
a constant-density turbulent ﬂow, scalar gradients are well known
to be preferentially aligned with the direction of the most
compressive principal strain rate [i.e., the lowest, negative eigen-
value of the ﬂuctuating strain rate tensor sij deﬁned by Eq. (32)]
[143–150].
Accordingly,
iso-scalar
surfaces
are
preferentially
normal to this direction. As a result, turbulence compresses iso-
scalar surfaces, produces scalar gradients, and increases scalar
dissipation. Mathematically, these physical processes manifest
themselves in the large positive value of the mean stretching term
2rDðvc=vxiÞsijðvc=vxjÞ in the balance equation for the mean scalar
dissipation rate cc deﬁned by Eq. (26). At high Reynolds numbers,
this production term is one of the two dominating terms in the
cc-equation [150].
In premixed turbulent ﬂames, the above alignment may not hold.
By analyzing DNS data, Swaminathan et al. [151–153] have shown
that the scalar gradient in the instantaneous ﬂame front may pref-
erentially be aligned with the direction of the most expansive strain
rate (i.e., the largest, positive eigenvalue of the tensor sij), with the
effect being more pronounced at high Damko¨hler numbers (cf. Fig. 4a
and d in Ref. [152]) and at higher values of c (cf. lines computed for
c ¼ 0.1 and c  0.3 in these Fig. 4a and d). This alignment is a result of
the dilatation within the ﬂame front [152]. A similar preferential
alignment was also observed in DNS by Mura et al. [154].
Dilatation tends to increase the distance between iso-scalar
surfaces
and,
thus,
counteracts
the
compressive
strain
rate
produced
by turbulent eddies. Due
to
dilatation,
the most
compressive principal strain rate may be positive (i.e. expansive)
within a premixed turbulent ﬂame brush [151]. In the limit case of
Da/N and Ka/0, the dilatation-induced velocity gradient is
much higher than velocity gradients produced by turbulent eddies
and the instantaneous ﬂame front is always normal to the direction
of the most expansive strain rate, which is controlled by dilatation.
At ﬁnite Da[1 and Ka  1, the dilatation-induced velocity
gradient may be so large that the most compressive strain rate
produced by turbulent eddies at the leading edge of the instanta-
neous ﬂame front becomes the most expansive strain rate in the
region of maximum dilatation.
Recent experiments by Hartung et al. [155] have indicated
the preferential alignment of the instantaneous ﬂame front
(associated with a line characterized by ‘‘the maximum gradient
in the OH ﬁeld’’) with the most expansive strain rate even in
intense turbulence characterized by u0/SL as large as 40 and
Ka > 1.
The orientation of scalar gradients with respect to strain rate
appears to be of substantial importance for modeling premixed
turbulent combustion. Indeed, the dominant role played by the
small-scale eddies in the growth of the area of the instanta-
neous
ﬂame
front
is
often
emphasized
by
invoking
two
features
of
constant-density
turbulent
ﬂows
and
these
features are associated with the preferential alignment of scalar
gradients with the direction of the most compressive principal
strain rate.
First, small-scale eddies are well known to stretch a material
surface in a constant-density ﬂow and increase the surface area
exponentially with time [143,147,149]. The preferential alignment
of the normal to this surface with the direction of the compressive
strain rate appears to play a substantial role in the exponential
growth rate of the surface area.
Second, if mixing in a constant-density turbulent ﬂow is
considered, a surface characterized by a large scalar gradient
evolves like a material surface [145], i.e. its area grows expo-
nentially with time [146]. Ashurst et al. [146] reported that the
lowest difference in the velocities of a material surface and of an
iso-scalar surface was observed in their constant-density DNS
when the scalar gradient was ‘‘aligned in the compressive
direction’’.
If, however, scalar gradients are preferentially aligned in the
direction of the most expansive strain rate in premixed ﬂames,
then, the role played by the small-scale turbulent eddies in the
growth of the area of the instantaneous ﬂame-front surface is
worth examining. For instance, the aforementioned mean stretch-
ing term 2rDðvc=vxiÞsijðvc=vxjÞ, which models the effects of small-
scale turbulent stretching on scalar dissipation, may be negative
and may serve to reduce cc in premixed ﬂames [151–153], contrary
to constant-density ﬂows.8 In other words, small-scale stretching
reduces the scalar dissipation in premixed ﬂames, but produces cc
in constant-density turbulence. It is worth remembering that the
mean reaction rate (and, hence, turbulent burning velocity) is
directly proportional to cc within the framework of the BML
approach [160].
The discussed alignment induced by heat-release in premixed
ﬂames [151–153,155] and the DL instability also caused by heat
release, may affect Ut in opposite directions. The DL instability is
commonly
expected
to
increase
turbulent
burning
velocity,
whereas the preferential alignment of scalar gradients in premixed

ﬂames in the direction of the most expansive strain rate may reduce
the scalar dissipation and, therefore, Ut.
Finally, a question raised by a reviewer is of great interest and
importance, but is nevertheless very poorly understood. Does
ﬂame-generated
turbulence
have
‘‘a
similar
scaling
with
conventional Kolmogorov turbulence’’? The rationale for this
question seems to be as follows: The above discussion shows the
important role played by such a small-scale effect as the inﬂu-
ence of heat release, density drop, dilatation, and pressure
gradient,
all
localized
at
thin
ﬂame
fronts,
on
small-scale
turbulent eddies. If this small-scale effect contributes substan-
tially to ﬂame-induced velocity ﬂuctuations, then, the physical
mechanism of the generation of these ﬂuctuations is very
different from the physics of constant-density turbulence. It is
worth remembering that, according to the theory of Kolmogorov
turbulence [161], large-scale eddies withdraw energy from the
mean ﬂow and, then, this energy is transported due to the
vortex stretching mechanism to smaller and smaller eddies.
Finally, the energy is converted into thermal energy by the
smallest eddies of the Kolmogorov scale. Thus, for constant-
density turbulence, the energy source is associated with the low-
frequency part of the velocity spectrum, while the source of the
energy
of
the
ﬂame-induced
velocity
ﬂuctuations
may
be
distributed
over
a
smaller
scale
range
of
the
spectrum.
Accordingly,
ﬂame-induced
velocity
ﬂuctuations
may
differ
substantially from Kolmogorov turbulence.
This
issue
was
theoretically
studied
by
Kuznetsov,
as
reviewed elsewhere [9]. In particular, Kuznetsov obtained a sca-
ling
for
‘‘the
conditionally
averaged
structure
function’’
DðbÞ
uu ðlÞ ¼ ½uðxÞ  uðx þ lÞ2
b=3fln2 l (see Eq. (6.34) in the cited
book), which differed substantially from the counterpart Kolmo-
gorov scaling Duu(l) f (3l)2/3. The present authors are not aware of
any experimental or DNS research into the scaling of the spectral
characteristics of ﬂame-induced velocity ﬂuctuations. A few spectra
of velocity ﬂuctuations measured within and behind premixed
turbulent ﬂames are discussed in Section 3.2.4. These data support
the importance of the discussed issue, but are not sufﬁcient to draw
a solid conclusion.
It is worth noting that since the focus of premixed turbulent
combustion theory is on predicting burning rate, the effects of
combustion on turbulent eddies immediately ahead of the instan-
taneous ﬂame fronts appear to be of more importance than the
effects of combustion on velocity ﬂuctuations in burned gas,
because it is the former eddies that stretch the fronts and increase
their surface area. The present authors are not aware of any study
that casts solid doubts on the applicability of Kolmogorov scaling to
modeling these eddies. Note that ﬂow perturbations induced by the
DL instability of the ﬂame front are potential in the unburned
mixture. They can only contribute to turbulence after a long
transformation time and, thus, do not seem to substantially affect
the Kolmogorov scaling in the unburned gas upstream and within
the ﬂame brush.
Certainly, as pointed out by a reviewer, the inﬂuence of
combustion on turbulence in products, i.e. behind the mean ﬂame
brush, is of interest for studying some important problems, such as
heat transport, pollutant (e.g. nitrogen oxide) formation in slow
chemical reactions, mixing of combustion products with ambient
air, etc. These issues are beyond the scope of the present review
paper which focuses on processes localized to the mean ﬂame
brush. Without understanding these processes, the prediction of
turbulence in products does not seem to be possible and the
former task appears to be much more difﬁcult than the latter.
Nevertheless, certain data on turbulence in products are reported
in Section 3.2.1.
3. Experimental and DNS data on the effects of premixed
combustion on turbulence
Experimental and DNS (we consider DNS to be a numerical
experiment rather than modeling) data on the behaviorof turbulence
characteristics and turbulent scalar ﬂux in premixed ﬂames will be
reviewed in Sections 3.2 and 3.3, respectively. However, before doing
so, it is worth discussing the validity and limitations of the BML
approach in premixed turbulent ﬂames, because Eqs. (41)–(52) are
widely used to interpret the aforementioned data. Accordingly,
experimental and DNS results that allow us to assess the accuracy of
the BML approximation are reviewed in Section 3.1.
Conditions and methods of experimental and DNS studies that
are addressed in the present section are brieﬂy reported in Tables 1
and 2, respectively. It is worth emphasizing that the two Tables
summarize solely work directly relevant to the subject of the
present paper.
Since particular features of the inﬂuence of combustion on
turbulence are sensitive to ﬂame geometry, we have divided the
experimental data in Table 1 into six groups with different groups
associated with different ﬂame types. The ﬁrst ﬁve groups
encompass data obtained from unconﬁned ﬂames, in particular, (a)
Bunsen or conical (i.e. rim-stabilized or piloted jet) ﬂames (see
Fig. 4), (b) V-shaped (rod-stabilized) ﬂames, (c) impinging jet
ﬂames, (d) swirl-stabilized ﬂames, and (e) other unconﬁned ﬂames.
The sixth group involves data obtained from conﬁned ﬂames.
Within each group in Table 1 and in all of Table 2, we have
arranged the contributions chronologically. However, because
a burner or a code was sometimes used for measurements or DNS
by a research group during several years, we have put together
investigations that share the same rig or the same computer code,
e.g. see measurements by Yoshida et al. [162–166] in Table 1.
Ratios of u0/SL reported in Table 1 differ sometimes from the
ratios reported in the original papers, because we have used recent
experimental data on laminar ﬂame speeds, e.g. SL(F) measured by
Bosschaart and de Goey [297] and Vagelopoulos and Egolfopoulos
Fig. 4. Various types of premixed turbulent ﬂames. (a) Bunsen or conical ﬂame,
(b) V-shaped ﬂame, (c) impinging jet ﬂame, and (d) swirl-stabilized ﬂame.

Sometimes, data reported in the original papers were not
sufﬁcient to evaluate Ret, Da, and Ka straightforwardly by using Eq.
(67). Since these numbers are mainly reported for the purpose of
illustration, we have not invoked a model for estimating them in
such cases and have retained empty places in the Tables, e.g. see
measurements by Moreau et al. [247,248]. Sometimes, data
necessary to evaluate Ret, Da, and Ka were not reported in the
earlier papers by a research group, but were provided in a later
paper by the same group. In such cases, we used the latter data to
estimate Ret, Da, and Ka in the earlier experiments. Results of such
estimates are marked with stars, e.g. see measurements by Bill et al.
[203,204].
Subscript zero in Table 2 indicates the initial characteristics (u00
and L0) of decaying turbulence.
Many DNS summarized in Table 2 were performed invoking the
simplest
single-step,
single-reactant
chemical
scheme,
while
certain DNS used more detailed schemes. A comparison of data
simulated with the different schemes could shed some light on the
impact of combustion chemistry on turbulence (an issue empha-
sized by a reviewer). Unfortunately, we have not noticed any rele-
vant trend when analyzing the DNS data.
Finally, it is worth noting that this section focuses on experi-
mental and DNS data, whereas the methods of measurements and
simulations are beyond the scope of the present paper. The readers
interested in experimental methods of premixed turbulent ﬂame
studies are referred to review papers by Driscoll [52], Gu¨nther [57],
and Kohse-Ho¨inghaus et al. [299]. The DNS of premixed turbulent
ﬂames was reviewed by Poinsot et al. [35,36].
3.1. Thin ﬂame-front regime of premixed turbulent combustion
Within the framework of classical thermal theory [67], a pre-
mixed laminar ﬂame can be divided into two zones, (i) a preheat
zone characterized by a negligible reaction rate, and (ii) a much
thinner reaction zone characterized by negligible convection but
high conduction and reaction rates. Accordingly, we may imagine
that small-scale turbulent eddies are too large to enter the reaction
zone, but are sufﬁciently small to penetrate into the preheat zone
and to substantially increase its thickness. Such a thin reaction zone
regime of premixed turbulent combustion (i.e. chemical reactions
that control the heat release rate are conﬁned to thin inherently
laminar zones) was brieﬂy mentioned in the literature (e.g. see

A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102

--- Page 20 ---

Ref. [121]) and was discussed in detail by Peters [11,300]. Experi-
mental data obtained using the most advanced diagnostic tech-
niques support the hypothesis of a thin reaction zone. For instance,
in a very recent and comprehensive review paper [52], Driscoll
claims that, ‘‘thin ﬂamelets exist for nearly all cases for which
images of the reaction zone have been obtained.’’
It is worth noting, however, that the existence of the thin
reaction zone regime is of minor importance for the main subject of
the present review paper. Much more important are the following
issues: Does the BML model, particularly Eqs. (41)–(52), work well
in premixed turbulent ﬂames? Are the local density changes (rather
than the aforementioned reactions!) conﬁned to thin zones?
Therefore, in this subsection, the discussion is focused on the
classical thin ﬂame-front regime of premixed turbulent combustion
(i.e. unburned and burned mixture are separated by a ﬂame front
that is thin, its other properties being of minor importance), rather
than on the speciﬁc thin reaction zone regime, where the preheat
zone may be thick. Readers interested in the recent experimental
research into the thin reaction zone regime are referred to the
review paper by Driscoll [52]. The problem of the internal structure
of the ﬂame front in a turbulent ﬂow (e.g. whether or not studies of
stretched laminar ﬂames allow us to approximate the front struc-
ture) is also beyond the scope of the following discussion.
There are ﬁve categories of experimental data that are used to
assess the thin ﬂame-front assumption and the BML approach.
First, the Rayleigh-scattering [179,181,182,209,215] and Mie-
scattering [170,173,183,224,232,233,257] signals recorded from
various premixed turbulent ﬂames resemble random telegraph
signals, i.e. the transition from high to low light intensities associ-
ated with unburned and burned mixture occurs very fast in
comparison with the mean time intervals during which the
intensity is either high or low, e.g. see Fig. 5. Such signals support
the thin ﬂame-front assumption.
Second, PDFs obtained from various premixed turbulent ﬂames
look like a bi-modal function, which has two peaks associated with
unburned and burned mixture and a plateau in the intermediate
range, e.g. see curve c in Fig. 6.
In particular, the bi-modal PDFs of the Rayleigh-scattering
signal characterizing the local gas density were documented by
Waldherr et al. [172], by O’Young and Bilger [191], by Chen and

Bilger [192,194], by Pfadler et al. [200], by Bill et al. [203,204], by
Namazian et al. [205,208], by Gouldin and Dandekar [214], by
Knaus et al. [221], by Azzazy et al. [216], by Shepherd et al. [237],
and by Gulati and Driscoll [242]. The bi-modal PDFs of the inten-
sity of the Mie-scattering signal were recorded by Moss [125] and
by Shepherd et al. [257]. Recently, Troiani et al. [246] reported the
bi-modal PDF of an OH LIF signal from highly turbulent bluff-body
stabilized ﬂames (see Fig. 6 in the cited paper).
The bi-modal temperature PDFs were documented by Yoshida
et al. [163,165–167], by Tanaka and Yanagi [168], by Bradley et al.
[217], by Magre et al. [249], by Lewis and Moss [256], and by Katsuki
et al. [259].
The bi-modal PDFs of ﬂow velocity were documented by Yanagi
and Mimura [126], by Cheng et al. [207], by Go¨kalp et al. [211], by
Dandekar and Gouldin [213], by Rajan et al. [215], by Chan et al.
[232], by Gulati and Driscoll [242], and by Veynante et al. [135].
The bi-modal PDFs of the combustion progress variable were also
obtained invarious DNS studies [263,264,266,270,271,283,284,295].
It is worth noting that the bi-modal form of PDFs is typically more
pronounced in DNS than in measurements (cf. Figs. 6 and 7), while
Mantel and Bilger [266] observed a broad peak on the unburned gas
side (c < 0.3) by processing the DNS database by Trouve´ and Poinsot
[265].
However, manyexperiments indicate that the probabilityof ﬁnding
intermediate products may be substantial even though the measured
PDFs have a bi-modal form [165,175,192,194,214,216,249,259]. For
instance, Chen and Bilger [192,194] and Azzazy et al. [216] have
reported the probability g [see Eq. (41)] as large as 0.2 and 0.4,
respectively, while the PDFs measured by them were similar to the bi-
modal functions. Chen and Bilger [194] have claimed that the value of
g z 0.2 casts ‘‘a strong doubt in the validity of’’ the thin ﬂame-front
assumption. An increase in the probability g by u0/SL has also been
documented in various ﬂames [167,175,259] (cf. curves c, b, and a in
Fig. 6), as well as clearly non-bi-modal PDFs [162,167,260] (e.g. see
curve a).
Cheng [233] has reported that while the joint PDF of two
velocity components obtained from the stoichiometric methane–
air swirl-stabilized ﬂame is bi-modal, the bi-modality is less
distinct in lean ﬂames (F ¼ 0.7–0.9) characterized by a higher u0/SL,
and the two peaks merge together in a very lean (F ¼ 0.6) ﬂame
characterized by the highest u0/SL.
The PDF measurements brieﬂy reviewed above imply that (i)
the PDFs are bi-modal in many ﬂames, in line with the BML
approach, but (ii) the validity of the approach deteriorates when
the ratio of u0/SL increases. We may note that Be´dat and Cheng
[234] have documented well-pronounced bi-modal PDFs of the
normalized Rayleigh-scattering signal in a highly turbulent (u0/
SL ¼ 21, Ka ¼ 13) swirl-stabilized methane–air ﬂame (see Fig. 8).
Moreover, certain DNS of premixed ﬂame propagation in intense
turbulence also yielded the bi-modal PDFs at high values of u0/SL
and Ka [264,284].
It is worth emphasizing, however, that obtaining a bi-modal
PDF from a ﬂame is not sufﬁcient to claim that the BML approach
properly describes this ﬂame. As already discussed, the proba-
bility g may be signiﬁcant even if a PDF has a bi-modal form
[165,175,192,194,214,216,249,259].
Therefore,
straightforward
tests of various BML equations are necessary to assess this
approach.

with

by

the

to

the

of

Several hypotheses were put forward in order to explain the
opposite trends measured by Dinkelacker et al. [190,218,262] and
by Mansour et al. [185–188], but this controversy still appears to be
unresolved.9 The interested reader is referred to papers [46,52,193]
for further discussion.
The DNS by El Tahry et al. [264] have shown no notable effects of
turbulent eddies on instantaneous mass fraction proﬁles at weak
turbulence, but indicate a broadening of the preheat zone by a more
intense turbulence. Sankaran et al. [296] have also reported
a broadening of the preheat zone, with jVcj(c ¼ 0.2) being two times
lower in the turbulent than in the laminar ﬂame. The PDF P(jVcj,
c ¼ 0.5) computed by Sankaran et al. [296] was broad with both
Df < DL and Df > DL being observed (see Fig. 4 in the cited paper).
Thinning of instantaneous ﬂame fronts were reported in several
DNS [268,275,276]. In particular, the most probable values of Df,
computed by Tanahashi et al. [275,276], were lower than DL by
approximately 20%, thus implying the thinning of ﬂame fronts in
turbulent ﬂows. However, since the computed PDFs P(Df) were
broad, a ratio of Df/DL as large as 3 was also observed.
In a highly turbulent ﬂame simulated by Hawkes and Chen
[292], the maximum value of the mean jVcj conditioned to the local
combustion progress variable was slightly larger (see Fig. 11 in the
cited paper) than the maximum value of jVcj in the corresponding
laminar ﬂame. Similar results were obtained by Chakraborty and
Klein [288] at Le ¼ 1, but turbulence increased (decreased) the
above maximum gradient if L ¼ 0.8 (Le ¼ 1.2).
By processing the DNS database by Trouve´ and Poinsot [265],
Mantel and Bilger [266] computed an increase in the normalized

production of spatial gradients by the small-scale turbulent eddies
that contorted iso-scalar surfaces within the ﬂame front.10 For non-
reacting turbulent ﬂows, such a production of spatial gradients is
well known to enhance scalar transport [143]. However, recent DNS
[151–153] and experimental [155] data imply that dilatation in
premixed ﬂames may substantially affect this process.
Fifth, instantaneous images of 2D temperature ﬁelds that
showed (i) signiﬁcant deviations in ﬂame-front topology in intense
turbulence from a typical structure of a laminar ﬂame, and (ii)
a thickening of the preheat zone were reported in several DNS
[282,285–288] and experimental [190,193,194,230,237,262] papers,
e.g. see Fig. 12. Such deviations were more pronounced in more
intense turbulence. For instance, Buschmann et al. [190] observed
‘‘strongly localized deviations from a laminar-like ﬂamelet struc-
ture’’ at Ka ¼ 11 and 35, whereas no such deviations were reported
at Ka ¼ 0.05.
It is worth noting, however, that such data do not prove the
statistical importance of ﬂame-front broadening by turbulent
eddies. For instance, Shepherd et al. [237] have shown that the area
between the c-contours 0.2 and 0.8 (and also 0.3 and 0.7) is
statistically independent of u0/SL and is approximately equal to the
area computed for the laminar ﬂame. Based on this result, Shep-
herd et al. [237] associated the aforementioned deviations with
purely geometrical effects (‘‘out-of-plane ﬂame tilting and ﬂame-
front curvature’’), rather than with ﬂame-front broadening by
turbulent eddies. Moreover, Dinkelacker et al. [190,262] have
reported Df < DL despite the discussed deviations. Furthermore,
the maximum value of the mean normalized gradient jVcjDL
–1
conditioned on the local value of the combustion progress variable
was equal to unity in the DNS by Chakraborty and Klein [288] in the
case of Le ¼ 1, despite the aforementioned thickening of the preheat
zone (see Figs. 1b and 2a in the cited paper).
Thickening of the instantaneous proﬁles of either the combus-
tion progress variable or the temperature was obtained in DNS by
van Oijen et al. [293,294] and in measurements by Kortschik et al.
[238], respectively. However, even if such instantaneous pertur-
bations look very strong (e.g. see Fig. 13a), they may be of minor
importance statistically. For instance, the mean temperature
proﬁles conditioned on the distance from the surface characterized
by T ¼ 800 K, reported by Kortschik et al. [238], are weakly per-
turbed even by intense turbulence, see Fig. 13b. The aforemen-
tioned DNS data by van Oijen et al. [294] show that intense
turbulence can both increase and decrease the thickness of the
instantaneous proﬁles (see the right Fig. 4a in the cited paper).
In addition to the ﬁve categories of data, discussed above, there
are other data that put the thin ﬂame-front assumption into
question. Chen and Bilger [192,230] have disputed this assumption
by showing that proﬁles of the mean combustion progress variables
calculated using either the measured temperature or the measured
OH mass fraction differ from one another, with the difference being
increased by Ka. Chen and Mansour [189] have reported different
fractal dimensions of the temperature and OH contours and have
associated this ﬁnding with the broadening of the preheat zone by
turbulent eddies.

Some
measurements
showed
that
the
turbulent
burning
velocity was substantially underestimated both by the fractal
model [180,197,225,226] and by the ﬂame surface density (FSD)
approach [198,199,236]. Such data imply that small-scale eddies
increase the mean local burning rate within the instantaneous
ﬂame fronts [302] and, thus perturb their structure. However,
such data allow us neither to evaluate the thickness of the
perturbed
ﬂame
front
nor
to

the
