assumption.
In summary, the experimental and DNS data reviewed above
show that the thin ﬂame-front approximation works reasonably
well in many ﬂames, but may be an oversimpliﬁcation in many
other ﬂames associated with more intense turbulence. Neverthe-
less, the following discussion will mainly be based on this
approximation not only because it is widely accepted and reason-
ably accurate (as compared with predictive capabilities of alterna-
tive approaches), but, primarily, because the effects of combustion
on turbulence appear to be most pronounced and most difﬁcult for
modeling in the thin ﬂame-front regime of premixed combustion,
which is associated with the highest instantaneous density
gradients.
3.2. Effects of combustion on ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁeld
Before discussing experimental data, it is worth deﬁning certain
symbols and terms used below.
First, since measured components of the velocity vector were
neither normal nor tangential to the mean ﬂame surface in many
experimental studies reviewed here, we will choose the coordinate
axes x and y so that jnxj > jnyj, i.e. the angle between the x-axis and
the unity vector n normal to the mean ﬂame brush is less than
the angle between the y-axis and the unity vector. Accordingly, the
u-component of the ﬂow velocity vector is closer (or equal) to the
ﬂow velocity normal to the ﬂame brush, whereas the v-component
is closer (or equal) to the tangential ﬂow velocity.
Second, in this section, the terms unburned and burned are used
when discussing conditioned quantities measured within a ﬂame
brush, whereas the terms reactants or products are used when
discussing
Reynolds-averaged
quantities
evaluated
ahead
or
behind the ﬂame brush, respectively.
Third, since the second-order moments ru00
i u00
j =r, which are
commonly used to characterize turbulence in constant-density
ﬂows, are substantially affected by the unburned–burned inter-
mittency, e.g. see Eq. (49), the use of such quantities to characterize
turbulence in ﬂames does not seem to be consistent with the
underlying physics. For this reason, we will call these moments to
be characteristics of ﬂuctuating velocity, rather than turbulence, in
the following.
3.2.1. Unconditioned characteristics of ﬂuctuating velocity
Combustion substantially affects ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁelds, but
particular features of the inﬂuence of a premixed ﬂame on
a turbulent ﬂow may be different for different types of ﬂames. For
this reason, experimental data obtained from geometrically similar
reacting ﬂows will be grouped and discussed together below.
In open V-shaped ﬂames, u0 [136,206,210–215], v0 [136,203,
206,210–215], and the ATKE [174] were reported to increase near the
leading edge, to reach peak values in the middle part, and then to
decrease toward the trailing edge of the ﬂame brush (e.g. see Fig. 14,
where small and large distances x are associated with reactants and
products, respectively). The peak values of u0 are typically higher
than the peak values of v0 [136,206,213,214]. The rms velocities u0
and v0 in products are often equal to or even lower than those in
reactants [136,203,206,211,213,214]. However, Cheng et al. [174]
have reported higher ATKE in products than in reactants. The
inﬂuence of a V-shaped ﬂame on ﬂow velocity is more pro-
nounced at far distances from the ﬂame-stabilization region
[136,206,207,214], whereas the inﬂuence is weak at short distances
[136] (cf. open and ﬁlled circles in Fig. 14). When incident rms
turbulent velocity u00 is increased [136,211,213] or SL and the heat
release factor s are decreased (e.g. by using leaner mixtures [203]),
the aforementioned peaks are less pronounced and disappear in
very lean mixtures [136,213].
Well-pronounced peaks were observed on the radial proﬁles of
u0v0 obtained by Cheng and Ng [136] from moderately lean
(F ¼ 0.7–0.8)
ethylene–air
ﬂames,
but
the
effect
was
not
pronounced in a leaner (F ¼ 0.66) ﬂame. In products and reactants,
u0v0 was approximately the same in all these ﬂames. Peak values of
u0v0 within the ﬂame brush were also reported by Cheng et al.
[206, 207]. However, the axial u and radial v velocities reported in
these three papers were neither normal nor tangential to the
ﬂame brush. In a subsequent paper, Shepherd and Cheng [210]
evaluated the normal and tangential velocities under essentially
the same conditions. In the new coordinate framework, the
Reynolds stress u0v0 was an order of magnitude lower than the
Reynolds stress evaluated in the coordinate framework attached to
the burner.
The integral length scale of turbulence increases within the
ﬂame brush and is highest in products [136,211,213,258], with the
effect being decreased by u00 [211].
In open Bunsen ﬂames, both u0 and v0 have been reported to
increase within the ﬂame brush if measured along a line parallel to
the burner axis [164,169,172,173,184,201], with the increase in u0
being more pronounced [173,184].
Yanagi and Mimura [126] obtained non-monotonic radial
proﬁles of u0 and v0, with the peaks being located within the ﬂame

brush. The v0-peak was almost identical both within the ﬂame and
in the counterpart non-reacting ﬂow, whereas the u0-peak was
associated with the inﬂuence of combustion on the ﬂow. A decrease
in both u0 and v0 in the radial direction was documented by Tanaka
and Yanagi [168] in a Bunsen ﬂame characterized by substantially
(ﬁve times) stronger incident turbulence.
Yoshida [164] reported a substantial increase in both u0 and v0
with the radial distance, with the two rms velocities being
approximately equal to one another. It is worth noting, however,
that the v0 obtained by Yoshida and Tsuji [162] from Bunsen ﬂames
at a moderate distance Z from the burner exit (Z/D ¼ 5, where D is
the burner diameter) were lower than the v0 in the counterpart
non-reacting shear ﬂow.
Proﬁles of u0 and v0 measured by Cheng and Shepherd [173] in
the radial direction indicate weak variations in v0(x) and a local peak
of u0(x) within the ﬂame brush. However, the peak value of u0 was
markedly lower than u0 at the burner axis (i.e. in the reactants),
because the rms velocity decreased with x in the vicinity of the
leading edge of the ﬂame brush. Cheng and Shepherd [173] asso-
ciated the different trends observed by them and by Yoshida [164]
for the radial proﬁles of u0 with a more intense incident turbulence
in the former ﬂow. Such an interpretation appears to be consistent
with the aforementioned data by Yanagi et al. [126,168].
Pfadler et al. [201] investigated weakly turbulent ﬂames and
reported non-monotonic radial proﬁles of u0 with the peaks being
located within the ﬂame brush.
An increase in the apparent turbulent kinetic energy within
Bunsen ﬂames was documented by Cheng and Shepherd [174], by
Gagnepain et al. [183], and by Hartung et al. [155]. Chen et al. [186]
have reported non-monotonic radial proﬁles of ATKE in highly
turbulent Bunsen ﬂames, but, at Z/D  6.5, the peak values of the
ATKE in the ﬂames are lower than the peak values of kðrÞ measured
in the respective non-reacting ﬂow. At Z/D ¼ 10.5, the former peak
value was higher.
An increase in L within the ﬂame brush was reported by Yanagi
and Mimura [126], by Chen et al. [186], and by Hartung et al. [155].
The latter authors also reported an increase in the Taylor length
scale in Bunsen ﬂames [155]. Steinberg et al. [202] reported that
a Bunsen ﬂame can increase L in the upstream ﬂow (cf. diamonds
with other symbols in Fig. 6 in the cited paper).
The Reynolds stress u0v0 is typically low within open Bunsen
ﬂames and is weakly affected by combustion [164,173].
Hartung et al. [155] have reported that Bunsen ﬂames substan-
tially affect the statistics of the ﬂuctuating rate of strain sij deﬁned by
Eq. (32). In particular, the mean and rms values of the PDF P(sij) ‘‘are
reduced by an order of magnitude because of the dilatation caused
by the ﬂame’’ [155] in comparisonwith the counterpart non-reacting
ﬂows.
In premixed ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets, u0-proﬁles with
well pronounced peaks were documented by several research
groups [174,212,223,224,227–229,231], but the u0 in the products
(i.e. near the wall the jet impinged at) was less than in the reac-
tants [174,212,223,224,227,228] or in the counterpart non-reacting
ﬂow [229]. When the equivalence ratio F < 1 was further decreased
[223] or u0 was increased [229], the u0-peak was less pronounced.
The peaks were also pronounced on v0-proﬁles obtained from the
stoichiometric
ethylene–air
[174]
and
nearly
stoichiometric
(F z 0.9) methane–air ﬂames [223,227,229], but the effect was not
observed in leaner ﬂames [223,228] or at higher u00 [229]. In the
products (near the wall), v0 substantially increased both in non-
reacting [229] and reacting [212,223,229,231] ﬂows, the increase
in v0 being more pronounced in the latter case and at lower u0. Cho
et al [223] have reported that the Reynolds stresses are ‘‘vanish-
ingly small through ﬂame zone’’ in all the cases investigated by
them.
In open ﬂames stabilized by low swirl, Chan et al. [232] recorded
u0-proﬁles with a well-pronounced peak within the ﬂame brush.
The rms velocity u0 was larger in the products than in the reactants
only if11 u0/SL < 1. The v0-proﬁles showed ‘‘a slight increasing
trend. associated with slow precession of the ﬂow’’ [232]. Cheng
[233] have reported both u0 and ATKE proﬁles (see Fig.15) with well
pronounced peaks within the ﬂame brush, whereas the peaks were
much less pronounced for v0 proﬁles. The velocities u0 and v0 and
the ATKE were higher in the reactants than in the products. These
effects decreased when the equivalence ratio was decreased from 1
to 0.6 (cf. ﬂames SWF12-SW16) and they were reduced when u0
was increased so that no inﬂuence of combustion on the ﬂuctuating
velocity ﬁeld was observed in a very lean (F ¼ 0.6) ﬂame SWF16 (see
triangles in Fig. 15) characterized12 by u0/SL ¼ 2.3. For open swirl-
stabilized ﬂames characterized by high u0/SL, Cheng et al. [234–236]
and Schneider et al. [239] observed no notable effects of combus-
tion on u0, v0, and u0v0.
Gulati and Driscoll [241–243] investigated oblique unconﬁned
ﬂames and reported that both u0 and v0 reached peak values within
the ﬂame brush, with the former peak being more pronounced and
increasing with the density ratio. Both u0 and v0 were higher in the
products than in the reactants.
Videto and Santavicca [245] have reported an increase in u0 by
a factor of 6 behind a propagating statistically planar ﬂame,
whereas v0 was increased by a factor of 3 within the ﬂame brush
and by a factor of 2 behind the ﬂame. As a result, even the density-
weighted ATKE r~k was higher in the products than in the reactants.
Moreover, the rms velocity u0, evaluated in the reactants before the
ﬂame reached the point of measurement, increased gradually by
50% as the ﬂame approached this point, thus supporting the
concept of ﬂame-generated turbulence, whereas v0 remained
constant until the ﬂame arrived. The integral length scale of
turbulence also increased in the ﬂame. The spectra of velocity
ﬂuctuations, reported in the same paper, imply that small-scale
eddies substantially contribute to the increase in u0 ahead of the

In oblique conﬁned ﬂames, Moreau et al. [247–249] docu-

divided the conditions of the measurements into three regions; 1
(vc0/SL < 2), 2 (vc0/SL z 2), and 3 (vc0/SL > 2).
In region 1, (i) the energy spectrum of turbulence obtained from
the ﬂame was higher (lower) than the spectrum obtained from the
air jet at low (high) wavenumbers, and (ii) a ratio of Lf/Lc was larger
than unity and increased when Lc increased or vc0 decreased [251].
In ranges 2 and 3, Lf/Lc < 1 with this ratio being reduced both by vc0
and Lc in region 3. The Kolmogorov length scale in the ﬂames was
always higher than in the air jets due to an increase in the kine-
matic viscosity in the ﬂames.
To assess the contribution of the ﬂame-induced shear stresses to
the difference in vf0and vc0, Ballal [251] measured rms turbulent

Katsuki et al. [258] studied conﬁned V-shaped ﬂames and
reported a substantial increase in v0 near the trailing edge of the
ﬂame brush and in the products.
Lindstedt and Sakthitharan [261] investigated ﬂame propaga-
tion in a conﬁned tube with an obstacle and found no notable
inﬂuence of combustion on turbulence ‘‘in the absence of mean
velocity gradients.’’
The DNS by Nishiki et al. [279] yielded peak values of u0 and v0
within the ﬂame brush in the case of ru/rb ¼ 7.53, but a weak effect
of combustion on turbulence in the case of s ¼ 1.5. The DNS by
Treurniet et al. [142] also yielded well-pronounced peaks of u0(x)
and ATKE and less-pronounced peaks of v0(x) within the ﬂame
brush in the cases of s ¼ 3 and 5 (with the effect being increased by
s), but a weak effect of combustion on ATKE in the case of s ¼ 1.
Many studies [136,142,173,174,184,207,214,223,227,233,245,247–
249,279] have shown that velocity ﬂuctuations become anisotropic
in ﬂames and in products. Such data cast doubt on the applicability of
the theory of isotropic turbulence to ﬂame-induced velocity ﬂuctu-
ations. As concerns the ﬂame brush, u0 and v0 do not seem to be
proper turbulence characteristics, because of the inﬂuence of the
unburned–burned intermittency on u0 and v0, see Eq. (49).
All in all, substantial inﬂuence of combustion on the uncondi-
tioned characteristics of velocity ﬂuctuations was documented in
various ﬂames. For instance, u0 and v0 often reach peak values
within
the
ﬂame
brush.
Such
effects
are
commonly
more
pronounced (i) in the velocity-vector component that is closer to
the normal to the ﬂame brush, (ii) in weakly turbulent ﬂows, and
(iii) in mixtures characterized by a higher density ratio and a higher
laminar ﬂame speed. The effects are less or not even pronounced in
lean mixtures and/or highly turbulent ﬂows. As concerns the ratio
of rms velocities (either u0 or v0) measured in reactants and prod-
ucts, this quantity is very sensitive to ﬂow geometry.
3.2.2. Discussion
As brieﬂy reviewed above, besides common trends, there are
also differences in effects of combustion on ﬂow ﬂuctuations in
ﬂames. Such differences are associated either with the inﬂuence of
processes not directly relevant to ﬂame-turbulence interaction or
with a hypothesis that different physical mechanisms of the effects
of combustion on turbulence play a dominant role under different
conditions. Let us begin with brieﬂy reviewing the former
processes.
First, even in the non-reacting ﬂows associated with the ﬂames
discussed above, turbulence is not spatially uniform. It decays with
distance from a grid or plate that is commonly placed upstream
from the burner exit to generate turbulence. Moreover, turbulence
is produced in a shear layer that separates the jet core and ambient
air, as in ﬂows associated with Bunsen ﬂames, or in a turbulent
wake behind a rod used to stabilize a V-shaped ﬂame. When
a ﬂame is ignited in a spatially non-uniform shear ﬂow, turbulence
characteristics within the ﬂame brush depend on ﬂame shape. For
instance, if ﬂames A and B are in a jet core and a shear layer,
respectively, turbulence characteristics within the two ﬂames will
be substantially different even if the upstream (at the burner exit)
ﬂows are statistically identical. Gulati and Driscoll [241] have
pointed out that turbulence generation by a shear layer (rim-
stabilized Bunsen ﬂames) or a wake (rod-stabilized V-shaped
ﬂames) play a more important role than ﬂame-generated turbu-
lence if the ﬂame incidence angle with the main ﬂow direction is
‘‘less than about 20 degrees.’’
Furthermore, due to the inﬂuence of heat release on the mean
ﬂow, the topology of the shear layer will be affected by combustion
and the same spatial point may be located in different regions of the
shear layer in the non-reacting and reacting cases, with all other
things being equal. Accordingly, differences in u0 measured in the
two cases at the same point may characterize such changes in the
shear layer topology, rather than the inﬂuence of combustion on
turbulence.
Second, ﬂow perturbations originating in the vicinity of a ﬂame-
stabilizing rod can propagate along the ﬂame surface both in
laminar [305] and turbulent [165, 241, 306] cases. Such perturba-
tions may contribute to measured values of u0 and v0, and may
mimic ﬂame-generated turbulence in V-shaped ﬂames.
Third, oscillations of a Bunsen ﬂame may also contribute to
measured values of u0 and v0 [241], and may mimic ﬂame-generated
turbulence.
Fourth, a typical premixed turbulent ﬂame is well known to
develop as distance from the ﬂame-stabilization region grows [43,
307]. Accordingly, data on u0 and v0 obtained from the same ﬂame
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
27


--- Page 28 ---

but at different distances may be substantially different [136, 206,
207, 214].
Fifth, the angles 4 between the u-component of the ﬂow velocity
and the normal to the mean ﬂame brush were different in different
experiments reviewed in the previous subsection, and such varia-
tions in 4 contributed also to the scatter of the experimental data.
Sixth, many premixed turbulent Bunsen ﬂames investigated in
the papers reviewed above were in mixing layers (between the jet
and ambient air). Therefore the entrainment of ambient air could
locally change the equivalence ratio and, thus, contribute also to
variations in the ﬂow characteristics measured under different
(e.g. F in the incident ﬂow) conditions.
Bearing in mind a number of the aforementioned phenomena
that impede characterizing turbulent reacting ﬂows, the scatter of
experimental data on the inﬂuence of combustion on ﬂuctuating
ﬂow velocities is not surprising. Nevertheless, there are certain
common trends in the behavior of the ﬂuctuating ﬂow velocity in
various ﬂames and these common trends offer an opportunity to
gain insight into the role played by various physical mechanisms of
the inﬂuence of combustion on turbulent ﬂows.
As far as Eqs. (22) or (23) are concerned, there are four such
physical mechanisms; shear stresses (term I), dilatation (term I),
viscous dissipation (term III), and pressure-driven phenomena
(terms IV and V), with the second and third mechanisms serving to
reduce u0, v0, and k. The Reynolds stress u0v0 is commonly accepted
[164,173,210,223,241] to be low in open premixed turbulent ﬂames,
at least if the angle 4 ¼ (u$n)/juj is sufﬁciently small [210]. Accord-
ingly, the shear stress mechanism appears to play a minor role in
such ﬂames and the observed peaks of u0 and v0 within the ﬂame
brush are mainly associated with pressure-driven mechanisms.
These mechanisms are modeled by terms IV and V in Eqs. (22) or
(23)and the twotermsmay vary when ﬂow typeischanged. Sincethe
mean pressure gradient is closely linked with the mean velocity ﬁeld,
which depends substantially on boundary conditions, the magnitude
of term V appears to be different in different ﬂows (e.g. in an
impinging jet and a free jet with a rod placed at the center of the
nozzle) even if the upstream turbulence is identical in these ﬂows. To
the contrary, term IV may be controlled by local turbulence and
mixture characteristics and be approximately the same in all these
ﬂows, at least if processes in the instantaneous ﬂame front control the
correlation between the ﬂuctuating pressure gradient and velocity.
Cheng and Shepherd [174] compared mean and ﬂuctuating
velocity proﬁles obtained from three types of ﬂames stabilized using
either a rod (a V-shaped ﬂame), or a stagnation plate (an impinging
jet), or a pilot ﬂame around the nozzle rim (a Bunsen ﬂame). Just
upstream of the nozzle, the ﬂow and mixture characteristics were
almost identical (statistically) in the three cases (the ﬂames stabi-
lized with the rod or the stagnation plate shared a ‘‘common ﬂow
generated system’’ [174]). The proﬁles of u measured along ﬂowlines
through the mean ﬂame brush were substantially different in the
three types of ﬂows (cf. Figs. 5a, 6a, and 7a in the cited paper),
whereas the proﬁles of q0 ¼
ﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃ
u
02 þ v
02
p
=2 were markedly closer to
each other (cf. Figs. 5b, 6b, and 7b there), with the peak values of q0
being almost equal in the three ﬂames. These data show that
the increase in the rms magnitude of ﬂow velocity ﬂuctuations is
not very sensitive to the mean velocity ﬁeld and, thus, imply
a substantial role played by more universal small-scale processes in
the ﬂame front.
The magnitude of various terms in Eqs. (22) or (23) is further
discussed in Section 4.1.1.

Furukawa et al. [176–178] have measured the power-spectrum

brush in order to study the inﬂuence of combustion on turbulence.
Typical results are shown in Fig. 17. These and other reported
experimental data indicate that (i) at a point B associated with the
middle of the ﬂame brush both in the radial and axial directions
(i.e. out of the centerline and far from the ﬂame tip), the spectra
conditioned on the unburned mixture were very close to the
spectra obtained from the non-reacting ﬂow [176,177]. (ii) In the
vicinity of the ﬂame tip (point A), both uu0 and vu0 were higher than
u0 and v0 in the non-reacting ﬂow [178]. (iii) The weak anisotropy of
the incident turbulence was strongly increased by the ﬂame with
u0
b > v0

radial and axial velocity components) [177,178]. (iv) Large- and
small-scale velocity ﬂuctuations were more intense in the burned
and unburned gas, respectively (see Fig. 17) [177,178]. (v) Flame
enhanced both axial and radial velocity ﬂuctuations, i.e. v0

A much stronger increase in kðcÞ than kuðcÞ was documented by
Gagnepain et al. [183] in Bunsen ﬂames.
Cheng et al. [174, 206, 207] investigated V-shaped ﬂames and
reported that; (i) The unconditioned rms velocities u0 and v0 were
markedly higher than ub0 and vb0, respectively [206]. (ii) Both uu0
and vu0 were smaller than ub0 and vb0, respectively, by approxi-

markedly (e.g. by a factor of two [206]) higher than kb [206,207].
(iv) kb was higher than ku by several times [206,207]. (v) Both

noted in Section 3.2.1, the u and v reported in these three papers
were neither normal nor tangential to the ﬂame brush. In a subse-
quent paper, Shepherd and Cheng [210] evaluated normal and
tangential velocities by rotating the coordinate framework so that
the slip velocity vector was directed along the new x-axis. Most
results obtained in this framework agreed qualitatively with the
early data [206,207], but (i) vb0 was higher than v0 at the leading
edge of the ﬂame brush and monotonically decreased toward the
trailing edge, and (ii) both the unconditioned and conditioned
Reynolds stress were of the same order and small.
Cho et al. [224] and Li et al. [227] have reported that the
conditioned rms velocities u0u and ub0 are much lower (see Fig. 16)
than the unconditioned u0 in premixed turbulent ﬂames stabilized
in impinging jets, whereas the difference between v0
u < v0
bzv0 is
much less pronounced. Similar results were obtained by Cheng and
Shepherd [174] and by Cheng [233] from ﬂames stabilized in
impinging jets and by low swirl, respectively, but vb0 was higher
than v0 at the leading edge and in the middle of the ﬂame brush.
Cheng and Shepherd [174] investigated V-shaped, Bunsen, and
stagnation ﬂames stabilized using three different techniques in
statistically

ﬂows.

In a highly turbulent ﬂame investigated by Heitor et al. [244],

Gulati and Driscoll [241–243] have also reported that the
velocity PDF Pu(u) conditioned on the unburned mixture is
Gaussian, whereas the form of Pb(u) is non-Gaussian, because low
speed products contribute substantially to this PDF conditioned on
the burned mixture. These authors assumed that the low speed
products originated from the instantaneous ﬂame fronts that were
almost perpendicular to the mean ﬂame brush. When a gas volume
passed through such a ﬂame front, the u-component of the ﬂow
velocity vector increased weakly, whereas the ﬂame fronts almost
parallel to the mean ﬂame brush locally accelerated the ﬂow
velocity in the x-direction by approximately six times. Since this
explanation was essentially the same as the well-known Karlovitz
hypothesis [55], Driscoll and Gulati [243] claimed that the non-
Gaussian PDFs Pb(u) provided ‘‘direct experimental evidence that
the Karlovitz mechanism’’ existed.
By processing the DNS data by Nishiki [278], Im et al. [281] have
reported a signiﬁcant increase in kb with the mean combustion
progress variable (see Fig. 8 in the cited paper), whereas ku
decreases within the ﬂame. They also noted that v0
b > u0
b in the
middle of the ﬂame brush, but v0
b < u0
b at c ¼ 1. To explain these
observations, Im et al. [281] proposed a simple model based on
Karlovitz’s ideas (ﬂow acceleration across randomly oriented
ﬂamelets). Within the framework of this simple model, they
calculated that v0
b=vb was increased by the density ratio and was
larger than u0
b=ub, with the latter ratio being independent of s. The
former ratio was very sensitive to a PDF presumed to simulate
random ﬂamelet orientation.
The hypothesis of ﬂame-generated turbulence (i.e. qb > qu, where q
is a second moment of the velocity ﬁeld) is supported in many afore-
mentioned
papers
[125,173,174,178,196,201,206,207,210,233,241–
243,257]. It is worth noting, however, that the Karlovitz concept of
ﬂame-generated turbulence comprises not only an increase in ub0 due
to local ﬂow acceleration across randomly inclined ﬂame fronts, but it
also postulates an increase in uu0 due to the transport of such ﬂame-
generated turbulence ‘‘against the ﬂow before the ﬂame front’’ [55].
Although an increase in uu0 [241–243], vu0 [257], and ku [183]
with c was documented in some ﬂames, this trend was not
pronounced in the vicinity of the leading edge of the ﬂame brush
(e.g. see Fig. 13 in Ref. [242]). Furthermore, at low c, no substantial
variations in either uu0, or vu0, or ku, or ðu0v0Þu were observed by
Cheng et al. [174,206,207,210] in V-shaped ﬂames, by Cheng and
Shepherd [174], Furukawa et al. [176,177], Chen and Bilger [196],
and Pfadler et al. [201] in Bunsen ﬂames, by Cho et al. [224], Cheng
and Shepherd [174], Li et al. [227] in impinging jet ﬂames, by Cheng
[233] in ﬂames stabilized by low swirl.
Since turbulence characteristics at the leading edge of a turbu-
lent ﬂame brush may control ﬂame speed [9,43,67], an assumption
that ﬂame-generated turbulence may accelerate ﬂame expansion,
which is the cornerstone of the Karlovitz concept, does not seem to
be proven by the experimental data reviewed above. Nevertheless,
these data clearly show that combustion may substantially affect
the unconditioned (stronger) and conditioned (weaker) second
moments of the velocity ﬁeld at least if the incident turbulence is
sufﬁciently weak (u0/SL is of the order of unity). Such effects are
increased by the density ratio and laminar ﬂame speed, but
reduced by u0.
The aforementioned increase in uu0 [241–243], vu0 [257], and ku
[183] with c in premixed turbulent ﬂames may be caused not only
by the transport of turbulence from burned to unburned mixture,
as emphasized by Karlovitz [55], but also by the acceleration of the
unburned mixture ﬂow by the mean pressure gradient induced by
the ﬂame [see term V in Eqs. (22)].
Finally, it is worth noting that Chen and Bilger [196] have found
that the longitudinal integral length scales, measured on the
reactant side of the Bunsen ﬂame brush and either unconditioned
or conditioned on the unburned mixture, are substantially lower
than the length scale measured at the same point in the counter-
part non-reacting ﬂow ‘‘despite the same mean and rms velocity
ﬁelds’’.
Pfadler et al. [200] have reported that various (integral, Taylor,
and
Kolmogorov;
longitudinal
and
transverse)
length
scales
conditioned on the burned mixture are higher than the same scales
conditioned on the unburned mixture in a number of weakly
turbulent (Ret < 50) Bunsen ﬂames.

3.2.4. Spectra of velocity ﬂuctuations in ﬂames
To the best of the authors’ knowledge, Ballal [251] was the ﬁrst
to document the inﬂuence of combustion on the spectrum of
velocity ﬂuctuations. He compared the energy spectra measured on
the axis of a conﬁned V-shaped ﬂame (i.e. in products) with the
corresponding spectra in the cold ﬂow. For weakly turbulent ﬂames
characterized by u0 < 2SL and h > dL, a decrease (increase) in the
former spectra at high (low) wavenumbers was reported, see Fig. 4
in the cited paper.
Go¨kalp et al. [211] applied a single-component LDA system to
obtain velocity spectra from two V-shape ﬂames characterized by
the same Ret ¼ 110, but different st ¼ L/u0 (st ¼ 19 and 7 in ﬂames A
and B, respectively). The spectra in the reactants, products, and in
the ﬂame brush were recorded by changing the measurement
points. For ﬂame B, the average slopes of the spectra obtained from
products were scattered around the value of 5/3 (see Table III in the
cited paper), which is associated with the Kolmogorov spectrum.
For ﬂame A, the average slopes of the spectra obtained from
products were substantially higher (see Table II therein) than 5/3. It
is worth noting that Fig. 3 from the cited paper shows that, even for
ﬂame A, the slope is about 5/3 in a narrow frequency range.
The u-spectra measured by Videto and Santavicca [245] in the
vicinity of the ﬂame brush showed departures from the Kolmo-
gorov scaling both in reactants (the mean slope is 1.4) and products
(the mean slope decreases from 2.7 to 2.2 with distance from the
ﬂame), see Fig. 11 in the cited paper. However, inspection of the
spectra obtained from the reactants in the vicinity of the ﬂame and
from the products far from the ﬂame (see curves 2 and 4 in the
discussed ﬁgure) reveals narrow frequency ranges characterized by
the Kolmogorov slope of 5/3. Such a range is not observed in the
spectrum obtained from the products in the vicinity of the ﬂame
(curve 3), where a local maximum of the power spectral density is
pronounced around a frequency of 150 Hz. Both the u-spectrum
measured in the reactants far from the ﬂame and the v-spectra
measured at different distances from the ﬂame are characterized by
an average slope of about 5/3, while v0 in the products is higher
than in the reactants.
The conditioned spectra of velocity ﬂuctuations, reported by
Furukawa et al. [176–178], show that combustion affects the
spectrum shape. The slope of a velocity spectrum conditioned on
burned mixture is commonly higher than the slope of the coun-
terpart spectrum conditioned on unburned mixture, e.g. see Fig. 17.
Nevertheless, for any reported burned-gas spectrum, we could
identify a narrow frequency range characterized by the Kolmogorov
slope 5/3. Unfortunately, the difference between the measured
conditioned spectra and the classical Kolmogorov spectrum is not
quantiﬁed in the cited papers, but ‘‘the greater departures from
possible Kolmogorov scalings in the burnt-gas spectra’’ [178] are
pointed out. In the middle of the ﬂame brush, the spectra condi-
tioned on the unburned mixture were very close to the spectra
obtained from the non-reacting ﬂow [176,177]. In the vicinity of the
ﬂame tip, both uu0 and vu0 were higher than u0 and v0 in the non-
reacting ﬂow and the slopes of the unburned-gas spectra differed
from the slopes of the corresponding spectra in the non-reacting
ﬂow (see Fig. 14 in Ref. [178]).
The few experimental investigations into the spectra of velocity
ﬂuctuations in ﬂames, reviewed above, are deﬁnitely not sufﬁcient
to lead to a solid conclusion, but neither do these data substantiate
questioning the applicability of the Kolmogorov theory to turbu-
lence in ﬂames.
3.3. Effects of combustion on turbulent scalar ﬂux
As brieﬂy discussed in Section 2.2.3, the turbulent transport of
a scalar q is called a countergradient if the second moment ru00
i c00
and the gradient vq=vxi have the same sign, i.e. ru00
i q00ðvq=vxiÞ > 0.
Based on the BML Eq. (47), many experts consider the inequality
juji;b > juji;u to be evidence of a countergradient scalar ﬂux. For
instance, for a statistically planar 1D ﬂame that propagates from
right to left, (i) both uu and ub are positive in the framework linked
with the ﬂame, (ii) vc=vx > 0, and (iii) Eq. (47) results in
ru00c00ðv~c=vxÞ > 0 if ub > uu. If the same ﬂame moves in the opposite
direction, (i) both uu and ub are negative in the coordinate frame-
work attached to the ﬂame, (ii) vc=vx < 0, and (iii) Eq. (47) results
again in ru00
i c00ðvc=vxÞ > 0 if jujb > juju.
Certainly the two inequalities, juji;b > juji;u and ru00
i c00ðv~c=vxiÞ > 0
are equivalent to one another only if the BML Eq. (47) holds.
Nevertheless, the former inequality may be useful even if the
probability of ﬁnding intermediate states of a reacting mixture is not
negligible. For instance, although the DNS by Trouve´ and Poinsot
[265] and by Veynante et al. [128] have shown that Eq. (47) over-
estimates ru00c00, Veynante et al. [128] have claimed that the sign of
the slip velocity is ‘‘a good indicator of the occurrence of counter-
gradient or gradient turbulent transport.’’ The DNS data reported by
Chakraborty and Cant [289] in Figs. 2a and 3e in the cited paper
support this claim even if the BML model does not work well under
the conditions of the DNS (see Fig. 6 therein).
The ﬁrst experimental evidence of countergradient scalar ﬂux,
published in English, was delivered by Moss [125] who showed that
rv00c00ðvc=vyÞ > 0 in a Bunsen ﬂame. Then, Yanagi et al. [126, 168]
reported the countergradient heat ﬂux u0T0 (ﬂux v0T0 was mainly
gradient) in Bunsen ﬂames. Shepherd et al. [257] and Shepherd and
Moss [169] obtained jvbj > jvuj from ducted and Bunsen ﬂames,
respectively.
Chandran et al. [171] have documented the countergradient axial
heat ﬂux v0T0 in a lean (F ¼ 0.8) propane–air Bunsen ﬂame. Cheng and
Shepherd [173] obtained positive signs of DUðvc=vxÞ and DVðvc=vyÞ
from a lean (F ¼ 0.6) ethylene-air Bunsen ﬂame. Here, DV designates
the y-component of the slip velocity vector DU. Frank et al. [129,130]
found that (i) both DUðvc=vxÞ > 0 and DVðvc=vyÞ > 0, i.e. the coun-
tergradient transport in both directions, in a weakly turbulent (u0/
SL ¼ 2.4) Bunsen ﬂame, (ii) DUðvc=vxÞ > 0 but DVðvc=vyÞ < 0, i.e.
countergradient radial and gradient axial transport, in moderately
turbulent (u0/SL  3.5) ﬂames, and (iii) both DUðvc=vxÞ < 0 and
DVðvc=vyÞ < 0, i.e. gradient transport in both directions, in a highly
turbulent (u0/SL ¼ 8.8) ﬂame. Chen and Bilger [196] obtained
DUðvc=vxÞ > 0 but DVðvc=vyÞ < 0, i.e. countergradient radial and
gradient axial transport, from moderately turbulent (u0/SL ¼ 1.2–3),
lean hydrogen–air Bunsen ﬂames. Pfadler et al. [201] have docu-
mented countergradient ﬂux ru00c00 in a number of weakly turbulent,
lean methane–air Bunsen ﬂames with the magnitude of the ﬂux
being increased by the equivalence ratio.
Positive signs of DUðvc=vxÞ and DVðvc=vyÞ, which imply coun-
tergradient scalar ﬂux, were obtained by Cheng [206] from a lean
(F ¼ 0.7) ethylene-air V-shaped ﬂame. Cheng and Shepherd [210]
processed similar experimental data by rotating the coordinate
axes so that vb ¼ vu in the new coordinate framework and also
found that DUðvc=vxÞ > 0.
In ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets, the countergradient axial
scalar ﬂux ru00c00 was documented by Cho et al. [224], by Cheng and
Shepherd [174], by Li et al. [227], and by Stevens et al. [228]. Kalt
et al. [132] have reported countergradient ﬂux in weakly turbulent
(u0/SL < 2) ﬂames, whereas the ﬂux was of a gradient nature at u0/
SL > 3.5 (see Fig. 18).
Positive signs of DUðvc=vxÞ were obtained by Cheng [233] from
a number of open ﬂames stabilized by low swirl.
Gulati and Driscoll [241, 242, 243] found (i) countergradient
density ﬂux along the normal to the brush of oblique unconﬁned
ﬂames, and (ii) DUðvc=vxÞ > 0, with the difference in ub and uu
being increased by the density ratio.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
31


--- Page 32 ---

Heitor et al. [244] have reported countergradient axial ﬂux rv00c00
but gradient transverse ﬂux ru00c00 in a lean (F ¼ 0.79) methane–air
ﬂame stabilized by the recirculation zone behind a disk bafﬂe.
Similar results were obtained by Veynante et al. [135] from
a conﬁned, nearly stoichiometric propane–air, V-shaped ﬂame at
a far distance from the ﬂame holder, whereas not only ru00c00 but
also rv00c00 were gradient in the vicinity of the holder.
Most et al. [220] and Pfadler et al. [240] have also showed that
the direction of turbulent scalar ﬂux changes from gradient to
countergradient within the ﬂame brush (e.g. see Fig. 48 in Section
5.1). Surprisingly, the countergradient axial ﬂux rv00c00 measured by
Most et al. [220] was more pronounced (i) in a highly turbulent (u0/
SL ¼ 12) lean (F ¼ 0.7) methane–air ﬂame stabilized by a bluff body
than in a weakly turbulent (u0/SL ¼ 0.5) lean (F ¼ 0.7) methane–air
ﬂame stabilized by a rod, and (ii) in a leaner (F ¼ 0.7) rather than in
a richer (F ¼ 0.8) methane–air ﬂame stabilized by the bluff body. To
the contrary, the magnitude of ru00c00 obtained by Pfadler et al. [240]
from swirl-stabilized V-shaped methane–air ﬂames was increased
by F in the range of F ¼ 0.67–0.83.
Troiani et al. [246] have documented countergradient scalar
ﬂux from near-stoichiometric methane–air ﬂames, whereas ru00c00
was gradient in a lean ﬂame (F ¼ 0.67) characterized by a lower
sSL/u0.
Countergradient scalar ﬂux was also found in many DNS. For
instance, Rutland and Cant [270] have reported that ub > uu > 0.
Swaminathan et al. [267] obtained countergradient ﬂux ru00c00 by
processing the DNS database by Zhang and Rutland [271]. The DNS
by Boger et al. [274] yielded gradient and countergradient normal
scalar ﬂuxes at s  2 and s ¼ 6, respectively. Nishiki et al. [280] tried
to obtain fully developed turbulent ﬂames in DNS and reported
countergradient normal scalar ﬂux even at s as low as 1.5.
The DNS data reported by Veynante et al. [128] indicate a tran-
sition from gradient to countergradient normal scalar ﬂux as the
ﬂame develops if u00/SL  5 (s ¼ 3). For a higher u00/SL ¼ 10, the ﬂux
was always gradient.
The DNS study by Veynante and Poinsot [131] has shown that
the direction of the computed scalar ﬂux may be changed by
imposing an external pressure gradient.
The DNSs by Chakraborty and Cant [289, 290, 291] have indi-
cated that a decrease in the Lewis number may cause counter-
gradient scalar transport. For instance, Fig. 2a from Ref. [289] shows
gradient scalar ﬂux for Le ¼ 1, but strong countergradient ﬂux for
Le ¼ 0.34. Moreover, the DNS results (see Table 4 in Ref. [291]) show
gradient transport behavior at early stages (e.g. t ¼ L0/u00) of pre-
mixed turbulent ﬂame development, but countergradient transport
behavior for Le  0.6 at t  2L0/u00. For Le  0.8, the transport shows
gradient behavior at t  3L0/u00 (the simulations was stopped at this
instant), but the magnitude of the scalar ﬂux decreases with time.
A transition from gradient to countergradient axial ﬂux rv00c00
was obtained in recent DNS of V-shaped ﬂames [295] by increasing
the distance from the ﬂame holder, whereas the radial ﬂux ru00c00
was either always gradient (u0/SL ¼ 2.5) or low and sometimes (not
far from the ﬂame holder) countergradient (u0/SL ¼ 1.25). The
magnitudes of both axial and radial scalar ﬂuxes were higher in the
former (u0/SL ¼ 2.5) than in the latter (u0/SL ¼ 1.25) case.
The above brief review of experimental and DNS data clearly
shows that combustion can substantially affect turbulent scalar
transport and even change its direction. Certainly, the magnitude of
these effects depends on mixture and turbulence characteristics,
ﬂame type, the point of measurement, etc. For instance, transition
from gradient to countergradient transport was observed in many
experimental [129,130,132,135,220,240] and DNS [128,131,274,295]
studies by varying either the density ratio [274], or the normalized
rms turbulent velocity u0/SL [128–132], or the distance from the
ﬂame holder [135,220,240,295], or ﬂame development time [128].
This problem will be further discussed in Section 5.1.
Another interesting issue is how strong countergradient scalar
transport can be. Since, according to the BML Eq. (47), the magni-
tude of scalar ﬂux ru00c00 is proportional to the magnitude of the slip
velocity, the issue may be reformulated as follows: How large can
the slip velocity be in premixed turbulent ﬂames?
Fig. 19 shows the normalized slip velocities DU/(sSL) obtained by
Cheng and Shepherd [174] from three stoichiometric methane–air
ﬂames stabilized by either a rod (V-shaped), or a rim (Bunsen), or
a wall (impinging jet) in turbulent ﬂows that had almost the same
characteristics at the burner exit. The values of DU/(sSL) depend on
c, ﬂame type, and the point of measurement, but in all the cases, the
normalized slip velocity was lower than unity. For the stoichio-
metric ethylene–air ﬂame s9 stabilized in an impinging jet and

are ‘‘more scattered for c > 0:5.’’
The data obtained by Gulati and Driscoll [241, 242, 243] from
stoichiometric methane–air oblique unconﬁned ﬂames show that
DU/(sSL) is markedly less than unity, but this ratio is higher for
a leaner ﬂame (F ¼ 0.8) and may be close to unity for a sufﬁciently
lean (F ¼ 0.7) ﬂame.
Pfadler et al. [240] obtained DU/(sSL) z 0.67 from a swirl-stabi-
lized, V-shaped, moderately lean (F ¼ 0.83) methane–air ﬂame.
The ratio of DU/(sSL) was about 0.2 in recent experiments with
a slightly rich methane–air ﬂame stabilized by a bluff body [246].
The DNS by Rutland and Cant [270] has yielded DU/(sSL) z 0.3.
The DNS by Chakraborty and Cant [289] has shown (see Fig. 3e in
the cited paper) that DU/(sSL) is about unity for Le ¼ 0.6, but may be
as large as three for Le ¼ 0.34. For Le  0.8 this ratio is negative
(gradient transport) under the conditions of their DNS.
Some authors did not evaluate slip velocity, but reported the
dependencies of the turbulent scalar ﬂux on the combustion
progress variable. To estimate DU and DV using such data, we
applied the BML Eqs. (44) and (47) to calculate the slip velocity
associated with the maximum values of jru00c00j and jrv00c00j,
respectively. The results were as follows:
For the lean hydrogen–air Bunsen ﬂames studied by Chen and
Bilger [196], DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.5 if F ¼ 0.45 (sSL ¼ 1.7 m/s), DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.3
if F ¼ 0.6 (sSL ¼ 5.2 m/s), DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.24–0.4 (depending on the
incident turbulence) if F ¼ 0.7 (sSL ¼ 8 m/s). In the ﬂames investi-
gated by Most et al. [220], DV/(sSL) was commonly less than unity,
but slip velocities higher (than sSL) were also recorded at large c.
For ﬂames studied in DNS, DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.7 [271, 267], DU/
(sSL) ¼ 0.2 [274], DU/(sSL)  1.3, 0.67, and 0.33 if u00/SL ¼ 2, 3, and 5,
respectively [128], DU/(sSL)  1.3 if u00/SL ¼ 5 and a strong pressure
gradient was imposed to promote countergradient transport [131],
DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.8, 0.6, and 0.4 if sSL ¼ 3.9, 2.1, and 0.62 m/s, respec-
tively [280]. The results of the DNS of V-shaped ﬂames, reported by
Domingo et al. [295], show that DU/(sSL) is much less than DV/
(sSL) < 0.5.
For ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets, Li et al. [227] and Stevens
et al. [228] have reported axial proﬁles of ru00c00=r. Invoking the BML
Eq. (47) and associating the peak values of this second moment
with the peak value of ~cð1  ~cÞ, we have estimated DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.8 if
sSL ¼ 1.9 m/s
[227],
DU/(sSL) ¼ 1.2
if
sSL ¼ 0.4 m/s
[228],
DU/
(sSL) ¼ 0.7 if sSL ¼ 2.4 m/s [228], and DU/(sSL) ¼ 0.4 if sSL ¼ 1.8 m/s
[228].
All in all, the above data show that if scalar transport is coun-
tergradient, then the slip velocity is typically of the order of, but less
than, sSL. In conﬁned ﬂames, however, the slip velocity may be
substantially higher under the inﬂuence of the mean pressure
gradient. For instance, Shepherd et al. [257] have reported
DV > 5 m/s for a rich F ¼ 1.2 propane–air ﬂame characterized by
sSL ¼ 2.7 m/s.
The above discussion focuses on the turbulent transport of
a scalar that characterizes a burning mixture. Combustion can also
affect
other
turbulent
ﬂuxes.
For
instance,
Cheng
et
al.
[136,206,207] have documented the so-called negative turbulent
viscosity in V-shaped ﬂames, i.e. both u0v0ðvu=vyÞ and u0v0ðvv=vxÞ are
positive so that term I in Eq. (23) serves to decrease the ATKE.
Fig. 19. Normalized slip velocities DU/(sSL) obtained from stoichiometric methane–air
V-shaped (up), Bunsen (middle), and impinging jet (down) ﬂames with similar char-
acteristics of the incident turbulence. In each ﬂame, measurements were performed in

--- Page 34 ---

Similar to countergradient scalar ﬂux, large positive values of the
mean Reynolds stress u0v0 were controlled by the slip velocity in
these experiments, see the intermittency term in Eq. (49). Negative
turbulent viscosity has also been reported by Heitor et al. [244].
Driscoll and Gulati [243] have measured the second ru02 and
third ru03 moments in an oblique unconﬁned ﬂame and have found
that the sign of the latter moment indicates the ﬂux of the ATKE
toward ~c ¼ 0:6 associated with the peak value of the former
moment. They called this phenomenon the ‘‘countergradient
diffusion of TKE.’’
3.4. Summary
The experimental and DNS data reviewed above clearly show
that premixed combustion can substantially affect both uncondi-
tioned (stronger) and conditioned (weaker) moments of a ﬂuctu-
ating velocity ﬁeld and reverse the direction of turbulent scalar ﬂux
at least if the incident turbulence is sufﬁciently weak, i.e. a ratio of
u0/(sSL) is low.
Such effects are commonly more pronounced (i) in the velocity-
vector component that is closer to the normal to the ﬂame brush,
(ii) in weakly turbulent ﬂows, and (iii) in mixtures characterized by
a higher density ratio and a higher laminar ﬂame speed. Particular
features of the inﬂuence of premixed combustion on turbulence are
very sensitive to ﬂame type (ﬂow geometry, stabilization mecha-
nism, conﬁned or not, etc.), incident turbulence and mixture
characteristics, the point of measurement or the time after ignition
(e.g. in unsteady DNS), etc.
The high values of the unconditioned moments (u02, v02, k, u0v0,
etc.) well documented in various weakly turbulent premixed
ﬂames are mainly caused by the unburned–burned intermittency.
The transition from gradient to countergradient transport has
been observed in many experimental [129,130,132,135,220,240]
and DNS [128,131,274,295] studies by varying either the density
ratio [274], or the normalized rms turbulent velocity u0/SL [128–
132], or distance from the ﬂame holder [135,220,240,295], or ﬂame
development time [128].
In open ﬂames, the slip velocity is typically of the order of, but
less than, sSL if scalar transport is countergradient.
4. Modeling of the effects of combustion on turbulence
The effects of combustion on turbulence are commonly modeled
using Eqs. (21)–(23) in RANS simulations. Accordingly, the large
part of this section is devoted to this approach. At the end of the
section, we will brieﬂy discuss some alternative approaches. Note
that we have already discussed direct numerical simulations in the
previous section, because we consider DNS to be a numerical
experiment, rather than modeling.
4.1. Balance equations for the second moments
The RHS of Eq. (21) for the ﬂux ru00
i c00 contains seven terms, ﬁve
of which have to be closed. These are transport term ii, cross-
dissipation terms iii and iv, term v, which involves the ﬂuctuating
pressure gradient, and reaction term vii. Term i is closed if the
Favre-averaged Reynolds stresses ru00
i u00
j are evaluated solving Eq.
(22). Term vi is closed if c00 is evaluated using Eq. (45). We may note,
however, that Nishiki et al. [278, 280] were forced to decrease the
RHS of Eq. (45) by a factor of 0.8 in order for the term c00vp=vx
evaluated in the DNS to agree with the same term closed using Eq.
(45) (see Fig. 7 in Ref. [280]). Fig. 8a reported by Chakraborty and
Cant [291] also shows that the BML Eq. (45) overestimates c00
approximately by a factor of two under the conditions of their DNS.
The RHSs of Eqs. (22) or (23) contain ﬁve terms, three of which
have to be closed. These are transport term II, dissipation term III,
and ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure gradient term IV. Term V is closed
if u00
i is evaluated using Eq. (48). The results of the DNS by Nishiki
et al. [278,279] agree well with Eq. (48), see Fig. 4 in Ref. [279],
where term r1u00vp=vx evaluated in the DNS is compared with the
same term closed using Eq. (48).
Let us ﬁrst consider the magnitudes of various terms in the
balance Eqs. (21)–(23) in order to highlight the most important
phenomena and, then, review various closures of the above terms
ii–v, vii, and II–IV. Since almost all the models discussed in this
subsection are based on the BML approach, let us assume that Eqs.
(44)–(52) hold unless otherwise stated. However, we should keep
in mind that the validity of the BML approach in highly turbulent
ﬂames may be disputed due to local quenching and broadening of
the instantaneous ﬂame front.
4.1.1. Various terms in the balance equations for the second
moments
All the terms on the RHSs of Eqs. (22) and (23) were ﬁrst eval-
uated using DNS by Rutland et al. [270,271]. The results (e.g. see
Fig. 20) show that both the Reynolds-stress term I (more precisely,
the mean dilatation part of it) and the viscous term III serve to sink
velocity ﬂuctuations (either ru00
1u00
1 [270] or ~k [271]), whereas the
pressure terms IV and V are the dominant source of ﬂuctuations.
The mean pressure gradient term V played a more important role in
the simulations by Rutland and Cant [270], whereas the ﬂuctuating
pressure gradient term IV was markedly higher than term V in the
ﬂame computed by Zhang and Rutland [271].
Basically similar results were obtained in DNS by Nishiki et al.
[278,279] (see Fig. 21). The pressure terms IV and V were the
dominant source of ~k, with term V playing a more important role in
ﬂame H characterized by s ¼ 6.53, but a less important role in ﬂame
L characterized by s ¼ 1.5. The mean dilatation (term I) and viscous
dissipation (term III) served to reduce ~k, with the latter term being
substantially larger, especially in the case L. The transport term II
was markedly less than but still comparable with terms III–V.
Treurniet et al. [142] have evaluated various terms in the
balance equation for the Reynolds-averaged ATKE written for
a variable-density case. Since this equation differs substantially
from Eq. (23), the reported results cannot be used straightforwardly
to assess terms I–V in the latter equation. Nevertheless, it is worth
Fig. 20. Normalized terms in the balance Eq. (23) for ~k determined by Eq. (24).
(i) ru00u00ðv~u=vxÞ, (ii) dissipation term, (iii) transport term including pressure diffu-
sion, (iv) u00ðvp=vxÞ, (v) p0ðvu00

A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
34


--- Page 35 ---

noting that these results show that (i) the mean dilatation term is
an important sink term, (ii) the mean pressure gradient term is the
main source term, but (iii) the ﬂuctuating pressure gradient plays
a minor role in the ﬂames modeled by Treurniet et al. [142].
All the terms on the RHS of Eq. (21) were ﬁrst evaluated using
DNS by Rutland and Cant [270] and by Trouve´ et al. [127]. In the
former DNS, the ﬂux ru00c00 was countergradient (with the excep-
tion of the leading edge of the ﬂame brush), whereas the latter
DNS yielded gradient behavior. In both cases, the ﬂuctuating
pressure gradient term v played an important role and served to
cause countergradient scalar transport. In the ﬂame studied by
Rutland and Cant [270], the mean pressure gradient term vi was
even more important than term v and also served to promote
countergradient scalar transport. In the ﬂame investigated by
Trouve´ et al. [127], term vi was markedly lower than term v and
acted in the opposite direction during the early stage of ﬂame
development. At later stages, the mean pressure gradient term
changed signs, served to promote countergradient scalar trans-
port, and become larger than term v. All other terms on the RHS of
Eq. (21) were substantially less than the pressure terms v and vi in
the simulations by Rutland and Cant [270], but the production
term i (more precisely, the contribution of the gradient of the
mean combustion progress variable into the production term)
played an important role and served to cause gradient scalar
transport in the DNS by Trouve´ et al. [127].
The DNS by Veynante et al. [128] also emphasized the
important role played by the pressure terms v and vi in Eq. (21).
At u00/SL ¼ 2, the two terms and the reaction term vii served to
promote countergradient scalar transport. The dissipation terms
iii and iv acted in the opposite direction. All ﬁve of the afore-
mentioned terms were of the same order, and the transport term
ii was also signiﬁcant. Thus, in this case, no unclosed term on the
RHS of Eq. (21) was negligible. At a higher u00/SL ¼ 5, the ﬂuctu-
ating pressure gradient term v and the contribution of ru002V~c to
term i increased (they were roughly proportional to u002) and
dominated in Eq. (21).
Veynante and Poinsot [131] have shown that the pressure terms
v and vi dominate and counterbalance one another when a strong
pressure gradient is imposed due to a constant acceleration of the
coordinate framework attached to the ﬂame. Based on this obser-
vation, Veynante and Poinsot [131] proposed closing the two
pressure terms jointly. When the imposed pressure gradient
promoted countergradient (gradient) scalar transport, the ﬂuctu-
ating pressure gradient term v promoted gradient (counter-
gradient) transport.
In the DNS by Nishiki et al. [278,280], the pressure terms v and vi
and the reaction term vii served to promote countergradient scalar
transport. The three terms were of the same order in case L, with
low heat release s ¼ 1.5, but the mean pressure term vi played
a more important role in cases M (s ¼ 4) and H (s ¼ 6.53). These
three terms were counteracted by terms i, iii, and iv. Although the
magnitudes of the latter three terms were markedly less than
the magnitudes of the pressure terms, none of the terms was
negligible. The transport term ii was also comparable with the
pressure terms.
In the DNS by Chakraborty and Cant [291], none of the terms on
the RHS of Eq. (21) was negligible at Le ¼ 1 (see Fig. 3d in the cited
paper). In this Figure, the pressure terms are the highest positive
terms and serve to cause countergradient scalar transport, while
term vii and the ﬁrst of two terms i are the largest negative terms.
Under the conditions of these DNS, the negative terms dominate
and the scalar transport shows gradient behavior for Le ¼ 1, see
Fig. 2a in the cited paper.
An important role played by the ﬂuctuating pressure in the
balance of the second moments was also shown by a few experi-
mental studies. In particular, Chandran et al. [171] have reported
that the so-called pressure-diffusion term vp0v0=vy, which is a part
of term IV, see Eq. (108), is higher than the shear-stress term
rv02ðvv=vyÞ (a part of term I) in a turbulent Bunsen ﬂame. In
a subsequent study, Waldherr et al. [172] evaluated the axial
gradient of r0p0 [this term is linked to the gradient of c0p0, which is
a part of term v in Eq. (21), see Eq. (109)] in a Bunsen ﬂame and
found this term larger by a factor of ﬁve than the largest of the other
terms in the balance equation for r0v00 (the second moments r0v00
and rv00c00 are straightforwardly linked within the framework of the
BML approach).
Ballal [253–255] measured (i) the convection and (ii) shear
stress terms in the statistically stationary balance equation for the
Reynolds-averaged k, and (iii) evaluated the dissipation term 3
using the measured values of k and L and the following estimate
3 ¼ 1:5k3=2=L. The remaining terms, which were called ‘‘advection’’
terms and involved the triple correlations and the pressure
gradient, were calculated to balance the three above terms. The
obtained results show that all the aforementioned terms may play
an important role depending on mixture and turbulence charac-
teristics. In the conﬁned ﬂames studied by Ballal [253–255], the
shear stress and dissipation terms were always positive and
negative, respectively. The advection term was negative (positive)
for lower (higher) values of u0/SL. In a subsequent paper [308], Ballal
(i) used the same experimental data to evaluate terms I, III, and
II þ IV þ V (advection) in the Favre-averaged balance Eq. (23), and
(ii) estimated the ratio of the advection term to term I to be equal to
Fig. 21. Various terms in the balance Eq. (23) for ~k, evaluated in cases H (s ¼ 6.53) and L
(s ¼ 1.5). (U) unsteady term, (C) convection term. From the Ph.D. thesis by Nishiki

A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102

0.2–0.4. It is worth noting that only positive values of the advection
term were reported in Ref. [308], probably because only part of the
original database [253–255] (e.g. the part associated with high u0/
SL) was processed.
Driscoll and Gulati [243] measured all the terms in the balance
Eq. (23) for ~k, with the exception of terms III and IV. The reported
results show that the mean pressure gradient is an important
source of ~k, whereas term I is a sink term due to the mean
dilatation.
In a ﬂame stabilized by a disk bafﬂe, however, the mean pressure
gradient was reported [244] to play a minor role in the total
production of ~k and ru00v00 ‘‘throughout the ﬂow,’’ in part, because
the mean axial pressure gradient changed signs in the middle of the
ﬂame brush. The total production of ~k and ru00v00 was dominated by
the shear-stress term I in this highly sheared ﬂow. Nevertheless,
even in this case, the mean pressure gradient term vi [see Eq. (21)]
was dominant in the balance equation for axial scalar ﬂux and
played an important role in the balance of radial ﬂux. In the latter
case, term vi was balanced by term i and served to promote
gradient transport, because the mean radial pressure gradient was
pointed at reactants. The transport term ii played an important role
in the balance of the axial scalar ﬂux.
Thus, the above DNS and experimental data show that the
pressure-gradient terms v–vi and IV–V play a signiﬁcant role in the
balance equations for the second moments ru00
i u00
j and ru00
i c00 in
premixed turbulent ﬂames. However, other unclosed terms in Eqs.
(21), (22), and (23) are not negligible in a general case and, thus, all
these terms need closure. Models developed for this purpose are
reviewed in the next subsections.

4.1.3.1. Dissipation balance equation. The exact balance equation
for ~3 can be derived [325] by differentiating the Navier–Stokes
equation for ui with respect to xj, multiplying the result with n(vui/
vxj), averaging, and summing up all nine such averaged equations.
Note that the exact balance equation derived in this way contains
66 terms in the case of variable density [326]. However, even in the
case of constant density, the exact equation is very difﬁcult to
model, because certain unclosed terms in it scale as Ret
1/2 and
become inﬁnite as Ret/N, whereas their sum remains ﬁnite
[325,327]. This basic problem stems from the fact that the instan-
taneous dissipation rate is very high in very thin zones character-
ized by large spatial gradients.
Contemporary engineering models of turbulence follow an
alternative approach, which does not deal with the exact balance
equation for the dissipation rate. The approach was ﬁrst suggested
by Kolmogorov [328] based on the Richardson cascade hypothesis
[329] and, then, was developed by many groups, as discussed
elsewhere [330].
According to Richardson [329] and Kolmogorov [328], largest-
scale turbulent eddies withdraw energy from the mean ﬂow and
transfer it to eddies of a smaller scale. The latter eddies further
transfer the energy to eddies of a still smaller scale, etc. Finally,
the energy dissipates, i.e. it is transformed into thermal energy by
the molecular viscosity in the smallest eddies, the size of which is
of the order of the Kolmogorov length scale h. Thus, the viscous
dissipation rate is a basic characteristic of turbulence on all scales,
as 3 is equal to the rate of the energy withdrawal from the mean
ﬂow. Therefore, difﬁculties in closing the exact equation for 3,
controlled by smallest-scale turbulent eddies, may be overcome by
considering balance equations for quantities k and kmLn, controlled
by largest-scale turbulent eddies, and then, evaluating 3fk3=2=L.
Such an approach was ﬁrst proposed by Kolmogorov [328] for k and
a turbulent frequency k1=2=L. Later on, several scientists proposed
using alternative pairs of balance equations, one for the TKE and
another either for the integral length scale L, or for the mean ens-
trophy ukukfk3=L2, or for the dissipation rate 3fk3=2=L, etc., as
discussed elsewhere [61,330].
Among
various
two-equation
models
of
constant-density
turbulent ﬂows, i.e. models that invoke balance equations for k and
kmLn, the k–3 model [122,331] is the most widely used in engi-
neering applications. It invokes Eq. (31) with

Fourth, the important difference between ﬂames and constant-
density ﬂows consists of the expansion of the ﬂow in the ﬂame,
which leads to substantial dilatation. In mathematical terms, the
difference consists of Vu s 0 in ﬂames, whereas Vu ¼ 0 in
constant-density ﬂows. Dilatation can play an important role in the
balance of the TKE, because the vorticity Eq. (13) straightforwardly
shows that dilatation reduces vorticity, see term 3, due to the
conservation of the angular momentum of gas. Accordingly, extra
terms that are proportional to dilatation have been incorporated
into the balance equation for dissipation in ﬂames. For instance, if
turbulent viscosity is invoked [see Eq. (93)], then, the part of the
production term P [see Eq. (35)], associated with the term in square
brackets on the RHS of Eq. (93), yields terms proportional to Vu and
(Vu)2 in Eq. (95).
Moreover, one more term C34r~3Vu has been added to the RHS of
either Eq. (91) recast into the Favre-averaged form [326,337–339]
or Eq. (95) [334]. Such a modiﬁed ~3-equation has mainly been
applied to turbulent ﬂows in reciprocating engines, however,
different authors specify substantially different values of constant
C34 based on different physical arguments.
After a review of various modiﬁcations of the 3-equation for
combustion applications, it is worth stressing that the dissipation
equation of the k–3 model, as well as a kmLn-equation of another
two-equation
model
of
constant-density
turbulent
ﬂows,
is
a balance equation for a large length or time scale of turbulence. The
use of such an equation for simulating combustion implies, in fact,
that turbulent ﬂow in a ﬂame can be characterized with the same
singlelargescale. Thisassumption has notyet been justiﬁed andmay
be disputed by pointing out that small-scale ﬂame-turbulence
interaction plays an important role in the balance of the ATKE in
combustion, e.g. see term IV in Eq. (23) and discussion in Section 2.3.
Modeling the dissipation rate is deﬁnitely one of the most difﬁcult
unresolved problems of premixed turbulent combustion.

4.1.4. Pressure ﬂuctuations
When applying Eqs. (21) and (22) to premixed ﬂames, the most
difﬁcult and still unresolved problem consists of closing terms v
and IV that involve the ﬂuctuating pressure gradient.
Initially, Bray et al. [98,100,101,104,105] neglected these terms.
Later, they were taken into account by invoking models developed
for constant-density ﬂows. Borghi and Dutoya [103] did this to
account for the ﬂuctuating concentration–pressure gradient term v
in Eq. (21). The problem of closing the ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure
gradient term IV in ﬂames was ﬁrst addressed by Jones [327] and
many subsequent models followed his proposal.

papers by Champion et al. [310–313].

combustion on the ATKE.

4.1.4.5. Pressure diffusion. The importance of the pressure-diffu-
sion term on the RHS of Eq. (129) has been demonstrated by Rut-
land and Cant [270], Zhang and Rutland [271], Nishiki et al.
[278,279], and Treurniet et al. [142] in the DNS of statistically
planar, 1D ﬂames. For instance, Fig. 10 in Ref. [271] shows that
pressure diffusion contributes substantially to the xx-component of
the ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure gradient tensor on the LHS of Eq.
(128). In these DNS, the pressure diffusion dominates at low ~c < 0:2
and is comparable to the sum of the redistribution and pressure
dilatation terms at large ~c > 0:6. Thus, although pressure diffusion
does not affect the net balance of the ATKE, it may play an impor-
tant role in the transport of energy within the ﬂame brush and,
thus, should not be neglected in combustion models. For instance,
the same DNS results (see Fig. 26) indicate that pressure diffusion
overwhelms other contributions to the transport term vT 1kk=vx in
the balance equation for ~k and cannot be closed invoking the
approximation of gradient transport, such as Eq. (75).
The present authors are not aware of any model aimed at
evaluating the pressure-diffusion term in ﬂames.

not involve such a term. On the other hand, the models of the
pressure dilatation term, developed by Zhang and Rutland [271] and
by Hulek and Lindstedt [350], are mentioned in Ref. [316].

When applying these and subsequent equations to RANS simula-

i c00 using Eqs. (46) and (47).
It is worth noting that Domingo and Bray [283] have used the
gradients Vpu and Vpb of the conditioned pressures instead of the
conditioned pressure gradients ðVpÞu and ðVpÞb in Eqs. (141) and
(142). The replacement of ðVpÞu and ðVpÞb by Vpu and Vpb
implicitly assumes that taking the conditioned mean and differ-
entiation commute. However, such an assumption is not justiﬁed in
a general case, because the domain of conditional averaging varies
in space and time. For instance, Libby [117] has shown that
‘‘conditioned velocity components are not divergence-free’’ even in
a constant-density case, see also Eqs. (189) and (190) in Section 4.4.
According to the above decomposition, term IV is affected by the
ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure gradient correlation conditioned on
the nonreactive mixture [the ﬁrst terms on the RHSs of Eqs. (141)
and (142)], the intermittency (the second terms on the RHSs of the
two equations), and by the ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure gradient
correlation within ﬂamelets [the last term on the RHS of Eq. (140)].
The DNS data obtained by Domingo and Bray [283] (see Fig. 3 in the
cited paper)17 show that the nonreactive contribution is more
important at higher turbulent Reynolds numbers (Ret ¼ 46–64), but
the two other contributions are more important at Ret ¼ 13–23.
Domingo and Bray [283] have proposed modeling the ﬂamelet
contribution as follows:

paper and the sensitivity of the model to these input parameters is
not discussed.

DNS data, see Fig. 7d in the cited paper.

Thus, Bradley et al. [309] have not used the BML approach, but
have invoked an approximation for the PDF P(c), which is more
general than Eq. (41). Indeed, Eq. (171) reduces to Eq. (41) in the
particular case of a/0 and b/0. On the face of it, such an approach
appears to be very attractive and it is widely used in simulations of
diffusion ﬂames after the work by Janicka and Kollmann [361]. This
approach was ﬁrst applied to modeling premixed ﬂames by Peters
[362] and it was addressed by other researchers from time to time,
e.g. see Ref. [363–365] for recent examples.
However, if we consider the ﬂame-front regime of premixed
turbulent combustion, the b-function approach may result in
substantial errors in evaluating the mean reaction rate W. Indeed, if
the probability g  1, then, the b-function parameters a  1 and
b  1, because the ﬁrst terms in the square brackets on the RHSs of
Eq. (173) are very close to unity. Accordingly, even a small error in
computing ~c by solving Eq. (18) and/or rc002 by solving Eq. (20), can
result in substantial errors in calculating the small parameters
Fig. 29. Comparison of DNS data (solid lines) with the closure (dashed lines) of the
reaction term vii, given by Eq. (175). From the thesis by Nishiki [278], reproduced with
permission.
18 The same approximation has been invoked by Libby and Bray [124] to close the
ﬂame-front contribution to the dissipation terms III and iii þ iv by Eqs. (101) and
(102).
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
48


--- Page 49 ---

a and b and, therefore, substantial errors in evaluating W. Errors in
computing ~c and rc002 are inevitable, not only for numerical reasons,
but also and mainly because of the limitations of the physical
models invoked to close the RHSs of Eqs. (18) and (20).
In other words, a too small quantity g cannot be determined
accurately by subtracting one ﬁnite quantity from another ﬁnite
quantity if the two quantities are calculated even with small errors.
Therefore, the mean reaction rate, which is equal to the probability
g multiplied by the reaction rate integrated over the ﬂame front,
cannot be determined accurately too.
The b-function approach may be promising if the probability g is
not too small. For instance, Chen and Bilger [194] have reported that
the b function calculated using measured values of ~c and rc002 agree
reasonably well with the PDFs P(c) obtained from six different
Bunsen ﬂames characterized by g z 0.2 in the middle of the ﬂame
brushes. Mantel and Bilger [266] have drawn a similar conclusion by
processing the DNS database by Trouve´ and Poinsot [265], associated
with the substantial probability g [the parameter g evaluated using
Eq. (70) was as low as 0.5]. To the contrary, Jin et al. [364] have
reported substantial differences between the b-function approxi-
mation and the PDF P(c) obtained by processing DNS data.

4.1.7. Applications and validation
Certain
applications
of
the
models
discussed
above
are
summarized in Table 3.
Bray and Libby [98] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (20), and (23) to
a statistically planar, 1D, oblique ﬂame. The dissipation term III
scaled as r~k3=2=L. The pressure terms IV and V in Eq. (23) were
neglected. The gradient diffusion approximation was invoked for
the second moments ru00
i q00 with q s ui00. The reported numerical
results show that if the angle 4 between the ﬂame and the
upstream ﬂow direction is sufﬁciently small, the production of
ATKE through shear stress exceeds the degradation of ~k due to
dilatation, and the ﬂame may increase the ATKE.
Libby and Bray [100] extended the above model by analyzing
various dependencies of turbulent diffusivity and scalar dissipation
rate on the mean density. For certain dependence, the computed
angle 4 was comparable with the data obtained by Wright and
Zukoski [366] from oblique conﬁned ﬂames stabilized behind
a bluff body. Note that the simulations show a weak dependence of
4 on s in line with the experiments.
Libby and Bray [104] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (21), and (22) to
a statistically planar, 1D, ﬂame normal to the mean ﬂow. The
pressure terms v in Eq. (21) and IV in Eq. (22) were neglected. The
transport terms ii and II were closed using Eqs. (51) and (52) sup-
plemented with Eqs. (76), (77), and ðu03Þu ¼ ðu03Þb ¼ 0. The dissi-
pation terms iii and iv were modeled using Eq. (102) and the
dissipation term III was modeled using Eq. (101). The reaction term
was closed using Eq. (169) with fn ¼ 5/6. Countergradient scalar
ﬂux was predicted in these simulations.
Bray et al. [105] modiﬁed this model by replacing Eq. (77) with
Eq. (78). By adjusting three model constants, K1 ¼0.85, K2 ¼ 0.25,
and K3 ¼ 0.1, these authors obtained dependencies of ru002ð~cÞ and
ru00c00ð~cÞ which were close to the data measured by Moss [125] (cf.
dashed lines and symbols in Fig. 30). In particular, CGD and an
increase in ru002 with ~c were predicted.
Driscoll and Gulati [243] tested ‘‘exactly the same’’ model by
comparing the proﬁles of ru002ð~cÞ, ru003ð~cÞ, uuð~cÞ, ubð~cÞ, ðu02Þuð~cÞ,
ðu02Þbð~cÞ, ru00c00ð~cÞ, u00ð~cÞ, and ru002c00ð~cÞ computed for a statistically
stationary, planar, 1D ﬂame with the experimental data obtained
from unconﬁned, oblique, methane–air, turbulent ﬂames. For most
proﬁles, the qualitative agreement between the measured and
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
49


--- Page 50 ---

computed results was impressive. In particular, the model pre-
dicted; (i) values of ru002 in the burned mixture higher than in the
unburned mixture, (ii) an increase in ru002 by s, (iii) the existence of
peak values of ru002 within the ﬂame brush, (iv) an increase in uu
and ub when either ~c or s increases, (v) an increase in ðu02Þu with ~c,
(vi) countergradient scalar ﬂux ru00c00 and an increase in its
magnitude
by
s,
and
(vii)
the
form
of
the
ru003ð~cÞ-
and
ru002c00ð~cÞ-curves with zero values at ~c ¼ 0 and ~c ¼ 1. Quantitative
agreement was even obtained between certain measured and
computed proﬁles [uuð~cÞ, ðu02Þuð~cÞ, ru00c00ð~cÞ and u00ð~cÞ]. However,
since the measured mean ﬂame brush thickness increased with
distance from the stabilizing rod in the experiments [243], the
agreement between the results of computations of a statistically
stationary, planar, 1D ﬂame with the data measured at a particular
distance (i.e. at a particular stage of ﬂame development) does not
seem to be a reliable validation.

Moreover, the model yielded an increase in ðu02Þb with ~c at
0:1 < ~c < 0:8, whereas the measurements indicated the opposite
trend. To resolve the problem, Driscoll and Gulati [243] measured
all terms in Eqs. (78)–(80) associated with the statistically planar
1D turbulent ﬂame, and found that Eq. (78), which was invoked in
the discussed computations, and Eq. (80) were contradicted by the
experiment, whereas Eq. (79) was ‘‘signiﬁcantly better.’’
Libby [19] extended the discussed model [105] by replacing Eq.
(78) with Eq. (79) with Kr ¼ Kp ¼ 0.5 and slightly improved the
agreement with the data measured by Moss [125] (cf. solid lines
and symbols in Fig. 30). For ðu02Þbð~cÞ, the agreement between the
measurements and computations was worse, but the difference
between measured and computed values was comparable to the
accuracy of the experiment.
This model was extended by Masuya and Libby [318] to simulate
oblique ﬂames. The extended model contained three more empir-
ical constants (in addition to K1 ¼0.85, K2 ¼ 0.25, and K3 ¼ 0.1). The
simulations showed that the mean pressure gradient term V was
much larger than two sink terms, I and III, and this resulted in an
increase in the ATKE within the ﬂame provided that the heat
release factor was sufﬁciently large (s > 3). It is worth emphasizing,
however, that the increase in ru002 within the ﬂame brush was
mainly controlled by the intermittency. Similarly, term vi in Eq. (21)
was the only term that caused the CGD in the simulated ﬂame,
whereas terms i, iiiþiv, and vii acted in the opposite manner. No
quantitative comparison with experiments was reported.
Chandran and Strahle [319] extended the above model by Bray
et al. [105] by modeling terms v and vi in Eq. (21) together using Eq.
(132) with lF ¼ L and modeling terms IV and V in Eq. (22) using Eq.
(131) with lI ¼ L. No comparison with experiments was reported. As
compared with the original model by Bray et al. [105], the simu-
lations by Chandran and Strahle [319] yielded (i) a monotonic
increase in ru002 with ~c provided that the constants C1 in Eq. (131)
and C1c in Eq. (132) were larger than 2.5, and (ii) the gradient
diffusion with the absolute value of ru00c00 was increased by C1 and
C1c. The lack of CGD in the simulations was attributed by Chandran
and Strahle [319] to limitations of the closures of terms ii, iii, iv, II,
and III, which did not involve the pressure gradient.
Borghi and Escudie [333] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (20), (21) and
(23) to a statistically stationary, 2D, oblique hydrogen-air ﬂame
stabilized on a catalytic wire near the walls of a channel. When
solving Eq. (21) written for the mass fraction of the deﬁcient reac-
tant, rather than for the combustion progress variable, the convec-
tion and transport terms were neglected, i.e. production was
assumed to be equal to dissipation. The model of the transport term
II in Eq. (23) was not speciﬁed in the cited paper. The dissipation of
the TKE was computed solving Eq. (95) with Cm ¼ 0.09, s3 ¼ 1.3,
C31 ¼ C33 ¼1.5, and C32 ¼ 2  0.44 exp(Ret/6)2. The dissipation
terms iii and iv in Eq. (21) were neglected. The ﬂuctuating velocity–
pressure gradient term IV in Eq. (23) was also neglected, whereas
term v in Eq. (21) was closed using Eqs. (125) and (126) with
b1 ¼0.66 and b2 ¼ 0.2. The reaction term vii in Eq. (21) was modeled
using Eqs. (166) and (167). Note that although Borghi and Escudie
[333] did not invoke the BML approach to close the mean pressure
gradient terms vi and V, the submodel they used still yielded
u00
i fru00
i c00=r and c00frc002=r. The net model was tested by comparing
the transverse proﬁles of the hydrogen mass fraction and the ATKE,
measured and computed at a single distance from the wire that held
the ﬂame. The agreement between measured and computed data
was acceptable for the former proﬁle, but poor for the latter one.
Masuya [323] tested his joint closure approach (see Section
4.1.6) against the data by Moss [125] and obtained substantially
better agreement than Libby [19] between the measured and
computed dependence of ðu02Þbð~cÞ. However, the model yielded
a slight decrease in ðu02Þu when ~c was increased (see Fig. 2c in the
cited paper). To the contrary, both the experiment by Moss [125]
and the model by Libby [19] show a substantial (by a factor of four
approximately) increase in ðu02Þu with ~c.
Cant and Bray [322] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (21), and (22) to
a statistically planar, 1D ﬂame propagating in a vessel. The transport
terms ii and II were closed using Eqs. (51) and (52) supplemented
with Eqs. (76), (80), and (81). The dissipation terms iii and iv were
Fig. 30. Comparison of the models by Bray et al. [105] (dashed lines) and by Libby [19] (solid lines) with the experimental data by Moss [125] (symbols). From the paper by Libby
[19] 1985, with permission fron Elsevier.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
