modeled using Eq. (102). The dissipation term III was modeled using
Eqs. (96) and (103). The reaction term was closed using Eq. (169)
with fn ¼ 5/6. Terms v and IV that involve pressure ﬂuctuations
were calculated using Eq. (125) and Eqs. (111)–(116), respectively.
No comparison with measurements was reported in the cited paper.
Bradley et al. [309] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (20)–(22) to a statis-
tically planar,1D, expanding, open ﬂame. The transport terms ii and
II were closed using Eqs. (74) and (75) with Cc ¼ 0.15 and Cs ¼ 0.22.
The dissipation terms iii and iv in Eq. (21) were neglected. The
dissipation term III in Eq. (22) was evaluated solving Eq. (96) with
C3 ¼ 0.15, C31 ¼1.44, C32 ¼ 1.92, C33 ¼1.44. Term IV that involve
pressure ﬂuctuations was calculated using Eqs. (111), (114), (118)
with C1 ¼1.8 and C2 ¼ 0. Term v that also involve pressure ﬂuctu-
ations was calculated using Eq. (125) with C1c ¼ 3.0, and C2c ¼ 0. The
reaction term was closed using Eqs. (170)–(173). No straightforward
comparison with measurements are reported in the cited paper, but
the turbulent ﬂame speeds computed using this model agree
reasonably well with the approximation [367] of experimental data
obtained from expanding spherical ﬂames. It is worth noting,
however, that the speeds of planar and spherical ﬂames, with all
other things being equal, should be different due to the substantial
effect of the mean ﬂame brush curvature on ﬂame kernel growth,
discussed elsewhere [368, 369, 370].
Lindstedt and Va´os [315,316] applied Eqs. (16)–(18), (21)–(22) to
simulate experiments by Gulati and Driscoll [242] with an oblique
lean methane–air ﬂame (F ¼ 0.8, u0/SL z 0.58, Ret ¼ 19), by Kostiuk
[371] with a lean propane–air ﬂame stabilized in two opposed jets
(F ¼ 0.9, SL ¼ 0.4 m/s), by Mastorakos [372] with a lean methane–air
ﬂame stabilized in two opposed jets (F ¼ 0.9, SL ¼ 0.32 m/s), and by
Cheng and Shepherd [174] with the stoichiometric ethane-air ﬂame
s9 stabilized in an impinging jet. The last experiment was numer-
ically modeled in Ref. [315] and the other three experiments were
simulated in Ref. [316].
All these measurements were computed using almost the same
model. In particular, the transport terms ii and II were closed using
Eqs. (74) and (75) with both Cs and Cc being equal to either 0.18
[315] or 0.22 [316]. The dissipation term III and the ﬂuctuating
velocity–pressure gradient term IV in Eq. (22) were jointly closed
using Eqs. (104) and (137), with ~3 being calculated solving either Eq.
(96) [316] or Eq. (105) [315]. The dissipation terms iii–iv, the ﬂuc-
tuating concentration–pressure gradient term v, and the reaction
term vii in Eq. (21) were jointly closed using Eqs. (138) and (168).
Although the whole model involves at least 15 constants (Cs, Cc, C0,
CAR, CAS, C3, C31, C32, C33, ﬁve constants in the tensor Gij, and a single
constant CR in an expression for the mean reaction rate W), only
constant CR was adjusted (but was the same in all the simulated
cases), whereas the other constants were either determined theo-
retically (CAR and CAS) or set to be equal to their counterparts used in
simulations of constant-density ﬂows.
The measurements by Gulati and Driscoll [242] were modeled
by simulating a statistically planar, 1D, expanding, open ﬂame until
steady state was reached in the ~c-space [316]. Such a method of
validation does not seem to be justiﬁed, because most practical
ﬂames are developing ﬂames, as reviewed elsewhere [43]. The
development of a statistically stationary turbulent ﬂow (e.g.
turbulence decay behind a grid, diffusion from a point source,
turbulent jets and wakes, premixed ﬂames) manifests itself in the
change of some ﬂow characteristic(s) along the mean streamline. In
particular, Gulati and Driscoll [242] clearly state that, ‘‘the ﬂame
brush has a thickness that increases with distance from the burner’’
(see p. 288 in [242]).
The proﬁles of ~uðxÞ, ~cðxÞ, uuðxÞ, and ubðxÞ, computed by Lindstedt
and Va´os [316] for a planar fully-developed ﬂame, agree very well
with the data measured along the normal to the mean ﬂame brush
at a particular distance from the burner exit. For the proﬁles of u0(x)
and v0(x), the agreement between measured and computed data
was markedly worse. In any case, such a test does not seem to
validate the model, which appears to underpredict both ﬂame
speed and thickness at larger distances from the burner due to the
growth of dt with distance, as reported by Gulati and Driscoll [242].
Since the mean velocity in the main stream direction was about
3 m/s and the domain of measurements was located at a distance
less than 42 mm from the burner exit [242], the ﬂame development
time was less than 14 ms at the point of measurement, i.e.
approximately equal to the turbulence time scale L/u0. Obviously,
the time is too short for the ﬂame to be fully developed.
The other aforementioned measurements were simulated by
solving 2D time-dependent balance equations, until steady state
was reached. This test of the model was straightforward and yiel-
ded encouraging results [316]. In particular, the computed axial
proﬁles of ~uðxÞ and cðxÞ agree very well with the data by Kostiuk
[371] (see Fig. 31a), and the agreement between the measured and

computed proﬁles of u0(x) and v0(x) is also good (see Fig. 31b). For
the experiments by Mastorakos [372], the proﬁles of ~cðxÞ and v0(x)
were well predicted in the simulations, whereas the agreement
between the measured and computed proﬁles of ~uðxÞ and u0(x) was
worse. Certainly the results are impressive, but such a test of the
model against only two sets of experimental data obtained from
very similar ﬂames characterized by low rms turbulent velocities
and by a very low turbulent Reynolds number is deﬁnitely not
sufﬁcient for validating the model.
The simulations of the experiments by Cheng and Shepherd [174]
performed by Lindstedt and Va´os [315] should not be considered to
validate the same model, because (i) constants Cs and Cc were
reduced in these simulations by 20%, (ii) Eq. (96) was replaced with
Eq. (105), but (iii) the effects of these changes on the computed
results were not discussed. The latter simulations were 2D and time-
dependent until steady state was reached. Comparison with the
measured data was straightforward. The measured proﬁles of ~uðxÞ
and ~cðxÞ were well predicted in the simulations. The agreement
between the measured and computed proﬁles of uuð~cÞ and ubð~cÞ
appears to be reasonable (see Fig. 32a), but the model under-
predicted rms velocities within the ﬂame (see Fig. 32b). Although
these results are encouraging, they are not sufﬁcient for thoroughly
validating the particular model used in these simulations. We may
note that the proﬁles of the mean velocity and the mean combustion
progress variable in the ﬂame s9 were also well predicted by
Karmed et al. [313] despite the fact that turbulent scalar ﬂux and
rms velocities were underpredicted in the latter simulations.
Recently, Lindstedt and Va´os [317] simulated highly turbulent
piloted jet ﬂames F1 and F3 (the well-known experiments by Chen
et al. [186]) by solving Eqs. (16), (17), and (22) jointly with
a transport equation for the mass fraction PDF. The Reynolds-stress
Eq. (22) was closed invoking Eqs. (74), (96), and the SSG model
[354] of the pressure terms IV–V. The radial proﬁles of ~k obtained
by Chen et al. [186] from a highly turbulent ﬂame F1 were well
predicted. However, the model substantially (by a factor of three)
overpredicted the ~k obtained from ﬂame F3 characterized by
a lower Reynolds number and, thus, associated with a stronger
inﬂuence of combustion on turbulence. Note, that the closures of
Eq. (22), used by Lindstedt and Va´os in the above three papers
[315–317], differed from one another.
Champion et al. [310–313] applied a model of the effects of
combustion on turbulence and turbulent transport to several
premixed turbulent ﬂames. The present authors are aware of the
following tests of the model; (a) a lean (F ¼ 0.65) propane–air ﬂame
stabilized by a bluff body in a rectangular channel (the Poitiers
experiments [310]), (b) a rich (F ¼ 1.2) propane–air ﬂame stabilized
by a backward-facing step in a rectangular channel (the experi-
ments by Shepherd et al. [257]), (c) a lean (F ¼ 0.89) methane–air
ﬂame stabilized in an impinging jet (the experiments by Li et al.
[227]), and (d) the stoichiometric ethane-air ﬂame stabilized in an
impinging jet (ﬂame s9 investigated by Cheng and Shepherd [174]).
The model by Champion et al. [310–313] involves Eqs. (16)–(18),
(21), and (22). The transport terms ii and II are closed using Eqs. (74)
and (75) with Cs ¼ 0.25 and Cc ¼ 0.18. The dissipation terms iii and iv
in Eq. (21) are modeled using Eq. (102) with K1 ¼1. The viscous
dissipation was computed solving Eq. (96) with C3 ¼ 0.18, C31 ¼1.44,
C32 ¼1.92, and C33 ¼ 1.44. Terms IV and v that involve pressure
ﬂuctuations were calculated using Eqs. (119) and (127), respectively,
with C1 ¼1.8, C2 ¼ 0.6, C3 ¼ 0.3, C1c ¼ 3.0, C2c ¼ 0.5, and C3c ¼ 0.5.
The reaction term was closed using Eq. (169) with fn ¼ 3/4.
Although themodel involves 14constants (Cs,Cc,K1,C3, C31,C32, C33,C1,
C2, C3, C1c, C2c, C3c, and fn), not one of them was changed in the
simulations. However, three different models were used to calculate
the mean reaction rate W in cases (a) and (b), (c), and (d). Moreover,
the two different expressions for W invoked in cases (c) and (d)
were, in fact, empirical.
In case (a), the computed transverse proﬁles of ru002 and rv002
agreed well with the measured ones [311], but this result was
strongly devaluated by the inability of the model to predict the
measured transverse proﬁles of the mean temperature, see Fig. 33.
In case (b), the computed proﬁles of ru002ð~cÞ and ru00c00ð~cÞ agreed
well with the measured ones (see Fig. 34), as well as the axial and
transverse proﬁles of ðu02Þ1=2 [312]. The model underestimated the
mean axial velocity measured at different distances from the ﬂame-
holder, but the differences in the measured and computed u were
comparable with the accuracy of the measurements. The results of
this test are encouraging.
However, the model was not able to yield the CGD [311] docu-
mented in case (c) [227] and also markedly overpredicted ru002 and
rv002 in the ﬂame, whereas the measured proﬁles of ~uðxÞ and ~cðxÞ
were well predicted [311], probably due to the adjustment of the
closure for W.
Similarly, in case (d) also simulated by Lindstedt and Va´os [315],
the model well predicted uðxÞ and cðxÞ, but underpredicted the

Bray et al. [324] applied Eqs. (16), (17), (21), and (22) to simu-
lating the experiments performed by Li et al. [227] with ﬂames
stabilized in impinging jet ﬂows. Two lean (F ¼ 0.89) methane–air
ﬂames were investigated, with the sole difference between the two
ﬂames consisting of using two different perforated grids in order to
generate turbulence. Since the goal of the simulation was to test the
model of the effects of combustion on turbulence and turbulent
transport, the mean combustion progress variable proﬁle was taken
from measurements, rather than computed.
Eqs. (21) and (22) were closed as follows. The transport terms ii
and II were neglected. The dissipation terms iii and iv were
modeled using Eq. (102). The dissipation term III was modeled
using Eqs. (97) and (103). The pressure term v in Eq. (21) was closed
using Eqs. (144), (145), and (150). The pressure term IV in Eq. (22)
was closed using Eqs. (143)–(148). The reaction term was modeled
using Eq. (169). An important merit of the model consists of
yielding the following analytical expression

The numerical model involves seven constants (C31 ¼1.44,
C32 ¼ 1.92, C33 ¼ 0.5, K2 ¼ 0.75, C1 ¼1.8, C2 ¼ 0.6, and fn  K1 ¼0.5)
and three unknown geometric parameters nx, n2x, and n2r . The value
of 0.5 was assigned to the ﬁrst parameter by referring to the DNS by
Domingo and Bray [283]. The two other parameters, n2x ¼ 0:422 and
n2r ¼ 0:075, and constant K2 were adjusted ‘‘to bring the theory and
experiment into agreement’’ [324]. Moreover, two boundary
conditions (the rms velocity in the radial direction and the dissipa-
tion rate at the jet exit) were not known and were selected by
comparing the distributions of the rms velocity in the axial direction,
measured and computed in the reactant stream. These boundary
values were different in the two different ﬂames investigated. Thus,
the model involved ﬁve tuning parameters, at least three of which
were adjusted using results measured within the ﬂames.
The agreement between the model and experiment, reported by
Bray et al. [324], appears to be excellent, see Fig. 36. Later, Bray et al.
[373] wrote that they knew ‘‘no other predictions of turbulence
quantities in these ﬂows that show such good agreement with
experiments.’’ We fully agree with this claim. However, we are not
sure that the obtained agreement is sufﬁcient to validate the model.

by the model. This analytical expression involves a single unknown
parameter, the constant value 0.5, which has not been adjusted, but
has been assigned using the DNS data by Domingo and Bray [283].
Thus, Eq. (181) is a real theoretical prediction. Figs. 3c and 4d
plotted by Bray et al. in Ref. [324] validate this prediction and might
be considered to be solid support of the entire model. However, the
original data by Li et al. [227] indicate that the maximum absolute
values of the normalized axial scalar ﬂux differ by 30% (see Fig. 38)
in the two ﬂames, whereas Eq. (181) with a constant nx yields
exactly the same ﬂux. It is therefore unclear why the aforemen-
tioned Figs. 3c and 4d plotted in Ref. [324] show agreement
between Eq. (181) and the measurements in both ﬂames.
4.1.8. Concluding remarks
Theabovereviewofmodelsthatdealwiththebalanceequationsfor
the second moments ru00
i c00 and ru00
i u00
j in premixed turbulent ﬂames
clearly shows that this approach is still very far from being a predictive
method. It needs further development and extensive testing.
For each unclosed term in Eqs. (21) and (22), there are several
competitive models, each involving at least one or even stronger
simpliﬁcations.
Only a
few
such
models
have
been
tested
straightforwardly using DNS databases and the majority of the
tested models have been compared with a single DNS database, e.g.
Eqs. (80) and (81) [270], Eq. (103) [291], Eq. (124) [291], Eq. (125)
[283], Eq. (139) [291], Eqs. (140)–(145) [283], Eq. (149) [283], Eqs.
(164) [278,280]. Very few models have been tested against two
different DNS databases, e.g. Eq. (131) is not supported by the DNS
data obtained by Zhang and Rutland [271] and by Domingo and
Bray [283]. When one DNS database validated a particular model
developed by the authors of the DNS, another database did not
support the same model. For instance, Eq. (163) was validated by
the DNS data obtained by Veynante and Poinsot [131], but did not
agree with the DNS data by Domingo and Bray [283] and by
Chakraborty and Cant [291]. Eq. (106) was validated by Nishiki et al.
[280], but did not agree with the DNS data by Chakraborty and Cant
[291]. Eq. (155) was validated by Domingo and Bray [283], but
overestimated the DNS data by Chakraborty and Cant [291]. The
present authors are aware of only a few exceptions. First, Eq. (134)
was supported not only by the DNS data by Zhang and Rutland
[271], but also by the DNS data by Nishiki et al. [278,279]. Second,
Eq. (164) was supported not only by the DNS data by Nishiki et al.
[278,279], but also by the DNS data by Chakraborty and Cant [291].
Third, Eq. (169) was supported by the DNS data by Nishiki et al.
[278,279] and by Chakraborty and Cant [291].
Thus, we cannot claim that any model of any unclosed term in Eqs.
(21) and (22) is a well-validated model, although certain models
might be claimed to be widely recognized, e.g. Eqs. (74) and (75) for
the transport terms ii and II or Eq. (169) for the reaction term vii.
Various combinations of different submodels for different
unclosed terms in Eqs. (21) and (22) have yielded several net
models, as summarized in Table 3. Since each group uses its own
net model, there is no well-validated net model and only a few net
models have been tested by straightforwardly comparing numer-
ical results with experimental data. The net model by Champion
et al. [310–313] has been tested against a wider set of experimental
data than the competitive net models by Bray et al. [324] and by
Lindstedt and Va´os [315,316]. Although certain tests of the net
model by Champion et al. [310–313] yielded encouraging results
(see Fig. 34), other tests (e.g. Figs. 33 and 35) did not conﬁrm its
predictive capabilities. Two other aforementioned net models,
which were already tested straightforwardly, strongly need vali-
dation against a wider set of representative experimental data
obtained from substantially different ﬂames.
It is also worth emphasizing that it is very difﬁcult to draw solid
conclusions about the quality of any net model by comparing
computed and measured results for a particular experiment unless
the particular submodels of the net model are separately validated as
well. The discussed net models contain at least four different sub-
models for different groups of unclosed terms (ii and II, iii–iv and III, v
and IV, and vi), and each submodel involves at least one constant and

--- Page 57 ---

one assumption. Therefore, when testing a net model, the advan-
tages of one submodel may be devaluated by limitations of another
submodel. Similarly, the drawbacks of two different submodels may
cancel one another so that a net model that invokes wrong
assumptions may yield an impressive result. For instance, Bray et al.
[105] well predicted the turbulent scalar ﬂux and rms velocity
measured by Moss [125] in spite of neglecting important terms that
involved the ﬂuctuating pressure gradient. To the contrary, after
extending this net model to account for the neglected terms, Chan-
dran and Strahle [319] were not able to obtain the countergradient
scalar ﬂux documented by Moss [125]. Thus, an improvement of
a particular submodel weakened the net model.
Consequently, studies of the Favre-averaged balance equations
for the second moments, Eqs. (21) and (22), have improved our
understanding of the behavior of various unclosed terms in these
equations, but have not yet led to the development of a model able
to quantitatively predict the effects of combustion on turbulence
and turbulent transport. A few alternative approaches capable of
addressing these effects will be brieﬂy discussed in the next
subsections. The brevity of the following discussion is due to the
fact that there are only just a few applications of alternative
approaches to study the effects referred to.
4.2. Large eddy simulation
Large eddy simulation (LES) is a promising approach to
modeling complex turbulent ﬂows and, in particular, turbulent
ﬂames. In LES, large-scale vortexes are directly calculated, but the
effects of small-scale eddies on ﬂow and ﬂame still require physical
modeling. Although LES is more expensive and difﬁcult than RANS
from
the
computational
viewpoint,
the
former
approach
is
commonly considered to be more accurate for ﬂows where large-
scale unsteady motions play a substantial role. Since major indus-
trial applications of premixed combustion involve such motions
(e.g. the combustion process in reciprocating engines is inherently
transient, while statistically stationary combustion in a gas turbine
engine can become unstable due to thermoacoustic instabilities),
applications of LES to turbulent ﬂames are rapidly growing and
substantial progress has already been obtained in this ﬁeld, as
reviewed elsewhere [49,51].
However, contrary to more expensive DNS, it is difﬁcult to ﬁnd
a positive example of the use of LES for improving our basic
understanding of the interaction between a ﬂame and a turbulent
ﬂow under simple conditions (e.g. in the statistically one-
dimensional case). For this particular task, LES does not seem to be
substantially better than RANS, because ‘‘the essential rate-
controlling processes of molecular mixing and chemical reaction
occur at the smallest scales’’ [375] not resolved in LES. Therefore,
both RANS and LES require models of the same small-scale
phenomena, but, while the models used in RANS have been
developed and tested over several decades, the models used in LES
are new and strongly need validation. In fact, the vast majority of
combustion models used in LES have been recast using RANS
models. The state of premixed combustion modeling for LES
resembles the state of closing terms II–IV in Eq. (22) for RANS of
turbulent ﬂames. There are a number of competitive models, each
research group develops its own, but no well validated and widely
recognized approach has yet emerged.
Thus, LES has not yet contributed notably to understanding the
effects of premixed combustion on turbulence and only very few
LESs have addressed these effects.
Boger et al. [274] processed a DNS database (see Table 2) in order
to evaluate unresolved turbulent scalar ﬂux in LES. Both the CGD
and the GD directions of the unresolved ﬂux were documented
depending on the value of s, with the large values promoting the
CGD direction. This result conﬁrmed that the problem of modeling
pressure-driven turbulent transport is relevant not only to RANS
but also to LES. However, Boger et al. [274] pointed out that (i) the
unresolved ﬂux in the LES was substantially lower than ru00c00=r in
RANS, because a part of the latter ﬂux was directly resolved in the
LES and, hence, (ii) uncertainties in modeling the LES-ﬂux would
‘‘have probably less dramatic consequences than in RANS.’’ By
processing a 2D DNS database obtained for a V-shaped ﬂame (see
Table 2), Domingo et al. [295] conﬁrmed that the unresolved
streamwise scalar ﬂux associated with LES was low (Crvfcf D/
<rvc > z 0.04, where CaD is a ﬁltered value of a quantity a and
<r>af ¼ <ra><r><a>). However, the unresolved ﬂux in the
transverse direction associated with the normal to the mean ﬂame
brush was countergradient and not negligible. It was approximately
equal to CrvcD. Nevertheless, Domingo et al. [295] have hypothesized
that ‘‘the modeling of turbulent transport in premixed ﬂames may
be easier to handle with LES than with RANS,’’ because the transport
terms in the ﬁltered balance equation for the combustion progress
variable are much lower than other terms in this equation. We may
note, however, that the transport term A in the Favre-averaged Eq.
(18) may be much smaller than other terms in that equation (e.g.
see DNS by Rutland and Cant [270], by Trouve´ et al. [127], and by
Veynante and Poinsot [131], discussed in Section 4.1.1).

problem of evaluating the subgrid turbulent velocity us0 which was
used to model the effects of small-scale eddies on a premixed
ﬂame. The Smagorinsky model [377] widely invoked in LES leads to

Although the vast majority of premixed combustion models for
LES do not address the effects of the instantaneous ﬂame front on
turbulence, several LESs successfully predicted the ﬁelds of rms
turbulent velocity obtained from experiments. In particular;
 Weller at al. [382] developed a ﬂame-wrinkling LES combus-
tion model and well predicted the transverse proﬁles of
streamwise rms velocity (see Fig. 2b in the cited paper)
measured by Pitz and Daily [383] in a conﬁned lean (F ¼ 0.57)
propane–air ﬂame stabilized in a shear layer formed at a rear-
ward-facing step (u0/SL z 20).
 Fureby [384] used basically the same combustion model and
well predicted the transverse proﬁles of streamwise rms
velocity (see Fig.10 in the cited paper) measured by Sjunnesson
et al. [385] in conﬁned lean (F ¼ 0.57) propane–air ﬂames
stabilized behind a bluff body (u0/SL z 60, u0 z 10 m/s).
 Giacomazzi et al. [386] well predicted the same experimental
data (see Fig. 3 in the cited paper) using another subgrid scale
combustion model for LES.
 Chakravarthy and Menon [387,388] used the linear eddy model
(LEM), developed by Kerstein [42], for the LES of stoichiometric
methane–air turbulent ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets and
reported good agreement (see Fig. 5 in Ref. [387] or Fig. 10 in
Ref. [388]) between the computed rms velocities and the
experimental data of Cho et al. [223,224] and Cheng and
Shepherd [174].
 Pitsch and de Lageneste [389] applied a G-equation model to
the LES of a stoichiometric methane–air Bunsen ﬂame F3
stabilized by a large pilot ﬂame and well predicted (see Fig. 6 in
the

the

measured by Chen et al. [186] near the burner exit, but mark-
edly overpredicted the ATKE at a larger distance from the exit.
 Selle et al. [390] and Roux et al. [391] applied the thickened
ﬂame model, developed by Colin et al. [376], to the LES of two
industrial gas turbine burners and reported a good agreement
(see Figs. 24–25 in Ref. [390] and Figs. 21–23 in Ref. [391])
between the computed and measured rms velocities.
 Domingo et al. [295] developed a FSD-PDF subgrid scale closure
for the LES of premixed turbulent combustion and well pre-
dicted (see Fig. 22 in the cited paper) the transverse proﬁles of
rms velocities measured by Nguyen and Bruel [392] in a lean
(F ¼ 0.75) premixed ﬂame stabilized in a dump combustor
(u0 z 2 m/s).
 Duwig and Fuchs [393] applied a ‘‘ﬁltered ﬂamelet technique’’
to a gas turbine burner, which looked similar to a dump
combustor (see Fig. 2 in the cited paper), and reasonably well
predicted (see Figs. 5 and 6 therein) the measured transverse
proﬁles of u0 and v0 for a lean (F ¼ 0.4) preheated methane–air
mixture.
 Nogenmyr et al. [394] applied a G-equation approach to the LES
of a lean (F ¼ 0.62) methane–air ﬂame stabilized in a low-swirl
ﬂow and well-predicted (see Figs. 3 and 4 in the cited paper)
the measured rms velocities within the ﬂame (u0/SL z 5).
 Freitag and Janicka [395] applied a different G-equation
approach to the LES of a lean (F ¼ 0.833) methane–air ﬂame
stabilized in a strongly swirled ﬂow and well-predicted (see
Fig. 8 in the cited paper) the radial proﬁles of the ATKE
measured by Schneider et al. [239] (u0/SL z 3).
 Duwig and Fuchs [396] applied a marker ﬁeld approach [400]
to the LES of the aforementioned experiments by Pitz and
Daily [383]. The measured transverse proﬁles of streamwise
rms velocity (see Fig. 3 in the cited paper) were well predicted
in the vicinity of the rearward-facing step used to stabilize the
ﬂame, but were substantially overpredicted at far distances
(x/h ¼ 7, where h is the step height).
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
58


--- Page 59 ---

 Duwig [397] combined the aforementioned ﬁltered ﬂamelet
technique with the fractal ﬂame-wrinkling model20 developed
by Fureby [398], then applied this combined approach to the
LES of the aforementioned experiments by Nguyen and Bruel
[392], and reported reasonable agreement between measured
and computed u0, v0, and u0v0 in the vicinity of ﬂame-stabiliza-
tion zone. ‘‘Further downstream,. the LES over-estimates the
ﬂuctuation levels’’ [397].
Even if this list is far from a complete list of successive applica-
tions of LES to predicting rms velocities measured in premixed
turbulent ﬂames, it is encouraging. However, it also raises questions.
First, why have a number of substantially different combustion
models all been claimed to well predict experimental data? The
history of science has shown that there is one ‘‘true’’ model of
a physical process and a number of wrong models. If a number of
models have all been claimed to be ‘‘true’’, as in the case considered,
the sole conclusion we can draw is that many more tests must be
conducted to rank the aforementioned LES combustion models.
Indeed, most of them have been tested using only a single set of
experimental data.21
Second, why has the aforementioned LES well predicted rms
velocities in various premixed ﬂames? The rationale for this ques-
tion is as follows: The small-scale processes conﬁned to the
instantaneous ﬂame front were not resolved and the inﬂuence of
the front on turbulence was not modeled in these LES. However,
processes such as unburned–burned intermittency, dissipation
within the ﬂame front, the ﬂame-front contribution to the ﬂuctu-
ating velocity–pressure gradient correlation, etc. are well known to
substantially affect turbulence in ﬂames. This issue deﬁnitely needs
clarifying.
A tentative answer to the latter question could be as follows: The
majority of the above tests [295,382,384,386,389–391,396] were
performed using experimental data obtained from highly turbulent
ﬂames characterized by u0/SL ¼ O(10). Probably, the effects of the
instantaneous ﬂame front on ﬂow are of minor importance in such
a strong turbulence, and the LES that ignore these effects can well
predict experimental data. Further studies of this issue are deﬁ-
nitely necessary.
All in all, although LES is a very promising and rapidly devel-
oping numerical approach, which will probably dominate in the
foreseeable future, it has not yet matured into a tool capable of
predicting turbulence characteristics in premixed ﬂames associated
with the substantial inﬂuence of combustion on turbulence. The
successful applications of LES to various premixed turbulent ﬂames
are encouraging, but are only a ﬁrst step, rather than a ﬁnal one. It
seems too optimistic to believe that LES is a universal predictive
tool until subgrid models of the aforementioned unresolved, small-
scale phenomena are well elaborated.
To conclude this subsection, it is worth stressing that we have
not tried to present even a brief review of the LES of premixed
turbulent ﬂames. The purpose of this subsection was simply to
show that the physical modeling (i.e. discussing physical mecha-
nisms and analyzing unclosed terms) of the effects of premixed
combustion on turbulence is still beyond the mainstream LES.
4.3. Balance equation for probability density function
A transport equation for the joint mass density PDF rP(t, x, u, c)
of the ﬂow velocity u(t, x) and the combustion progress variable
c(t, x) is another alternative tool for modeling turbulent combus-
tion, as reviewed elsewhere [3, 4, 9, 20, 21]. When simulating the
effects of premixed ﬂames on turbulence, in particular an impor-
tant merit of this approach consists of the opportunity to
straightforwardly determine the third moments ru00
i u00
j u00
k, ru00
i u00
j c00
and the reaction term vii in Eq. (21) using rP(t, x, u, c) without
invoking additional model assumptions. Nevertheless, due to
difﬁculties in modeling unclosed terms and performing numerical
integration in multi-dimensional space (the PDF depends on eight
variables in a general unsteady 3D case and on ﬁve variables, u1, u2,
u3, c, and x, in the simplest statistically stationary, planar, 1D case),
there have been few attempts to study the discussed effects by
solving the transport equation for the joint PDF, and these studies
are restricted to a statistically planar 1D ﬂame.
Anand and Pope [320] simulated a statistically stationary, 1D,
planar ﬂame by using stochastic mixing and stochastic reor-
ientation models to close terms in the transport equation for the
joint mass density PDF that are associated with molecular viscosity
and ﬂuctuating pressure gradient. ‘‘The combined effect of the
stochastic models on the Reynolds stresses is equivalent to that of
Rotta’s return-to-isotropy model’’ [320], i.e. the two stochastic
models together yield a term similar to F11
(1) given by Eq. (114) in the
balance equation for the ATKE derived from the transport equation
for the joint PDF. Similarly, the two stochastic models together yield
the term C1cru00c00ð~3=~kÞ [cf. Eq. (125) with C2c ¼ 0] in the balance
equation for ru00c00. The simulations were performed using the
Monte Carlo method [20].
Anand and Pope [320] compared their numerical results with
the data measured by Moss [125] and computed early by Bray et al.
[105] and by Libby [19] using the BML model. All these approaches
have been claimed [320] to yield very similar distributions of ubð~cÞ
and uuð~cÞ in spite of several particular differences between the
models (e.g. the ﬂuctuating velocity–pressure gradient terms v and
IV

BML model overestimated ru02ð~cÞ in comparison with the model by
Anand and Pope [320].
Hulek and Lindstedt [350] applied the joint PDF transport
equation and the Monte Carlo method to study both transient and
fully developed, statistically 1D, planar premixed turbulent ﬂames.
The molecular viscosity and ﬂuctuating pressure gradient terms in
the transport equation were closed invoking two alternative
models; either the generalized Langevin model by Haworth and
Pope [351,352] or the realizable Lagrangian model (LSSG), i.e. the
SSG model of Speziale, Sarkar, and Gatski [354] modiﬁed by Pope
[353]. It is worth recalling that the redistribution terms in Eq. (137)
are associated with the former model.

data, see, e.g. Fig. 3 in Refs. [197,199,302,399]. It is also worth noting that experi-
mentally determined D is very sensitive to the method used to process ﬂame-front
images. For instance, Cintosun et al. [199] processed the same images using three
different methods (the caliper technique, the box-counting method, and Minkowski
circle technique) and obtained D¼2.2, 2.32 and 2.4, respectively. Although, the

0.4, respectively. Due to this large difference, the use of the fractal dimension as an
input parameter known from measurements offers ample opportunity for tuning.

LSSG models yielded very similar St. For the propane–air ﬂame, the
model well predicted the experimental data at u0/SL < 5, but
markedly overpredicted a ratio of St/SL at higher u0/SL (see Fig. 5 in
Ref. [350]). The latter disagreement was associated with local ﬂame
quenching by strong turbulence in the experiments.
From our viewpoint, the above test disproves, rather than vali-
dates the model. First, the speed of an expanding statistically
spherical turbulent ﬂame is substantially reduced due to the
curvature of the mean ﬂame brush as compared with a statistically
planar ﬂame, all other things being equal [368–370]. Under typical
conditions of the Leeds experiments, the magnitude of the effect
can be as large as 200–300% [369,370].
Second, the ﬂames investigated by Abdel-Gayed et al. [401,402]
were developing ﬂames, rather than fully developed ones. Hulek
and Lindstedt [350] have claimed that ‘‘a correction of the experi-
mental data to account for a fully developed turbulent ﬂame results
in a modest increase in the turbulent burning velocity’’ by referring
to an empirical parameterization of the development of St(t),
reported by Abdel-Gayed et al. [401]. However, such disregard for
ﬂame development does not seem to be fully consistent with the
computed data. On page 489, Hulek and Lindstedt [350] report that
the computed thickness of the methane–air ﬂame brush is about
ten times larger than the turbulence length scale. Even if we
assume that L ¼ 20 mm following Hulek and Lindstedt [350]
(Abdel-Gayed et al. [401, 402] reported that L z 40 mm), then, the
computed ﬂame brush is still larger than the radius (152.5 mm) of
the bomb used by Abdel-Gayed et al. [401, 402] and the maximum
possible radius of the ﬂame kernel (60 mm), i.e. the half distance
between two identical spark plugs that simultaneously ignited the
mixture in the measurements.
Hu˙ lek and Lindstedt [350] also simulated a transient, statisti-
cally planar, 1D, stoichiometric propane–air ﬂame and compared
the computed ﬁrst and second moments of the velocity ﬁeld with
the data measured by Videto and Santavicca [245] in front and
behind an expanding ﬂame. Note that the ﬁeld u(t, x) used in the
comparison was extracted from the numerical solution ‘‘after the
steady burning rate was achieved’’ [350] and the effect of ﬂame
development on the measured data was not addressed. The
computed values of ~uð~c/0Þ and ~uð~c/1Þ agreed well with the
mean velocities documented by Videto and Santavicca [245] in
reactants and products. For the second moments, the agreement
between the numerical and experimental results was also reason-
able, although the model underpredicted both ru00
i =rð~c/0Þ and
ru00
i =rð~c/1Þ, see Fig. 39.
Hu˙ lek and Lindstedt [350] also compared the computed distri-
butions of u1uð~cÞ and u2uð~cÞ with the experimental data by Moss
[125] and the numerical results by Anand and Pope [320]. The
measured and computed results agreed well, see Fig. 16 in
Ref. [350].
The above tests are deﬁnitely not sufﬁcient to draw conclusions
about the capabilities of the discussed PDF approach to predict
effects of combustion on turbulence. It is worth noting, however,
that the models discussed in this section do not address ﬂamelet
contribution to the ﬂuctuating pressure terms IV on the RHS of Eqs.
(22) and (23).

Although Eq. (210) is asymptotically correct in this limit case,
Eqs. (204) and (205), which result from Eqs. (198)–(201), raise
questions. In the cited papers by Huh et al. [281,404,405], we have
not found any explicit deﬁnition of the conditioned pressure
gradients. Only the above Eq. (201) was written and these deﬁni-
tions were claimed to hold ‘‘for any quantity’’ q (see Eqs. (3) and (4)
on p. 479 in Ref. [281]). However, if we apply Eq. (201) to deﬁne the

pressure gradient, cf. Eqs. (196) and (211).
It could be proposed considering Eqs. (199) and (200) as the
deﬁnitions or closures of the conditioned pressure gradients that
should be used instead of Eq. (211). However, Huh et al. [281]
clearly stated that Eqs. (199) and (200) were obtained from Eq.
(198), see the ﬁrst paragraph of Appendix A in the cited paper.
Unfortunately, the method used to obtain Eqs. (199) and (200) is
not disclosed therein.
Moreover, the last terms on the RHSs of Eqs. (199) and (200)
appear to be much larger than the other terms in these equations,
because the pressure gradient outside ﬂamelets within the turbu-
lent ﬂame brush is typically much less than p/L or p/dt.
All in all, the derivation of the conditioned Eqs. (189)–(192), as
published in Ref. [281], does not seem to be fully consistent.23

Second, the RHSs of Eqs. (223)–(226) were substituted into the

As proved elsewhere [407], the conditioned mass balance Eqs.
(215) and (216) are equivalent to the Favre-averaged continuity Eq.
(16) and the combustion progress variable balance Eq. (18), the sum
of Eqs. (217) and (218) yields the Favre-averaged Navier–Stokes Eq.
(17), whereas the sum of Eqs. (219) and (220) is not equal to Eq.
(22), by virtue of Eq. (49).
As compared with the second-order balance Eqs. (21) and (22),
the conditioned Eqs. (217)–(220) are less general, because their use
is only justiﬁed if the probability g is sufﬁciently low. Moreover, the
conditioned approach employs a larger number of balance equa-
tions. Furthermore, it involves unclosed terms that have not yet
been studied, e.g the conditioned pressure gradients ðVpÞu and
ðVpÞb in Eqs. (217) and (218), respectively.
Nevertheless, in some cases, the above disadvantages of the
conditioned approach may be outweighed by the form of the
unclosed terms on the RHSs of Eqs. (217)–(220). Indeed, the inﬂuence
of combustion on the conditioned characteristics of the velocity ﬁeld
is mainly associated with a few ﬂame-front terms that are propor-
tional to the probability g, whereas other terms are not straightfor-
wardly affected by chemical reactions and heat release in a ﬁrst
approximation. Therefore, the conditioned Eqs. (217)–(220) appear
to offer an opportunity to separate the modeling of phenomena
controlled
by
turbulence
from
the
modeling of
phenomena
controlled by combustion, in a ﬁrst approximation.
The resemblance of the ﬂame-front terms in different condi-
tioned equations may allow us to express these terms one through
another. For instance, if we consider a planar, 1D, stationary,
laminar ﬂame, then rD/Dt ¼ ruSLd/dz in the coordinate framework
attached to the ﬂame, with the spatial distance z being counted
along a line normal to the ﬂame. Accordingly, the ﬂame-front terms
may be evaluated as follows [407]

has been proposed to be used at low Karlovitz numbers [407]. This
simple approach allows us to reduce modeling the effects of pre-
mixed combustion on turbulence [i.e. closing the ﬂame-front terms
on the LHSs of Eqs. (230)–(232)] to substituting s > 0 into these
equations, and modeling the effects of turbulence on premixed
combustion (i.e. closing the mean mass rate W of product creation

If the Karlovitz number is not very small, but the probability
g is much less than unity, then the above simple approach may
be extended by assuming that the ratios of ½D=DtðuncÞf =½Dc=Dtf
and ½D=Dtðu2ncÞf =½Dc=Dtf can be parameterized as functions of
the ﬂame stretch rate _s by simulating stretched laminar ﬂames,
similar to the ﬂamelet library concept [25].
It is worth noting that the discussed approach yields a joint
closure for pressure, dissipation, and reaction terms within the
instantaneous ﬂame front. The idea of such a joint closure was put
forward by Kuznetsov [106] and was employed by Masuya [323]
when analyzing Eqs. (21) and (22) in the simple 1D case.
Eqs. (215)–(220) and (230)–(232) involve nine groups of
unclosed terms;
1. conditionally averaged viscous terms in Eqs. (217) and (218),
which may be neglected in the case of a high Reynolds
number,
2. conditioned pressure gradients26 in Eqs. (217) and (218),
3. triple correlations ðu0
iu0
ju0
kÞb and ðu0
iu0
ju0
kÞu in Eqs. (219) and
(220), respectively,
4. viscous dissipation terms in Eqs. (219) and (220),
5. pressure gradient terms in Eqs. (219) and (220),
6. the mean rate W of product creation in Eqs. (230)–(232),
7. terms ni and ninjdij in Eqs. (230)–(232), which characterize the
orientation of the ﬂame front,
8. terms ui;f ;u and ðuiujÞf ;u in Eqs. (231) and (232), which deal with
the ﬂow velocity conditionally averaged in the unburned
mixture at the ﬂame-front surface, and
9. cross-terms njui;f ;u in Eq. (232).
Certain aforementioned terms are not speciﬁc to the conditional
average approach and similar terms are addressed by various
models of the effects of turbulence on combustion. For instance;
A number of models reviewed elsewhere [12,43,45] place the focus
of consideration on the rate W. An averaged level set approach
[11,410] involves the conditioned velocity ui;f ;u. FSD models [12,45]
deal with ui;f ;u and ninj. The model by Cant et al. [411] involves
njui;f ;u. Since the effects of turbulence on combustion are beyond
the scope of the present paper, the above models are not discussed
here and interested readers are referred to the books and papers
cited above.
Since Eqs. (215)–(220) and (230)–(232) were obtained recently,
closure for many of the terms enumerated above has not yet been
elaborated and the approach has only been applied to the single
problem
of
countergradient
scalar
transport
in
statistically
stationary, 1D, premixed, turbulent ﬂames stabilized in impinging
jets. For this reason, the rest of this subsection will be restricted to
closures used to simulate these ﬂames [408] while certain more
general closures are brieﬂy discussed in Ref. [407].
Flames stabilized in impinging jets were simulated [408] by
restricting attention to the neighborhood of the jet axis, which is
the symmetry axis. Such a widely used method [324,412–416]
offers the opportunity to deal with statistically stationary, 1D,
ordinary differential balance equations. To further simplify the
problem, the viscous terms were neglected in Eqs. (217)–(218), the
balance Eqs. (219)–(220) for the conditioned Reynolds stresses
were omitted, and the spatial variations in the conditioned Rey-
nolds stresses ðu0
iu0
jÞu and ðu0
iu0
jÞb along the jet axis were disregarded
in Eqs. (217)–(218). The last assumption, also invoked by Biagioli
and Zimont [415], is justiﬁed by the fact that due to the intermit-
tency of unburned and burned mixture, the conditioned Reynolds
stresses are substantially lower than the mean ones [see Eq. (49)]
within impinging jet ﬂames (e.g. see Fig. 4 reported by Cho et al.
[224] or Fig. 5 reported by Li et al. [227]). The assumption is also
supported by the results of simulations by Lee and Huh [404] (see
Figs. 13 and 14 in the cited paper), and by the fact that the ﬁrst term
on the RHS of Eq. (233) below is much less than last two terms in all
the ﬂames studied. We must point out that the simpliﬁcation of
ðu02Þu ¼ ðu02Þb ¼ u02 ¼ const was used solely for simulating scalar
transport, but not for investigating ﬂame-generated turbulence.
Under the aforementioned simpliﬁcations, the scalar ﬂux is
modeled by the BML Eq. (47) and the following balance equation
[408].

were invoked due to their simplicity and the lack of adjustable

The mean mass rate W of product creation was computed

balance Eq. (18) for the Favre-averaged combustion progress vari-
able. Such a method is widely used [324,413,415,416] to model
ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets.

In all the ﬂames simulated in Ref. [408], these two terms, i.e. the

the three other terms on the RHS of this equation. Therefore, the
obtained results were weakly sensitive to variations in C2c and/or u0
in Eq. (240).
Figs. 42 and 43 summarize the results of testing the conditioned
balance Eqs. (215)–(220), closed using Eqs. (230), (231), (234), (235),
and
(240),
against
experimental
data
obtained
from
seven
impinging jet ﬂames by four research groups [174,224,227,228]. It is
worth stressing that no model constant or input parameter that was
directly relevant to the tested balance equations was tuned to
improve the agreement between the measured and computed data
shown in Figs. 42 and 43. Indeed, Eq. (234) did not involve any
constant or unknown input parameter. The constant 2/3 on the RHS
of Eq. (235) was set based on other experimental data obtained from
other ﬂames beforethe simulations.The computed results were only
weakly sensitive to variations in C2,c and u0 in Eq. (240). Therefore,
Figs. 42 and 43 allow us to claim that the performed tests indicate the
predictive capabilities of the conditioned balance equations.
Certainly, one single encouraging test is not sufﬁcient to consider
the conditioned balance Eqs. (215)–(220) to be a breakthrough. This
new approach deﬁnitely needs further study and applications to
various ﬂames. For instance, the above tests do not allow us to assess
the used closure of the difference in the conditioned pressure
gradients, Eqs. (239) or (240), because the counterpart term on the
RHS of Eq. (233) was small in the simulated ﬂames. Moreover, the
ability of the approach to predict the conditioned Reynolds stresses
in ﬂames has not yet been demonstrated. Nevertheless, the ﬁrst test
[408] appears to be sufﬁcient to consider the above conditioned
balance equations as a promisingapproach.
4.5. Simple models of turbulent scalar ﬂux
The models discussed in the above part of Section 4 consider
balance equations (either for second-order moments, or for the
joint PDF P(u, c), or for ﬁltered quantities, or for conditioned
velocities and Reynolds stresses). Discussed in this subsection are
two much simpler models proposed to evaluate scalar ﬂuxes in
premixed turbulent ﬂames.
4.5.1. Model by Chomiak and Nisbet [334]
Chomiak and Nisbet [334] have decomposed turbulent scalar
ﬂux as follows

(the term that involves ﬂuctuating density). Subsequently, they
have drawn an analogy between the pressure-driven scalar ﬂux in
ﬂames and the behavior of a gas bubble of a different density in an
accelerated ﬂow. Then, they have invoked the results of experi-
mental investigations of turbulent thermals in a constant gravita-
tional ﬁeld in order to model the pressure-driven ﬂux. Finally, they
have proposed using the following simple expression

Chomiak and Nisbet [334] incorporated Eq. (242) into a numer-

behind a bluff body. This model was based on the standard k–3
model, but involved several improvements, e.g. Eq. (95) with an
extra term r~3Vu=3 on the RHS or Eq. (242). The developed model
predicted the mean temperature and axial velocity ﬁelds better
than the k–3 model, but the role played by the second term on the
RHS of Eq. (242) in the simulated ﬂame was not discussed in the
cited paper.
Veynante and Poinsot [131] reported that their DNS yielded the
linear dependence of the buoyancy-induced scalar ﬂux on the
imposed constant acceleration and, thus, did not support Eq. (242),
which implied a square-root dependence. However, the DNS dealt
with low and moderate Reynolds number cases, whereas the model
considered disrupted ﬂames in highly turbulent ﬂows.

To analytically solve Eq. (244) supplemented with the integrated

terms proportional to ðu02Þb, and (ii) either
pu  pb ¼ 0
(245)
or
pu  pb ¼ sruS2
L:
(246)
Eq. (245) was used by Zimont and Biagioli [417] to simulate
experiments performed by Moss [125] and by Frank at al. [129]
with Bunsen ﬂames. Eqs. (244) and (245) were straightforwardly
applied to the former ﬂame, i.e. the difference ðub  uuÞpdt=Ut
normalized using the turbulent burning velocity Ut was analytically
determined to be a function of c and s (see Eqs. (24) and (25) in
Ref. [417]) and the normalized ﬂux ru00c00=Ut was evaluated by
neglecting the ﬁrst (gd) term on the RHS of Eq. (243). The obtained
dependence of ru00c00=Ut on ~c agreed well (see Fig. 3 in the cited
paper) with the experimental data by Moss [125] processed by Bray
et al. [105].
The Bunsen ﬂames investigated by Frank at al. [129] were ﬁrst
numerically simulated using a CFD code and a model of the effects
of turbulence on combustion, developed and validated by Zimont
and Lipatnikov [418–420] (this model has already been reviewed by
us [43]). Then, the computed 2D ﬁelds of ~c, ~u, and p were post-
processed by applying the above 1D model [i.e Eqs. (243)–(245)
supplemented with the integrated mass and momentum conser-
vation equation] along the direction normal to the mean ﬂame
brush. In this case, the ﬁrst (gd) term on the RHS of Eq. (243) was
kept and, therefore, the computed proﬁles of ru00c00 depended not
only on c and s, but also on turbulent diffusivity and the mean ﬂame
brush thickness. The reported proﬁles agreed reasonably well with
the experimental data (see Fig. 5 in Ref. [417]). The transition from
gradient to countergradient scalar transport with either increasing
the equivalence ratio for lean mixtures or decreasing u0 was well
predicted.
In a subsequent paper, Biagioli and Zimont [415] applied the
discussed model [with the gd term in Eq. (243) being neglected this
time] to simulate the experiments by Cheng and Shepherd [174]
and by Li et al. [227] who investigated premixed turbulent ﬂames
stabilized in impinging jets. For these ﬂames, Eq. (246) resulted in
a better agreement with the experimental data than Eq. (245), but
the situation was the opposite for the ﬂame studied by Moss [125].
Good agreement (see Fig. 44) between the computed and measured
axial proﬁles of either uu and ub (ﬂame s9 [174]) or jru00c00j (ﬂames
h4 and h6 investigated by Li et al. [227]) was reported. However, to
obtain the agreement for ﬂame s9, the mean jet exit velocity U was
reduced from 5 m/s to 3.3 m/s. Moreover, to obtain a solution for
ﬂame h4, the difference in pu and pb calculated using Eq. (246) was
decreased by a factor of two. Such reduction of U or pu  pb casts
doubt on predictive capabilities of the discussed approach, espe-
cially as its implementation details were different when simulating
the ﬂame by Moss [125], the ﬂames by Frank et al. [129], and the
ﬂames stabilized in impinging jets [174,227].
Biagioli [416] further extended the discussed model to allow for
buoyancy and the scalar transport in the direction tangential to the
ﬂame, and tested the extended model by simulating the experiments
by Kalt et al. [132] with premixed turbulent ﬂames stabilized in
impinging jets. Only ﬁve of 11 experimentally studied ﬂames were
simulated. The sum of uu þ ub and the sign of the difference in ub and
uu were well predicted, whereas the magnitude of this difference
was not accurate. Note that the proﬁles of ubðcÞ computed using the
original model by Biagioli and Zimont [415] [with Eq. (245)] and the
extended model [with Eq. (246)] differed substantially from one
another. The inﬂuence of the model extension onprevious tests [415]
of the approach was not discussed by Biagioli [416].
It is worth noting that by combining Eq. (43) with q ¼ p and Eq.
(246), we can easily obtain

the aforementioned ﬂames s9 [174], h4 and h6 [227] in Ref. [408]. In
ﬂame h6, the computed LHS of Eq. (248) was almost constant. In
ﬂame h4, it increased by more than 30% from the leading to the
trailing edge of the ﬂame brush. In ﬂame s9, variations in the LHS of

not conﬁrm the universality of Eq. (248), which results from the
model by Zimont and Biagioli [415,417]. The above variations in the
LHS of Eq. (248) are consistent with the fact that Biagioli and
Zimont [415] were forced to change the mean exit velocity U or the
RHS of Eq. (246) when simulating the ﬂames s9 or h4, respectively,
in order for the computed results to agree with the experimental
data obtained from these two ﬂames.
Furthermore, the DNS data reported by Lee et al. [405] indicate
that the difference in pu and pb markedly increases from the
leading to the trailing edge of a statistically planar, 1D, premixed
turbulent ﬂame (see Fig. 4 in the cited paper). These data does not
support Eq. (245) or Eq. (246).
4.6. Summary
Modeling the effects of a premixed ﬂame on turbulence is still
an unresolved issue.
Most approaches to resolving this problem deal with the
balance Eqs. (21) and (22) for the second-order moments ru00
i c00 and
ru00
i u00
j . Although a substantial progress in understanding the
behavior of different terms in these equations and the role played
by them has been made, and a number of advanced models have
been proposed to close these terms; a well-substantiated and
widely-recognized model has not yet been elaborated. There are
a number of competitive models with the validation of each
particular model being limited to a few particular cases, different
for different models.
A recently developed approach dealing with conditioned
balance equations [407,408] appears to be promising but also
strongly needs further validation.
An interesting simple approach has been developed by Zimont
and Biagioli [415–417] to simulate scalar transport in premixed
turbulent ﬂames. Although the validation of this approach appears
to be impressive, it should not be overestimated as discussed in
Section 4.5.2 and further tests appear to be necessary.
5. Some basic considerations
Experimental and DNS data reviewed in Section 3 and various
models discussed in Section 4 show that the inﬂuence of premixed
combustion on a turbulent ﬂow is very complex and is not yet well
understood. Nevertheless, discussion of certain basic issues rele-
vant to the effects of a ﬂame on turbulence and turbulent transport
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
68


--- Page 69 ---

is sometimes oversimpliﬁed in the literature. Such simpliﬁcations
may be good for teaching, but not always for research. The goal of
this section is to critically discuss certain widely accepted concepts
and to emphasize alternative standpoints.
5.1. Transition from gradient to countergradient transport
Experimental and DNS data reviewed in Section 3 show that the
directions of the scalar ﬂux and the mean scalar gradient may be
the same in one premixed turbulent ﬂame, but may be the opposite
in another. Accordingly, the following issue is often discussed in the
literature. Is there a universal criterion for determining the direc-
tion of the scalar ﬂux? Under what conditions is the ﬂux gradient
(countergradient)?
5.1.1. Bray number
This issue was ﬁrst addressed by Bray et al. [33,127,128] who
have proposed using a criterion given by Eq. (60). To obtain the
criterion, ﬁrst, the ﬂow velocity ui;f conditioned on the instanta-
neous ﬂame surface has been modeled as follows [127,128]
ui;f ¼ ð1  KÞui;u þ Kui;b
(249)
by referring to an unpublished paper by Bidaux and Bray (1994).
Here, 0 < K < 1 is a constant. Second, using Eqs. (46), (47), and
(249), the difference in ui;f and ~ui is determined

turbulence level, this motion is mainly controlled by turbulent
diffusion and the following simple closure

brush with respect to the mean ﬂow to be of the order of the rms
turbulent velocity.27
The function F has been introduced into Eq. (251) by referring
(see p. 280–281 in Ref. [128]) to numerical data that indicate that
small-scale eddies contribute weakly to the local ﬂame wrinkling
and motion, because they are rapidly dissipated due to the viscosity
jump at the instantaneous ﬂame front [421,422]. We may note,
however, that this argument does not seem to be cogent, because
turbulent diffusion is commonly considered to be controlled by
large-scale turbulent eddies [112,423,424] and the enhanced
dissipation of small-scale eddies is unlikely to affect turbulent
diffusion at high Reynolds numbers [if u0[SL in Eq. (251), then
Ret ¼ u0L=nu[SLL=nuwL=dL[1]. Therefore, the use of the func-

tuning, rather than well-substantiated modeling. DNS data plotted
in Fig. 15 in Ref. [128] show that F was increased from 0.3 to 1.0
when L/dL was increased from 4 to 40.
At a low turbulence level, both the relative motion of the
instantaneous ﬂame surface with respect to the mean ﬂow and the
difference in ub and uu appear to be weakly affected by turbulence
and to be mainly controlled by the laminar ﬂame speed and the
density ratio. For a statistically planar 1D ﬂame that propagates
from the right to the left, Veynante et al. [128] have assumed that

Veynante et al. [128] tested Eqs. (250) and (253) with K ¼ 0.5

the cited paper) and the DNS databases (see Fig.16 therein) obtained

the reported cases and Eq. (250) also worked reasonably well.
Eq. (250) agrees reasonably well with the recent DNS data by
Chakraborty and Cant [289] (see Fig. 5 in the cited paper), but
constant K has been tuned to achieve the agreement. The tuned
values of K are scattered between 0.55 and 0.7 for various Lewis
numbers with K ¼ 0.7 for Le ¼ 1.

natural gas-air and four propane–air Bunsen ﬂames. The ratio of sSL/
u0 was changed either by varying the equivalence ratio or ‘‘by altering
the upstream position of the turbulence generating grid’’ [129]. In the
ﬂames characterized bya high (low) sSL/u0, the difference in ub and uu
was positive (negative), thus, implying countergradient (gradient)
scalar transport, see Eq. (47). In two ﬂames C (natural gas-air [129]
and propane–air [130] with F ¼ 0.7 in both cases) characterized by
sSL z u0, the velocities conditioned on unburned and burned
mixtures were approximately equal to one another, thus indicating
a transition from gradient to countergradient scalar transport. If we
assume that NB z 1 for the two ﬂames C, then F ¼ 0:5 in Eq. (60), in
line with the DNS database by Veynante et al. [128].
Kalt and Bilger [425] processed the aforementioned experi-

Unfortunately, the method used to obtain Eqs. (256) and (257)

obtained by utilizing the method of Kalt and Bilger [425] [i.e. by
processing the experimental data on ub  uu in the range of

then, Eqs. (256) and (257), in fact, parameterize the function F on
the LHS of Eq. (254). As concerns a criterion of transition from
gradient to countergradient scalar transport, Eq. (256) does not
seem to be better than the Bray number criterion given by Eq. (60),
as the latter equation is also supported by the experimental data by
Frank et al. [129,130] and by Chen and Bilger [196] provided that
Fz0:5.
Recently, Pfadler et al. [201,240] documented countergradient
scalar ﬂux in six
swirl-stabilized V-shaped and 15 Bunsen
methane–air ﬂames. For the two leanest among the V-shaped
ﬂames (F ¼ 0.71 and 0.67), characterized by the lowest ratio of sSL/
u0 z 0.8 and 0.5, respectively, the Bray number criterion yields
countergradient ﬂux if F is less than 0.4 (F ¼ 0.71) or even 0.25
(F ¼ 0.67). For the four other V-shaped and for all the Bunsen
ﬂames, NB calculated using Eq. (60) with Fz0:5 was larger than
unity, in line with the Bray number criterion. Pfadler et al. [201] also
tested Eqs. (250) and (253) and found that they overestimated an
increase in the maximum value of ru00c00 with the equivalence ratio.
Since 2u0 was substantially less than sSL in most investigated
ﬂames, the overestimation was weakly affected by variations in
F ¼ Oð1Þ.
Thus, the Bray number criterion works well in the 29 Bunsen
ﬂames studied by Frank et al. [129,130], Chen and Bilger [196],
and Pfadler et al. [201], while Eqs. (250) and (253) used to obtain
this criterion show poorer agreement with the same experimental
data.
Recently, Troiani et al. [246] investigated four (F ¼ 0.67, 0.8, 1.03,
and 1.12) open methane–air ﬂames stabilized behind a bluff body.
They evaluated F by ﬁtting each measured dependence of ru00c00 on
~c with a parabola, see Eq. (47), then determining the average (over
the ﬂames) values of ub  uu from these ﬁts and Eq. (47), and ﬁnally
using Eq. (254). The obtained values of F were increased from 0.5 to
1.3 when DL was decreased due to an increase in the equivalence

5.1.2. Pressure gradient
The Bray number concept highlights the ratio of sSL/u0 as the
major factor that controls the direction of turbulent scalar ﬂux.
However, there are other important factors. For instance, Veynante
and Poinsot [131] have pointed out that externally imposed accel-
eration g and/or a pressure gradient can substantially affect not
only the magnitude but also the direction of turbulent scalar ﬂux.
To model such effects, they have proposed adding the buoyancy slip

by Borghi and Dutoya [103].

mean pressure gradient. In the following discussion, we shall omit

are developing ones and are characterized by a growing mean
ﬂame brush thickness, as discussed in detail elsewhere [43,307]
and in Section 5.1.4.

approach is based on integration of the scalar ﬂuctuation Eq. (20) in

Eq. (275) implies that the scalar ﬂux direction is controlled by
the sign of the integral IB, which is affected by the dependence of
the dilatation Vu on the combustion progress variable within the
instantaneous ﬂame front. Based on these theoretical results,
Swaminathan et al. [267] have stressed that ‘‘the scalar ﬂux, which
would be expected to be related to large-scale features of the
turbulence, is in fact related to the much thinner structure of the
instantaneous ﬂame front.’’
To support these claims, Swaminathan et al. [267] processed the
DNS databases by Trouve´ and Poinsot [265] and by Zhang and
Rutland [271]. The numerical results shown in Figs. 1, 5–7, 9, and 10
in Ref. [267] indicate that ﬁrst, the integral IB is mainly positive (see
Fig. 9a in the cited paper) and the ﬂux ru00c00 is countergradient (see
Fig. 1a there) in the ﬂamelet regime of turbulent combustion,
simulated by Zhang and Rutland [271].
Second, in the thickened ﬂame regime studied by Trouve´ and
Poinsot [265], the integral IB is negative at t/st ¼ 1.4, increases with
time, and reverses the sign at sufﬁciently large t/st (see Fig. 9 in the
cited paper).
Third, the negative values of IB are accompanied by a substantial
increase in the conditioned diffusion CDjCD (see Fig. 6a there) and in
the conditioned dilatation <VujC> (see Fig. 7a there) at low C,
while variations in the conditioned reaction rate <WjC> are very
weak (see Fig. 5a therein) in the case of unity Lewis number. The
increase in CDjCD is attributed to the strong production of spatial
gradients by turbulence [267].
Fourth, the increase in IB with time is accompanied by
a reduction in the conditioned diffusion and dilatation to the
laminar ﬂamelet levels, with this reduction being attributed [267]
to the decay of the turbulence in the DNS by Trouve´ and Poinsot
[265].
Finally, despite the positive values of the integral IB, calculated at
large t/st using the database of Trouve´ and Poinsot [265], the ﬂux
ru00c00 is gradient in the DNS. Swaminathan et al. [267] have
considered this fact to indicate that the unsteady term on the LHS of
Eq. (275) played a signiﬁcant role. This hypothesis is further sup-
ported by calculating the dependence of the integral R N
N rc002dx on
time (see Fig. 11 in the cited paper).
Although the analysis of Swaminathan et al. [267] appears to be
cogent on the face of it, certain issues are worth discussing further.
In particular, the sign of the integral IB depends not only on the
inner structure of the instantaneous ﬂame front via the conditioned
dilatation <VujC>(C), but also on the structure of the mean ﬂame

Furthermore, it is worth noting that expressions commonly
invoked to model the dependence of the mean rate of product
creation on the combustion progress variable yield countergradient
scalar ﬂux in the statistically stationary, planar,1D case (see Fig. 45).
Accordingly, the study of Swaminathan et al. [267] shows that
turbulent ﬂame development promotes gradient scalar transport
[in line with the model used to introduce the Bray number, see Eq.
(251)], and the instantaneous ﬂame-front structure may affect the
ﬂux direction, e.g. the perturbation of the front structure by
turbulent eddies may also cause gradient scalar ﬂux. The latter
claim is based on Eqs. (275), (277) and on the aforementioned
numerical results, in particular, on (i) the strong increase in the
conditioned dilatation <VujC> at low C in the turbulent as
compared to the laminar ﬂame (see Fig. 7a in Ref. [267]), (ii) the
reduction of this effect with time, and (iii) the transition from
negative to positive IB with time. However, the dependencies of
IBð~cÞ shown in Fig. 9 in Ref. [267] to emphasize this transition seem
contradictory. The point is that the integral IB appears to be positive
as ~c/0 by virtue of its deﬁnition given by Eq. (277), whereas solid
curves in the aforementioned Fig. 9 show that IB < 0 at low ~c.
Finally, Eqs. (272), (280), and (281) do not involve the mean
pressure gradient. This fact does not mean that an external pressure
gradient does not affect the ﬂux ru00c00. This fact means that the
dependencies of ~cðxÞ or/and Wð~cÞ should be sensitive to the pres-
sure gradient. For instance, the DNS data reported by Veynante and
Poinsot [131] imply the dependence of the turbulent burning
velocity (and, hence, W) on the pressure gradient (see Fig. 8 in the
cited paper31).
5.1.4. Flame development
Numerous experimental data already reviewed by us [43,307]
indicate that most industrial and laboratory premixed turbulent
ﬂames, including statistically stationary Bunsen and V-shaped
ones, are developing ﬂames. The most evident manifestation of
ﬂame development consists of the growth of the mean ﬂame brush
thickness dt. The thickness of an expanding premixed turbulent
ﬂame grows with time, while the thickness of a premixed turbulent
ﬂame stabilized in a statistically stationary ﬂow increases with
distance Z counted from the ﬂame stabilization area along a mean
ﬂow line. The increase in dt is associated with ﬂame development,
because according to the Taylor hypothesis, the dependence of
a quantity q, which characterizes the perturbation of a statistically
stationary turbulent ﬂow, on distance Z from the source of this
perturbation (e.g. an admixture source) is mainly controlled by the
development of the perturbation convected by the mean ﬂow
[423].
As discussed in detail elsewhere [43,307], the growth of the
mean ﬂame brush thickness is well described by the Taylor theory
of turbulent diffusion [78,79,423,424], i.e. the thickness determined
using the maximum gradient method

The inﬂuence of ﬂame development on the direction of
turbulent scalar ﬂux was ﬁrst documented in the DNS by Veynante
et al. [128]. In particular, Fig. 46 shows that the scalar ﬂux is
gradient during an early stage of ﬂame development, but reverses
direction at a later stage, with the countergradient transport
observed earlier in ﬂames characterized by a lower u00/SL. The
normalized

tu00/L0
associated
with
the
appearance
of the countergradient transport is about 0.7 for u00/SL ¼ 2, and is in
the ranges of 1.3–1.7 and 1.6–2.2 for u00/SL ¼ 3 and u00/SL ¼ 5,
respectively. An increase in the Bray number due to the decay of
turbulence cannot explain these results. For example, for the ﬂame
characterized by u00/SL ¼ 5, the scalar transport shows the coun-
tergradient behavior at tu00/L0  2.2, but the ratio of u0/SL is larger
than 3 at this instant (see Fig. 11 in the cited paper). To the
contrary, the transport was initially gradient for the ﬂame char-
acterized by u00/SL ¼ 3.
The recent DNSs by Chakraborty and Cant [291] have also
indicated the transition from GD to CGD as ﬂame develops. For
Le  0.6, Table 4 from the cited paper shows GD at t ¼ L0/u00, but
CGD at t  2L0/u00. For Le  0.8, the transport shows gradient
behavior at t  3L0/u00, but the magnitude of the scalar ﬂux
decreases with time, thus, implying the transition from GD to CGD
at a later instant.
The inﬂuence of ﬂame development on the direction of turbu-
lent scalar ﬂux was mentioned explicitly for the ﬁrst time by
Swaminathan et al. [267], as already discussed in Section 5.1.3, and
was highlighted later by Zimont et al. [133,417] and by the present
authors [134]. Zimont et al. [133,417] have pointed out that the ﬁrst
(diffusion) term on the RHS of Eq. (243) is large during the early
stage of ﬂame development and decreases with time due to the
growth of the mean ﬂame brush thickness. Accordingly, scalar ﬂux
should always be gradient just after ignition (or in the vicinity of
the ﬂame-stabilization zone in a statistically stationary ﬂow) fol-
lowed by transition to countergradient ﬂux at a certain instant
(distance Z from the zone), which may depend on sSL/u0, for
instance.
The above argument implies that a ratio of dt=Dt/0 as t/0 (or
Z/0). However, Eqs. (283) and (284) yield Dt/dt ¼ u0 during the
early stage ðt  sLÞ of ﬂame development characterized by the
linear growth of the mean ﬂame brush thickness with time (or Z).
Many experiments are restricted to exactly this stage of ﬂame
development. For instance, the linear dependence of dt on Z has
been
documented
in
Bunsen
[431–433]
and
V-shaped
[209,434,435] ﬂames, along with the linear dependence of dt on t
in expanding ﬂames [436,437].

Thus, the hypothesis put forward by Zimont [133,417] that scalar
ﬂux is always gradient during an initial stage of turbulent ﬂame
development does not seem to be well justiﬁed by the argument he
provided. Certainly, another hypothesis he proposes [namely,
transition from gradient to countergradient transport may occur
due to a decrease in the ﬁrst (diffusion) term on the RHS of Eq. (243)
during ﬂame development] appears to be cogent if t > sL.
The present authors [134] have emphasized the inﬂuence of
ﬂame development on the direction of scalar ﬂux using another
method. We have noted that during the transition from gradient to
countergradient (or vice versa) scalar transport, many terms in Eq.
(21) vanish, because ru00c00/0. For instance, terms iii and iv vanish
if they are closed using Eq. (102). Term v either vanishes if it is
modeled invoking Eq. (125) or is similar to term vi if Eq. (127) is
used. Terms iii–v closed jointly by Eq. (138) reduce to a term similar
to term vi. Term vii vanishes if either Eq. (168) or Eq. (169) is used.
Based on this argument, the present authors [134] hypothesize
that, in the vicinity of the transition point, the direction of the scalar
ﬂux should mainly be controlled by the balance of terms i and vi,
with the latter term being multiplied by a constant in order to
account for the non-vanishing part of term v.
For a statistically planar, 1D, self-similarly developing premixed
turbulent ﬂame, the two dominating (at ru00c00/0) terms may be
evaluated using the following method. First, the velocity ~uðt; xÞ is
analytically determined from the mass conservation Eq. (16) with
the mean density being calculated from Eqs. (44) and (286). Second,
the mean pressure gradient is estimated from the Favre-averaged
Navier–Stokes Eq. (17) without viscous and turbulent transport
terms. Third, the BML Eq. (45) is used. Fourth, the partial derivative
v~c=vx is calculated from Eqs. (44) and (286). Fifth, the rms velocity
u0 ¼ ru002=r is assumed to be independent of t and x. Finally, the
following model [43]

increase in sSL/u0, in line with the experimental [129,130,132] and

to yield the effect of the turbulence length scale on the transition
[134] that agrees with the DNS data by Veynante et al. [128].

modeled. The simplest solution consists of invoking Eqs. (288)
and (283), respectively, as a ﬁrst approximation. However, it is

scalar transport slows down at least the growth of dt and, prob-
ably, the growth of Ut, as discussed elsewhere [429,430,438,439].

tative errors if applied to ﬂames associated with the transition
from gradient to countergradient transport. Nevertheless, for
a qualitative analysis, the use of the two equations appears to be
reasonable.

By invoking Eqs. (283) and (288) and varying the normalized
time w ¼ t/st, we can easily show that the LHS of Eq. (294) is well
approximated by 3 w–0.6 if 0.2  w  2 [427]. Therefore, the LHS of
Eq. (294) is much higher than the RHS at t  st and the ﬂux ru00c00 is
initially gradient followed by the transition to the countergradient
transport at the certain instant ttr.
If the laminar ﬂame speed is increased, then the ratio of Ut,N/u0
is also increased and the transition time is decreased. If t is kept
constant, however, we can observe gradient (countergradient)
transport for mixtures characterized by a lower (higher) SL, in line
with the experimental data by Frank, Kalt et al. [129,130,132].
If u0 is increased, then the ratio of Ut,N/u0 is decreased, because
turbulent burning velocity scales as u0q with q < 1 as discussed in
detail elsewhere [43]. Therefore, the normalized transition time is
increased in line with the DNS results shown in Fig. 46.
If u0 is increased and t is kept constant (such a case is associated
with ﬂames B and C investigated by Frank et al. [129] or with ﬂames
B and C studied by Kalt et al. [130]), then, the normalized time
w ¼ tu0/L is increased and the LHS of Eq. (294) is decreased as w–0.6.
Accordingly, the ratio of u0/Ut,N should substantially be increased
by u0 (much stronger than u00.6) in order for Eqs. (283), (288), and
(294) to yield the transition from CGD to GD with increasing u0, as
observed by Frank, Kalt, and Bilger [129,130]. If u0/Ut,N f Da–1/4, see
Eq. (288), the above very simple 1D model cannot yield the afore-
mentioned transition. Probably, the 2D effects emphasized by Chen
et al. [428] play a role in the discussed ﬂames.
Since Eq. (294) does not involve the mean pressure gradient
and/or acceleration, but the direction of turbulent scalar ﬂux may
depend on an externally imposed pressure gradient and accelera-
tion [131], we have to assume that Ut,N, and/or X, and/or dt/L are
sensitive to the pressure gradient. For instance, in the DNS by
Veynante and Poinsot [131], both Ut and dDt/dw were decreased by
an acceleration that was favorable for countergradient transport
(see Figs. 8 and 9 in the cited paper).

One more theoretical argument that emphasizes the inﬂuence
of ﬂame development on the direction of scalar ﬂux will be dis-
cussed in the next subsection.
Transition from gradient to countergradient scalar ﬂux with
distance from the ﬂame-stabilization zone was ﬁrst experimentally
documented by Veynante et al. [135], but the ﬂux measured by
them was almost tangential to the mean ﬂame brush. Most et al.
[220] also reported the reversal of the direction of tangential scalar
ﬂux with distance, but they observed countergradient (gradient)
transport in the vicinity of (at far distance from) the ﬂame-stabi-
lization zone, contrary to the above discussion.
Recently, Pfadler et al. [240] documented the transition from
gradient to countergradient scalar ﬂux with distance from the
ﬂame-stabilization zone (see Fig. 48), but only for the axial ﬂux,
which was almost tangential to the mean ﬂame brush, whereas the
radial ﬂux was countergradient everywhere it was measured (at
heights more than 10 mm above the burner exit).
5.1.5. Countergradient scalar transport and growth of ﬂame
brush thickness
As discussed above, scalar ﬂuxes in many premixed turbulent
ﬂames are countergradient, but the mean thickness of many
premixed turbulent ﬂames grows either with time or with distance
from

both

(see Fig. 9 in the cited paper). Therefore, the following issue should
be addressed: Can Eq. (18) yield the growth of dt if the turbulent
ﬂux ru00

introduced in order to allow for basic phenomena that control the

the scales sf and V depended neither on t nor on x, and the solution
had to be self-similar, i.e. ~c had to be dependent on the normalized
distance x determined by Eq. (285), rather than on the two inde-
pendent variables t and x. Then, Eq. (286) is the exact analytical
solution to the problem, and Eq. (299) reduces to Eq. (296) with

The development of various terms in Eq. (300), evaluated using
Eqs. (284), (288), and (301) is shown in Fig. 49. The normalized
generalized diffusivity D=Dt;N (see curve 4) rapidly grows during
the early stage of ﬂame development, the growth being controlled
by the turbulent diffusivity dt(t) (curve 1). As the ﬂame further
develops, the magnitude of the countergradient term (curves 2) on
the RHS of Eq. (300) markedly increases, whereas dt(t) grows very
slowly at t > 2st. Consequently, the generalized diffusivity D reaches
a maximum value at t z 2st, followed by a slow decrease in DðtÞ
caused by the countergradient term. If constants b1 and b2 are of the
same order (see ﬁne lines), the reaction term (curves 3) counteracts
the countergradient term and reduces the rate of the decrease in D
with time. As b1/  N (see bold lines with symbols), this reduction
effect vanishes, because the constant a in Eq. (300) tends to zero. As
a result, the decrease in D with time is most pronounced as
b1/  N, but the total scalar ﬂux is always gradient in this case at
ﬁnite t.
For ﬁnite values of b1, the transition from gradient to counter-
gradient transport occurs as the ﬂame develops. If b2 ¼1.5, b1 ¼ 3,
and Ut,N ¼ u0, as in Fig. 49, the transition time evaluated using Eq.
(302) is close to 15st (cf. ﬁne line 2 and line 1 in Fig. 49). The
simulations have shown that ttr decreases when a ratio of Ut,N/u0
increases [439], e.g. due to an increase in SL or a decrease in u0.
Even if the total scalar ﬂux is countergradient, the generalized
diffusivity D determined by Eq. (300) is positive due to the reaction
