term on the RHS of this equation. Therefore, the mean ﬂame brush
thickness may grow even if the scalar transport is countergradient.
The above discussion of Eq. (300) sounds similar to the discus-
sion of Eq. (297). However, there is an important difference. In the
latter equation, Dt is the turbulent diffusivity. Therefore, the growth
of the mean ﬂame brush thickness (i) is controlled by the turbulent
diffusion, (ii) may be modeled by Eq. (283), but (iii) is not affected
by combustion, because the transport term on the LHS is fully
balanced by a part of the reaction term. To the contrary, Eq. (300)
may yield a decrease in D with time (D/0 as t/N), and the effect
of the ﬂame-induced countergradient transport on ﬂame thickness
growth is reduced but not fully balanced by the reaction term.
Accordingly, Eq. (283) provides an upper estimate of the mean
ﬂame brush thickness which may be lower due to the aforemen-
tioned effect. The magnitude of this reduction effect depends on
constants b1 and b2, which, however, are not determined within the
framework
of
the
discussed
approach,
which
involves
four
unspeciﬁed functions and two unspeciﬁed scales.
5.1.6. Geometrical effects
The transition from gradient to countergradient transport is also
sensitive to ﬂame geometry. For instance, Chen and Bilger [196]
have shown that 2D effects may play an important role in the
Bunsen ﬂames investigated by them and by Frank et al. [129,130].
Moreover, the mean pressure gradients in different kinds of
premixed turbulent ﬂames may be substantially different. For
instance, Fig. 50 shows the normalized mean pressure difference
½pðxÞ  pð0Þ=ðruU2
t Þ calculated using Eq. (286), the Favre-averaged
mass conservation Eq. (16), and the Favre-averaged Navier–Stokes
Eq. (17) without viscous and Reynolds stress terms. For ﬂames
stabilized in impinging jets (curves 1–3), the pressure difference
was calculated at three different ﬂame positions, xf/d ¼ 0.4, 0.3, and
0.2, where d was the distance from the jet exit to the wall. For
expanding spherical ﬂames (curves 4–6), the pressure difference
was evaluated at three different normalized ﬂame thicknesses, dt/
rf ¼ 0.4, 0.2, and 0.1, where rf was the mean ﬂame radius. For
simplicity, the growth of the mean ﬂame brush thickness was
neglected in these simulations.
The obtained results show that although the mean pressure
gradients are approximately equal in the middle ðjxj  1Þ of all
these ﬂames, including a statistically planar 1D ﬂame (see curve 7),
this is not so in the leading (x > 0) zone. For instance, strong
negative pressure gradients that cause gradient transport are
observed in the leading zone of the spherical ﬂames characterized
by dt/rf ¼ 0.4 and 0.2 (curves 4 and 5, respectively), whereas the
gradient is weak and positive in the planar ﬂame (curve 7).
Therefore, scalar transport may be gradient in an expanding
spherical ﬂame, but countergradient in an expanding planar ﬂame,
all other things being equal. It is worth noting that the above ratio
of dt/rf ¼ 0.4 is quite typical for the expanding premixed turbulent
ﬂames studied in combustion bombs [440].
5.1.7. Summary
All in all, the above discussion shows that the transition from
gradient to countergradient scalar transport in premixed turbulent
ﬂames is a complex phenomenon, which involves various physical
processes and is affected by various factors. These include (i) a ratio
of sSL/u0 (the Bray number [128]), which is mainly associated with
the effects of the ﬂame front on turbulent transport, (ii) the mean
pressure gradient induced by the ﬂame itself, (iii) an externally
imposed acceleration and/or pressure gradient [131], (iv) ﬂame
development [133,134], (v) the internal structure of the instanta-
neous ﬂame front [267], and (vi) ﬂame geometry. A comprehensive
theory of this phenomenon has not yet been elaborated and the
validity of any available criterion of the transition is limited to
certain simple conditions.
5.2. Do high values of measured ﬂame speeds prove the generation
of turbulence by combustion?
As discussed in Section 2.2.1, an observation that stimulated the
development of the concept of ﬂame-generated turbulence by
Scurlock [53] and by Karlovitz et al. [55] consisted of high (mark-
edly larger than unity) ratios of turbulent ﬂame speed St to the rms
velocity u0, obtained from certain laboratory ﬂames characterized
by u0 > SL. The high ratio of St/(u0 þ SL) is still often considered to be
an indirect support of the aforementioned concept. However, since
there is an alternative and better elaborated explanation of this
effect, the high ratio of St/(u0 þ SL) should not be recognized as an
evidence of ﬂame-generated turbulence.
A method for determining turbulent ﬂame speed appears to be
a clue to explaining the high measured values of St. Contrary to the
laminar case the correct deﬁnition of ﬂame speed is intricate for
turbulent ﬂows. Since this issue has already been discussed in our
previous review paper [43] and in a couple of papers [370,441–444]
of a narrower scope, the present section is mainly restricted to
Fig. 49. Normalized generalized diffusivity D=Dt;N vs. normalized time t/st. Curves 1, 2, and 3 show the three terms in the square brackets on the RHS of Eq. (300), respectively.
Curves 4 show the sum of these three terms. Fine lines and bold lines with symbols have been calculated using Eqs. (284), (288), and (301) with b1 ¼ 2b2 and b1/  N,
respectively. Ut,N ¼ u0, b2 ¼ 1.5.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
80


--- Page 81 ---

emphasizing the relationship between this issue and the high ratios
of St/(u0 þ SL).
Typically,
a
turbulent
ﬂame
speed
St
(sometimes
called
displacement speed Sd) characterizes the speed of some reference
surface in the mean ﬂame brush with respect to the unburned
mixture, whereas a turbulent burning velocity Ut (sometimes called
consumption velocity Uc or consumption speed Sc) characterizes
the mass rate of reactant consumption per unit area of this or
another reference surface. Although the above formulation cannot
be considered to be a rigorous deﬁnition, as the reference surface is
not speciﬁed, we shall utilize it as a ‘‘deﬁnition,’’ because it has been
used in numerous research contributions that deal with turbulent
ﬂame speed behavior. The main problem impeding a more rigorous
deﬁnition is that the mean turbulent ﬂame brush thickness is wide
and growing (see Section 5.1.4). A surface characterized by some
arbitrarily chosen32 reference value for temperature or progress
variable is usually used to identify the ﬂame position. Unfortu-
nately, ﬂame speed may strongly depend on the choice of the
reference surface. This dependence was experimentally proven by
Gouldin [445], who measured the convective ﬂux along the normal
to a V-ﬂame and showed a substantial decrease (by a factor of more
than four, see Fig. 2 in the cited paper) in the ﬂux from the leading
to the trailing edge of the ﬂame.
Several physical mechanisms can contribute to the dependence
of the value of St on the choice of a ﬂame surface and on ﬂame
geometry. The ﬁrst one is the development of the mean ﬂame brush
thickness considered in detail elsewhere [43,307]. For example, the
difference between the speed of surfaces characterized by ~c  1
and ~cz1 is proportional to ddt/dt for expanding ﬂames or Uddt/dZ
for stationary ﬂames stabilized after a ﬂame-holder. Here, U is the
mean gas ﬂow velocity and Z is the distance from the ﬂame-holder.
Thus, the highest ﬂame speed is associated with the leading edge of
the mean ﬂame brush.
Second, the normal convective mass ﬂux varies across the ﬂame
in divergent ﬂows and, therefore, a signiﬁcant portion of the
unburned gas entering a control volume through the normal side
associated with the leading edge of the ﬂame can leave the control
volume without burning through the tangential sides. As a result,
the ﬂux ruSt;~c/0 measured at the leading edge substantially
overestimates the mass burning rate integrated along the normal to
the ﬂame [446–448]. This mechanism appears to substantially
affect the values of St obtained either from ﬂames stabilized in
strongly divergent ﬂows (e.g. in impinging jets) or from expanding
ﬂames when double-kernel techniques were used [449]. In the
latter technique, high-speed Schlieren movies are used to deter-
mine the velocity of the leading edge of the ﬂame at the moment of
collision of two statistically identical kernels. Shepherd [447] has
shown that, at this moment, the mean ﬂow is strongly divergent,
and the discussed effect must be properly addressed in order to
correctly interpret the experimental data on St measured with the
technique.
Due to the above effects, the values of turbulent ﬂame speeds
reported in many papers may be substantially overestimated. To take
these effects into account, six different methods have been devel-
oped. First, for a ﬂame expanding in a closed vessel, the mass burning
rate and, hence, ruUt can be evaluated by processing the pressure
history [450,451]. The turbulent burning velocities Ut determined so
are markedly lower than the turbulent ﬂame speeds measured with
the high-speed Schlieren technique under the same conditions [6].
A similar effect was found by Abdel-Gayed et al. [452] by comparing
the mass burning rate evaluated by recording ‘‘the intensity of
emission from the CH radical’’ and the ﬂame speed obtained using
the high-speed Schlieren technique (see Fig. 5 in the cited paper).
Second, Gouldin et al. [453–455] related the mean ﬂame surface
density S to certain measured mean characteristics of the ﬂame-
front orientation, and integrated S across the mean ﬂame brush to
determine the so-called burning rate integral BTwruSL
R Sdx in V-
shaped ﬂames. The reported values of Bt were lower than ru(u0 þ SL)
in all the cases with the exception of two lean (F ¼ 0.69) ethylene-
air ﬂames (nos. 7 and 8 in Ref. [453]),
Third, Shepherd and Kostiuk [446,448] and Gouldin [445] have
proposed evaluating turbulent burning velocity in statistically
stationary turbulent ﬂames by jointly measuring velocity and scalar
ﬁelds, then evaluating the convection and transport terms in the
mean combustion progress variable balance Eq. (18), and ﬁnally
integrating these terms across the mean ﬂame brush in order to
calculate the mass burning rate. The experimental results obtained
by employing this approach in stagnation [448,456], V-shaped
[219,445], Bunsen [457] and low-swirl [458] ﬂames indicate that
ruUt is by a factor of two [219,445], three [458] (cf. squares and
diamonds in Fig. 51), or even four [456] lower than the reactant ﬂux
at the leading edge. In all these ﬂames with the exception of one
Fig. 50. Normalized pressure difference ½pðxÞ  pð0Þ=ðruU2

lean (F ¼ 0.69) ethylene–air ﬂames nos. 7 and 8, the values of Bt
reported by Gouldin [445] were approximately equal to ru(u0 þ SL)
and were markedly lower than the values of Bt evaluated earlier by
Gouldin and Miles [453].
Fourth, Shepherd [459] has proposed evaluating turbulent
burning velocity by measuring the mean ﬂame surface density,
followed by the spatial integration of this quantity across the ﬂame
brush. An application of this method to low-swirl [458] ﬂames has
also shown that ruUt is signiﬁcantly lower than the reactant ﬂux at
the leading edge (i.e. ruSt, cf. pluses and diamonds in Fig. 51) and
Ut < u0. The values of Ut/SL evaluated by Cheng and Bilger [194] by
applying the same method to Bunsen ﬂames were less than u0/SL.
Recently, Lawn and Schefer [460] obtained both turbulent ﬂame
speed (Sd in their notation) and burning velocity (Sc) from a number
of various premixed turbulent ﬂames stabilized using a novel
apparatus (a wide-angle conical diffuser). The values of Sc were
evaluated using the above method developed by Shepherd [459],
whereas Sd was associated with the mean ﬂow velocity normal to
the mean ﬂame brush at c ¼ 0:02. The reported results (see Fig. 52)
show that a ratio of Sc/Sd is markedly lower than unity, with as low
ratios as 0.2 being documented in certain ﬂames.

explosions. In this case, a meaningful deﬁnition of turbulent ﬂame
speed St is impeded by the fact that the speed of any iso-scalar
surface inside a turbulent ﬂame brush is affected not only by the
conversion of reactants into products, but also by the growth of the
mean thickness of the ﬂame brush. To resolve this problem, we
have proposed associating the ﬂame speed with the radius33

In summary, the turbulent ﬂame speed may be signiﬁcantly
higher than the turbulent burning velocity due to the transient and
geometrical effects discussed above. To the best of the authors’
knowledge, no experiment that was performed using advanced
optical diagnostic techniques and a well-elaborated method of
determining Ut yielded a turbulent burning velocity notably
higher,34 than (u0 þ SL). Therefore, the high ratios of St/(u0 þ SL)
reported in the literature have more likely resulted from the tran-
sient and geometrical effects than fromﬂame-generatedturbulence.
5.3. Hydrodynamic instability of laminar premixed ﬂames and
ﬂame-generated turbulence
As already discussed in Section 2.2.1, the so-called hydrody-
namic or Darrieus-Landau (DL) instability predicted theoretically
by Darrieus [64] and Landau [65] causes wrinkling of a laminar
ﬂame surface, ﬂow velocity perturbations, and self-acceleration of
ﬂame propagation. The DL instability of laminar ﬂamelets in
a turbulent ﬂow is sometimes considered to play a substantial role
in
premixed
turbulent
combustion
[9,142,263,265,463–471].
Accordingly, the following two issues are relevant to the scope of
the present review paper. Under what conditions does the DL
instability substantially affect premixed turbulent combustion?
Can the DL instability generate turbulence under these conditions?
The former issue has already been considered in our previous
review paper [44] in which we conclude that the instability is ‘‘of
importance at weak turbulence only.’’ Therefore, the discussion of
the issue in the present subsection is restricted to papers published
over the past few years.
First, Treurniet et al. [142] performed 3D DNS of premixed
ﬂames stabilized in a statistically 1D turbulent ﬂow for four density
ratios (s ¼ 0, 1, 3, and 5). In each case, the inﬂow velocity Uin was
adjusted to stabilize the ﬂame in the middle of the computational
domain. Turbulent ﬂame speed appears to be assumed to be equal
to Uin (cf. Table 1 and Fig. 10 in the cited paper). The reported data
indicate an increase in A ¼ (Uin  SL)/u0 by a factor of three when
increasing s from 0 to 5 (see Figs. 10 and 12 in the cited paper,
where u0 appears to be the rms velocity at the leading edge of the
ﬂame brush, while the inﬂow rms velocity was the same at all the
density ratios), and an anisotropic increase in u0, v0, and w0 within
the ﬂame brush in the ﬂames characterized by s ¼ 3 and 5 (see Figs.
14 and 15 there). These results are associated by Treurniet et al.
[142] with the DL instability.
From our standpoint, the increase in the factor A by s stems from
the commonly disregarded instability of the whole turbulent ﬂame
brush, rather than the commonly emphasized instability of laminar
ﬂamelets within the ﬂame brush.
Indeed, in a statistically planar, 1D case, the classical analysis of
Darrieus [64] and Landau [65] may be applied not only to a laminar,
but also to a turbulent ﬂame. The only difference between the two
cases consists of the wavenumber K of the perturbation. Since
dL  dt, the wavenumber in the latter case should be much smaller
than in the former. Therefore, a premixed turbulent ﬂame appears
to be unstable with respect to a perturbation if its wavelength is
much larger than dt [9].
In DNS of turbulent combustion, the hydrodynamic instability of
a ﬂame brush has not yet been studied, because the width of the
Fig. 52. Ratio of turbulent ﬂame speed to burning velocity obtained from a number of lean CH4–air and CH4/H2–air ﬂames stabilized in a wide-angled conical diffuser. From the
paper by Lawn and Schefer [460] 2006 by The Combustion Institute, reproduced with permission.
34 Recently, Burluka et al. [462] have reported Ut/(u0 þ SL) z 1.5 for a statistically
spherical, expanding, lean (F ¼ 0.5) H2/CO/air, turbulent ﬂame.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
83


--- Page 84 ---

computational domain is comparable with dt. Therefore, the growth
rate of unstable perturbations is limited by purely geometrical
effects and the ﬂame brush takes a conical shape. The conical shape
of the mean ﬂame brush in the case of s ¼ 3 investigated by
Treurniet et al. [142] is clearly shown in Fig. 11 in the cited paper
[142]. Moreover, the conical shape is indirectly indicated by the
long tails of the PDF Pf(x) that characterizes the probability of
ﬁnding the ﬂame front at a position x (see Fig. 13 there). Since the
mean area of the conical surface is larger than its projection on the
yz-plane, the total mass burning rate and, hence, the Uin is
increased when the ﬂame brush takes a conical shape even if the Ut
(associated with an inﬁnitesimal small area of the mean ﬂame
surface) is not changed along the ﬂame brush and is not affected by
the DL instability.
Therefore, the discussed DNS data do not seem to prove that the
DL instability of laminar ﬂamelets within a turbulent ﬂame brush
controls the large values of the factor A at s ¼ 3 and 5. As concerns
the reported increase in the rms velocities within the ﬂame brush,
we are not able to ﬁnd evidence that this effect is controlled by the
instability. In the cited paper, this increase has been shown to be
controlled by the following production term r0u0
iujðvui=vxjÞ, which
can be positive for i ¼ j ¼ 1 only if the correlation r0u0 is negative
(u > 0 and vr=vx < 0 in the DNS). However, the fact that this
correlation was negative in the simulations of both turbulent
ﬂames and unstable laminar ﬂames is not sufﬁcient to claim that
the instability controls the production of u0 within a turbulent
ﬂame brush.
Second, Kobayashi et al. [472–474] experimentally studied high-
pressure premixed turbulent Bunsen ﬂames. By varying the
mixture composition, turbulence characteristics, and pressure in
wide ranges, they were able to substantially vary the length scales
that characterize the wrinkling of the instantaneous ﬂame front in
a turbulent ﬂow, the turbulence length scales, and the character-
istic length scale li of the DL instability. The latter length scale is
associated with the length scale of perturbations that have the
maximum growth rate in the laminar case. Certain reported data
indicate that the smallest scale 3i (the so-called inner cut-off scale)
of the ﬂame front wrinkling is independent of the turbulence
Reynolds number Rel based on the Taylor microscale l of turbu-
lence if li > 12h (see ﬁlled diamonds in Fig. 53). The instability
length scale is also independent of Rel (see dotted line), whereas
the scale of the smallest turbulent eddies (which was estimated to
be equal to 12h by Kobayashi et al. [472,473], where h was the
Kolmogorov length scale) is decreased when Rel is increased (see
open circles). Therefore, the data shown in Fig. 53a (and other
similar data reported by Kobayashi et al. [472,473]) imply that the
smallest wrinkling scale is controlled by the instability scale if
li > 12h. This hypothesis is also supported by a decrease in both li
and 3i when methane–air mixtures are diluted with CO2 [474]. It is
worth noting that Fig. 7 in the cited paper shows that the above
criterion of li ¼ 12h is not universal.
However, even if we recognize the hypothesis to be decisively
proven by the discussed experimental data, this fact does not
indicate that the DL instability plays a substantial role in premixed
turbulent combustion. The data show that both the stabilization of
a laminar premixed ﬂame with respect to perturbations of short
wavelengths, and the stabilization of the instantaneous ﬂame front
with respect to small-scale turbulent eddies are controlled by the
same physical mechanism. However, the data do not allow us to
compare the increase in the ﬂame-front surface area caused by the
instability with the increase in the area caused by turbulent
stretching.
It is worth noting that the dependence of the normalized
turbulent ﬂame speed St/SL (Kobayashi et al. measured the turbu-
lent burning velocity integrated over the entire ﬂame volume) on
the Reynolds number Rel, reported by Kobayashi et al. [473],
appears to show bending at Rel z 55 and li z 12h at Rel z 55 also
(see Fig. 53b). However, the bending does not prove that the DL
instability accelerates turbulent ﬂame propagation at Rel < 55. The
bending just implies that if li > 12h, the smallest turbulent eddies
characterized by l < li cannot wrinkle the instantaneous ﬂame front
due to the aforementioned stabilization mechanism, whereas the
whole turbulence spectrum contributes to the ﬂame front wrin-
kling if li < 12h.
Earlier experimental data by Kobayashi et al. [467,475] that
indicate a substantial increase in the turbulent burning velocity
caused by the pressure are often considered to be evidence of the
important role played by the DL instability in premixed turbulent
combustion. Although these important and impressive data were
already discussed in our previous review papers [43,44], it is worth
brieﬂy touching on the key points here in order to make the picture
complete.
A decrease in li when pressure increases is mainly controlled by
a decrease in laminar ﬂame thickness due to a decrease in molecular
transport coefﬁcients. Accordingly, any model that yields a proper
dependence of Ut on dL (e.g. via a ratio of dL/L, or the chemical time
scale sc ¼ dL/SL, or the Markstein length, etc.) may be invoked to
explain the discussed data. For instance, the dependence of Ut on P,
predicted by Zimont [477,478] [see Eq. (288) at t/N], agrees very
well with the data by Kobayashi et al. [467,475] (see Table 1 in
Ref. [43]). More recent simulations by Muppala and Dinkelacker
[476] have conﬁrmed that the Zimont model [418–420,477,478] well
predicts the high-pressure experimental data obtained by Kobaya-
shi et al. [467,475] from ﬂames characterized by Ret > 150 (it is
worth recalling that the model addresses turbulent ﬂows with well-
pronounced Kolmogorov spectrum). At u0/SL  3, the model, which
yields Ut/0 at u0/0 [see Eq. (288)], underpredicts the measured
Ut. To resolve this problem, Muppala and Dinkelacker [476] have
modiﬁed the Zimont expression for Ut, but have not addressed the
DL instability. Such a modiﬁed model well predicts [476,479] the
weakly turbulent burning velocities measured by Kobayashi et al.
[467,475]. Therefore, the inﬂuence of the DL instability on Ut is not
a unique explanation of the discussed data.
Moreover, ‘‘we may note that the same trends (SL,0 increases but
Ut decreases with decreasing P) were documented by several
independent groups (see Section 3.3.5 in our recent review [43]) at
u0/SL,0 > 1 under reduced pressures, i.e., under such conditions that
the corresponding laminar ﬂames were stable’’ [44].
Third, Savarianandam and Lawn [471] measured the speeds of
premixed turbulent methane- and ethylene-air ﬂames stabilized in
a wide-angled conical diffuser and reported that 2 < St/SL < 5 at u0/
SL  0.2 (see symbols with error bars in Fig. 54), with the measured
values of St/SL being in excellent agreement with values calculated
using the model by Bychkov and Akkerman [469,480] (see squares
without error bars) in the range of u0/SL < 0.5. Based on this
observation, Savarianandam and Lawn [471] have claimed that ‘‘the
DL instabilities play an important role in modifying the turbulent
burning velocity’’ in the region of 0.2 < u0/SL < 0.6.
From our viewpoint, the data reported by Lawn et al. [460,471]
support neither the model by Bychkov and Akkerman [469,480]
nor the cited claim. Since the model yields the turbulent burning
velocity, it should be tested using the measured values of the
turbulent burning velocity, rather than turbulent ﬂame speeds,
which are signiﬁcantly higher than Ut in many ﬂames stabilized in
the diffuser (see Fig. 52 and discussion in Section 5.2). Figs. 52
and 54 appear to be sufﬁcient in order to conclude that the dis-
cussed model substantially overestimates the turbulent burning
velocity.
Moreover, Fig. 55 does not indicate a large ratio of Ut/SL (Sc/SL,0 in
the notation of Lawn and Schefer [460]) at u0/SL < 1. All the
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
84


--- Page 85 ---

experimental points plotted in this range show that Ut/SL < 2, and
almost all the points imply a decrease in Ut/SL when u0/SL decreases,
with the interpolation of these data (see solid line) yielding Ut/SL ¼ 1
at u0/SL > 0. Therefore, a hypothesis of an important role played by
the DL instability in premixed turbulent combustion is not neces-
sary in order to explain the data on turbulent burning velocities and
ﬂame speeds, obtained by Lawn et al. [460,471], because the
reported high values of St are mainly associated with the problem of
deﬁning turbulent ﬂame speed, as discussed in Section 5.2.
Fourth, Bychkov et al. [469,480–484] have developed an inter-
esting approach to evaluating turbulent burning velocity. The
approach combines sophisticated theoretical and numerical results
obtained for laminar and weakly turbulent ﬂows, but invokes
a number of assumptions which seem arbitrary and may easily be
disputed. The approach can be divided into three constituents.
(A) The model by Bychkov [469] assumes that turbulence and the
DL instability affect turbulent burning velocity independent of
each other, and linearly combines the renormalization ideas
[485,486] and the fractal approach [487] to simulate the
turbulence and instability effects, respectively. We can note,
however, that the assumption that the inﬂuence of the DL
instability on Ut is independent of turbulence does not seem to
be consistent with the well-known stabilizing effect [488–490]
of external straining on a laminar ﬂame (in simple words, the
straining ﬂattens out the ﬂame surface), as the instantaneous
strain rates may be very high in a turbulent ﬂow. The reader
interested in further discussion of this model is referred to
Section 4.3 of our recent review paper [44].
(B) The Searby–Clavin equation [491] simulates the dynamics of
small perturbations of a ﬂame within the framework of the
linear theory of weakly turbulent premixed ﬂames. Akkerman
and Bychkov [480] have theoretically solved this equation for
a periodic external ﬂow in the 2D case and have reported that
the normalized increase (DU)T/SL in the burning velocity by an
imposed 2D ‘‘turbulence’’ scales as CT(u0/SL)2, with the factor CT
strongly decreasing when the density ratio increases, e.g.
CT ¼ 0.5 for s ¼ 0, but CT < 0.1 for s > 5. Based on this drop in
CT(s), Akkerman and Bychkov [480] argued that ‘‘the relative
role of external turbulence in the velocity increase is much
smaller than is usually assumed,’’ a highly original and
pretentious claim which strongly needs both validation and
detailed discussion of physical mechanisms that may control
the drop in CT(s). Subsequently, Akkerman and Bychkov [480]
incorporated the dependence of CT(s) into the aforementioned
model by Bychkov [469], i.e. they assumed that the above
theoretical solution obtained in the limit case of weak turbu-
lence was also valid at moderate and strong turbulence.
(C) In subsequent papers [481–484], Bychkov et al. have associated
the factor CT evaluated by Akkerman and Bychkov [480] with
‘‘vortices perpendicular to the direction of ﬂame propagation’’
[483] and have denoted this factor Ct. To allow for ‘‘vortices
Fig. 53. Length scales relevant to turbulent premixed Bunsen ﬂames and normalized turbulent ﬂame speed St/SL vs. the Reynolds number Rel ¼ u0l/nu based on the Taylor
microscale l. From the paper by Kobayashi et al. [473] 2004 by The Combustion Institute, reproduced with permission.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
85


--- Page 86 ---

parallel to the direction of ﬂame propagation,’’ Bychkov et al.
[483] replaced CT in the above model [469,480] with the sum of
CT ¼ Ct þ Cjj. The factor Cjj(s ¼ 5) z 3Ct(s ¼ 5) (see Fig. 7 in
Ref. [484]) was evaluated by semi-analytically [484] and
numerically [481] studying laminar ﬂame propagation in
rotating gas and by associating this process with ﬂamelet
propagation along the axis of vortex ﬁlaments in a turbulent
ﬂow. The latter phenomenon is discussed in Section 7.3 in our
recent review paper [44].
The whole approach and its constituent parts were tested by
straightforwardly comparing the calculated turbulent burning
velocity with turbulent ﬂame speeds obtained (i) by Aldredge et al.
[492] from ﬂames propagating in Taylor-Couette ﬂows [469,482],
(ii) by Klingmann and Johansson [493] from ﬂames stabilized in
impinging jets [480], and (iii) by Lee and Lee [494] from ﬂames
propagating in a rectangular tube [469,480,483,484]. Moreover,
Akkerman and Bychkov [480] tested the approach (see Fig.16 in the
cited paper) against the experimental data by Kobayashi et al. [475]
on turbulent burning velocities averaged over the surfaces of lean
(F ¼ 0.9) propane–air Bunsen ﬂames at atmospheric pressure and
various u0. The values of St/SL or Ut/SL documented in all these
experiments were scattered around straight lines St/SL w u0/SL.
Since such a linear dependence is yielded by almost all models of
premixed turbulent combustion, it cannot be used to rank the
models [43]. The prediction of the linear increase in St/SL by u0/SL is
not sufﬁcient to justify the discussed approach nor the far-reaching
claims cited above (e.g. ‘‘in many experiments on turbulent ﬂames
the Darrieus-Landau instability is more important for the ﬂame
velocity than the external turbulence’’ [480] or ‘‘the DL instability is
of principal importance when the integral turbulence length scale
is large’’ [483]).

without error bar show the ﬂame speeds calculated using the model by Bychkov and Akkerman [469,480]. From the paper by Savarianandam and Lawn [471] 2006 by The

--- Page 87 ---

The challenging feature of the above data might be a high ratio of
St/SL at moderate u0/SL. However, similar effects have already been
well explained by highlighting the difference in the turbulent
burning velocity and ﬂame speed in divergent ﬂows, as discussed in
Section 5.2. The quantitative agreement between the measured data
on St and the calculated Ut, reported by Bychkov et al. [469,480–484]
and by Savarianandam and Lawn [471], seems to put the discussed
approach into question rather than validate it, bearing in mind the
discussion in Section 5.2. To support the approach, the values of Ut
yielded by it should be compared with the turbulent burning
velocity measured using advanced methods of determining Ut, but,
to the best of the present authors knowledge, Bychkov et al. have not
reported any results of such a solid test.
Furthermore, the comparison of validation results published by
Bychkov et al. in different papers is confusing. For instance, the data
by Lee and Lee [494] were used to support all the versions of the
above approach, in particular, the model by Bychkov [469] (Ct ¼ 0.5
and Cjj ¼ 0, see Fig. 4 in the cited paper), the model by Akkerman and
Bychkov [480] (Ct z 0.1 and Cjj ¼ 0, see Fig.17 in the cited paper), as
well as the model by Bychkov et al. [483,484] (Ct z 0.1 and
Cjj z 0.3, see Fig. 3 in Ref. [483] or Fig. 8 in Ref. [484]). Each time,
Bychkov et al. have claimed that ‘‘the obtained analytical results
agree well with experiments’’ [469], ‘‘the theoretical results are in
a reasonable agreement with the experimental data’’ [480], ‘‘we
obtain very good agreement of the theory with the experiments’’
[483]. It is difﬁcult to trust such validation or to justify the value of
the constituents B and C of the approach (if the factors Ct and Cjj
affect Ut weakly). Thus, we do not consider the discussed studies of
Bychkov et al. [469,480–484] to cogently indicate any important
role played by the DL instability in premixed turbulent combustion.
Recently, Akkerman et al. [495] reported results of a 2D
numerical simulation of the interaction of a laminar premixed
ﬂame with a combination of vortexes in a channel. Since the
channel width was small for numerical reasons, the simulations
dealt with the effects of small-scale perturbations on a laminar
ﬂame. The reported numerical results (see ﬁlled symbols in Fig. 56)
do not agree with the model by Akkerman and Bychkov [480] (see
solid lines), and show notable inﬂuence of the DL instability on the
mass burning rate integrated over the channel only at weak
intensities of the vortexes associated with u0/SL < 0.5. By comparing
the numerical results with the aforementioned experimental data
by Lee and Lee [494], Akkerman et al. [495] found that the
measured St was by a factor of four higher than the computed Ut,
and associated this difference with the inﬂuence of the large-scale
DL instability, not studied numerically. From our viewpoint, the
ratio of St/Ut z 4 is related to the problem of determining turbulent
ﬂame speed, discussed in Section 5.2, rather than the DL instability.
Finally, it is worth emphasizing the following very important
prediction of Bychkov et al. [480–484,495]. Both the analytical and
numerical results obtained in these papers indicate that the
normalized increase (DU)T/SL in the burning velocity is rapidly
decreased by the perturbation length scale (see Fig. 56) if this
length scale is of the order of the aforementioned characteristic
length scale of the DL instability. This prediction, which is consis-
tent with the experimental data by Kobayashi et al. [472–474] (see
Fig. 53) and with the experimental data on the inner cut-off scale,
analyzed by Gu¨lder and Smallwood [496], clearly indicates that the
same molecular transport processes stabilize both a laminar ﬂame,
with respect to perturbations, and ﬂamelet in a turbulent ﬂow, with
respect to small-scale eddies.
Fifth, Bradley et al. [470] have analyzed various factors (ﬂamelet
stretching by turbulent eddies, local combustion extinction, PDFs
of ﬂame stretch rate, ﬂamelet instabilities, etc.) that could affect
turbulent burning velocity. To assess the inﬂuence of the DL
instability on Ut, Bradley et al. [30,470,497,498] analyzed experi-
mental data [499–501] on the cellular structure of expanding
spherical laminar ﬂames within the framework of the linear
instability theory of Bechtold and Matalon [502]. In particular,
Bradley et al. [30,470,497,498] stressed that the ﬂame instabilities
manifested themselves when the local stretch rate was below
a critical stretch rate _scl, whereas higher stretch rates suppressed
the instabilities. Further, they parameterized _scl by analyzing
experimental data [499] on the critical ﬂame radius rf,cl and Peclet
number Pecl ¼ rf,cl/dL at which cells appeared at the ﬂame surface.
Subsequently, they associated the inner cut-off scale of ﬂamelet
surface wrinkling in a turbulent ﬂow with the smallest wavelength
lcl of unstable cells at the surface of the counterpart expanding
spherical laminar ﬂame, i.e. the cell wavelength ‘‘just prior to ﬁs-
sioning’’ of the cell into smaller cells. Fissioning happened when,
due to the growth of cell size, the local stretch rate decreased
below the critical value and the cell became unstable. Finally,
Bradley et al. [30,470,497,498] have parameterized the wavelength
lcl by combining the linear instability theory of Bechtold and
Matalon [502] and experimental data [500,501]. The two param-
eterizations (for _scl and lcl) were incorporated into a model for
evaluating Ut.

been computed at u00/SL ¼ 0.05, 0.5, and 1, respectively. Solid lines and open symbols have been calculated using the model by Akkerman and Bychkov [480] with the initial u00 and

The results of calculations reported by Bradley et al. [470] (see
Figs. 3, 4, and 10 in the cited paper) imply that the DL instability can
signiﬁcantly affect Ut only at weak turbulence (u0 < SL). The minor
inﬂuence of the instability on Ut at higher u0 is mainly associated
with the stabilizing effect of turbulent stretching on ﬂamelets. For
instance, the critical Peclet numbers reported by Gu et al. [499] are
typically higher than 1000. Since the stretch rate is equal to (2/
rf)(drf/dt) z 2(1 þ s)SL/(dLPecl), the ratio of the critical stretch rate to
the rms strain rate u0/l ¼ (3/15nu)1/2 of a randomly oriented surface
in isotropic turbulence [9,147] is of the order of
ﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃ

provided that 3 ¼ 0.5u03/L [503]. For s ¼ 7, this ratio is larger than
unity if Ka < 0.04, i.e. if turbulence is very weak.
Sixth, Steinberg et al. [202] measured 3D instantaneous velocity
ﬁelds within a Bunsen ﬂame by using a new ‘‘cinema stereoscopic
PIV’’ technique, and processed these data in order to determine the
evolution of the vorticity ﬁeld and the ﬂame-front surface, which
was associated with ‘‘an iso-contour of particle image density
gradient’’. In some cases (see Fig. 9 in the cited paper), the obtained
2D images of the velocity ﬁeld and ﬂame front were similar to the
ﬂow picture shown in Fig. 1, i.e. the vorticity was negligible just
upstream of the ﬂame front and the ‘‘ﬂow was channeled into the
negatively curved ﬂame regions and away from those positively
curved’’ [202]. Steinberg et al. [202] associated this observation
with the DL instability.
It is worth noting, however, that the images do not seem to
prove any important role played by the DL instability in premixed
turbulent combustion. The key peculiarity of the DL instability
consists of the self-perturbation and self-acceleration of an inﬁni-
tesimally perturbed laminar premixed ﬂame due to the inﬂuence of
the pressure drop in the ﬂame on the upstream ﬂow. Such an effect
is only possible if D h ru/rb > 1. However, the DL-solution given by
Eq. (40) is valid not only for D > 1 but also for D ¼ 1 (the growth rate
U is zero in the latter case), and the dependence of uu and vu on x
and y is not affected by the density ratio. The upstream ﬂow lines
shown in Fig. 1 result straightforwardly from the Navier–Stokes
equations regardless of whether or not the ﬂame is unstable.35 Due
to the internal intermittency [9,61,111,112], both regions charac-
terized by high vorticity and regions characterized by negligible
vorticity co-exist in a constant-density turbulent ﬂow, and the
discussed picture may be observed in the latter regions even if the
ﬂame is stable (e.g. if D ¼ 1).
Seventh, Yuan et al. [504,505] performed 2D DNS of statistically
planar premixed ﬂames and reported that the DL instability
dominated the ﬂame-front dynamics at u0/SL  0.05, whereas
turbulence dominated the ﬂame-front dynamics at u0/SL ¼ 0.5.
All in all, the recent studies discussed in this subsection do not
seem to indicate that the DL instability can substantially increase
the turbulent burning velocity at u0 > SL, although such an increase
in Ut due to the instability appears to be possible at weak turbu-
lence (u0 < SL) [44].
As concerns the inﬂuence of the instability on turbulence, the
classical solution [see Eq. (40)], obtained by Darrieus [64] and
Landau [65] shows that vorticity is generated in products, whereas
the unburned mixture remains irrotational. Since turbulence is
rotational motion, this solution yields no turbulence generation in
the unburned mixture. Certainly, in a turbulent ﬂow, vorticity
generated in the products may diffuse to the unburned mixture as
assumed by Karlovitz et al. [55]. However, we are not aware of data
that unambiguously indicate an increase in the turbulence in the
unburned mixture caused by the DL instability.
It is worth noting that the development of the DL instability
takes time. For instance, the growth rates of the instability, exper-
imentally obtained by Truffaut and Searby [506] from slightly rich
(1.05  F  1.33) propane–air ﬂames, did not exceed 400 s–1 (see
Fig. 6 in the cited paper), i.e. a perturbation of a laminar ﬂame
surface needed at least 2.5 ms in order for the perturbation
amplitude to grow by a factor of 2.7. Such a growth rate appears to
be too slow in comparison with the rate of changes of the local
strain rate in a typical turbulent ﬂow. Accordingly, even if the DL
instability begins to develop at a certain point x at a certain instant t
(e.g. due to a low strain rate there), the process is likely to be
suppressed after a short time (as soon as the local strain rate
becomes high enough). Moreover, the length scale of the instability
appears to be restricted by the distance from the point x to a region
characterized by a high local strain rate. Since the length of the
most rapidly growing perturbations in the aforementioned exper-
iments was as large as 60dL or even larger (see Fig. 6 in the cited
paper), i.e., substantially larger than the Kolmogorov length scale
under typical conditions, such perturbations are likely to be sup-
pressed by the highly non-uniform spatial distribution of local
strain rates in a turbulent ﬂow.
The above discussion in the present subsection deals with pre-
mixed ﬂame propagation against a turbulent ﬂow. In laminar ﬂows,
the DL instability may play an important role and may signiﬁcantly
accelerate ﬂame expansion. The following phenomenon docu-
mented in experiments with large, freely propagating, spherical
ﬂames ignited in an initially quiescent mixture [507–510] appears to
be an obvious example of the inﬂuence of the instability on laminar
ﬂame propagation. As an initially laminar spherical ﬂame kernel
grows, it becomes unstable, and a self-similar regime of ﬂame
expansion, characterized by rf f t3/2 and a high ﬂame speed, is
reached provided that the kernel radius rf is sufﬁciently large. This
phenomenon is often associated with the growth of unstable laminar
ﬂame disturbances, as discussed elsewhere [497,508,511–515].
5.4. Can we characterize turbulence in premixed ﬂames?
As brieﬂy discussed in Section 2.1.5, in order to model the effects
of turbulence on premixed combustion, we have to know the rms
velocity ut0 that characterizes the turbulence, rather than the Rey-

Although the hypothesis that the conditioned Reynolds stresses
characterize true turbulence appears to be obvious on the ﬁrst
sight, it was recently put into question [516] by studying certain
very simple constant-density problems.
For instance, if we consider a planar, inﬁnitely thin, self-propa-

The following simple problem questions not only Eq. (308) but
also Eq. (309). Let us consider an inﬁnitely thin ‘‘ﬂame’’ stabilized in
the following ﬂow uðyÞ ¼ U  ðSL  UÞcosðKyÞ; where U > SL. To
draw an analogy with premixed turbulent combustion, let us (i)
average all quantities in the y-direction and (ii) consider the peri-

j to be a perturbation which increases the mean ﬂame speed and
thickness in the x-direction similarly as turbulent eddies increase
the burning velocity and the mean thickness of a premixed ﬂame.
Then, the mean value qðx*Þ of a quantity q at point x* is evaluated by
averaging q along the line x ¼ x*, whereas the conditioned quantities
quðx*Þ and qbðx*Þ are obtained by averaging q along parts of this line,
associated with unburned and burned mixture.
Some results of such calculations are reported in Fig. 57. Curve 1
in Fig. 57b shows the mean rms velocity u02h½uðyÞ  U2, which
characterizes true ‘‘turbulence,’’ because the ﬂame does not affect
the ﬂow in the constant-density case considered. A comparison of

a ﬂow, the method of conditional averaging divides the ﬂow ﬁeld into
two regions; the ﬂow in the burned and the ﬂow in the unburned
mixture. Since the instantaneous ﬂame surface has to be closer to
(farther from) the leading edge of the mean ﬂame brush in order for
us to observe the burned (unburned) mixture at a point (x, y) of
measurement, the ﬂow velocity in the vicinity of this point should be
sufﬁciently low (high). Thus, the conditional averaging in the burned
(unburned) mixture is associated with averaging over low-speed
(high-speed) regions of the whole ﬂow ﬁeld in the constant-density
case considered. Accordingly, the conditionally averaged quantities
do not represent the mean quantities in a general case.
To further support this claim, let us consider turbulent mixing in
the constant-density case. If an inert admixture is introduced into
statistically stationary, homogeneous, isotropic turbulence (the
mass fraction Y of the admixture is equal to Y0 and zero at x > 0 and
x < 0, respectively, at t ¼ 0), and the molecular diffusivity D of the
admixture is sufﬁciently small, then for a long time the molecular
mixing layer will be much thinner than the turbulent mixing layer
formed by the random motion of the former layer, caused by
turbulent eddies. Accordingly, the well-known BML Eq. (47) holds
with c ¼ Y/Y0. Since the turbulent diffusion approximation works
well in such a simple case, we obtain
ub  uu ¼ 
Dt
cð1  cÞ
dc
dx < 0
(310)
combining Eqs. (47) and (53), i.e. the velocities uu and ub, condi-
tioned on c ¼ 0 and c ¼ 1, respectively, are not equal to one another.
Therefore, the last term on the RHS of Eq. (49) does not vanish and
Eq. (309) underestimates the true rms turbulence velocity.
The difference between u0 and either u0u or u0b may further be
emphasized by applying the conditioned balance Eqs. (215)–(220)
discussed in Section 4.4.2 to a constant-density ﬂame with the
probability g  1 everywhere. In such a case, variations in the ﬂow
velocity and pressure across the instantaneous ﬂame front may be
neglected. If we assume that perturbations in the internal structure of
the front, caused by turbulent eddies, are weak, then we obtain [516]

(312)
The ﬁrst line of Eq. (311) look very similar to the standard
Reynolds-averaged Navier–Stokes Eq. (17), but the former equation
contains two extra terms, A and B, which come from conditional
averaging which cuts off parts of the ﬂow characterized by either
high or low velocities. Due to the extra terms A and B in Eq. (311),
the assumption that u ¼ uu appears to be wrong in a general
constant-density case.
Similarly, the ﬁrst four lines of Eq. (312) resemble Eq. (22) if
r ¼ const, but the former equation involves three extra terms, C, D,
and E. Even if term C may be neglected in isotropic turbulence for
symmetry reasons, and term E may be omitted by referring to
recent DNS data indicating that uuzuf;u (see Fig. 41), there is still
no justiﬁcation for ignoring term D. Due to this extra term in Eq.
(312), the assumption that the conditioned Reynolds stresses
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
89


--- Page 90 ---

characterize true turbulence appears to be wrong in a general
constant-density case.
Thus, both the above analysis of simple cases and the discussion
of the extra [in comparison with Eqs. (17) and (22)] terms in Eqs.
(311) and (312) clearly show that the conditioned Reynolds stresses
should not be considered to characterize the true turbulence,
because they are also affected by the unburned–burned intermit-
tency. The conditioned Reynolds stresses differ from the rms
velocity in constant-density ﬂows, because conditional averaging
cuts off the ﬂow regions characterized by either high or low
velocities.
This claim certainly does not mean that the use of Eqs. (307), or
(308), or (309) always yields substantial errors. First, if a ratio of u0/SL
is sufﬁciently high, as is often the case in internal combustion
engines, then, the inﬂuence of premixed combustion on turbulence
appears to be of minor importance, and the above simple expres-
sions may be a reasonable approximation. Second, the simple
models considered above may overestimate errors associated with
Eqs. (308) and (309). For instance, the velocity uf;u conditioned on
the ﬂame front differs substantially from uu (cf. solid and dashed
lines in Fig. 57a) in the simple ﬂow studied above, whereas DNS [281]
indicate that uf;uzuu in premixed turbulent ﬂames (see Fig. 41).
Nevertheless, the hypothesis that either Eq. (308) or Eq. (309)
will always allow us to evaluate true turbulence velocity with
satisfactory accuracy does not seem to be justiﬁed. Further research
into the contribution of the unburned–burned intermittency to the
magnitude of the conditioned Reynolds stresses in reacting ﬂows is
necessary in order to clarify the range of applicability and the
limitations of Eqs. (308) and (309).
From this perspective, the DNS of a constant-density premixed
ﬂame propagating in homogeneous isotropic turbulence appears to
be of paramount interest. Since a ﬂame does not affect turbulence
in a constant-density ﬂow, true turbulence characteristics are
known in such DNS and the evaluation of ðu0
iu0
jÞu and ðu0
iu0
jÞb by
processing the computed database offers us the opportunity to
attain a new insight into the difference between the true turbu-
lence velocity ut0 and the velocity associated with the conditioned
Reynolds stresses.
5.5. Vorticity in premixed ﬂames
The above discussion shows that the ATKE and the standard rms
velocity u0 may not be proper characteristics of turbulence in
a premixed ﬂame. Since turbulent motion is always rotational, an
attractive approach to characterizing turbulence in premixed
ﬂames would be based on analyzing vorticity, rather than u0.
Unfortunately, there is little contemporary knowledge of the
behavior of vorticity in premixed turbulent ﬂames.
Several researchers [64,65,517–520] have analyzed the genera-
tion of vorticity by an inﬁnitely thin laminar premixed ﬂame in the
case of negligible viscosity [terms 2 and 20 in Eqs. (13) and (15),
respectively, vanish]. Darrieus [64] and Landau [65] considered
a 2D inﬁnitely thin laminar ﬂame wrinkled by a weak velocity
perturbation and predicted vorticity generation behind the ﬂame

[see Eq. (40)]. Matalon and Matkowsky [141] theoretically deter-

ﬂame by resolving its thermal structure and allowing for viscous
effects.
Uberoi et al. [518] and later Pindera and Talbot [520] studied
vorticity generated by a 2D ﬂame in the stationary case. By
rewriting the Euler Eq. (3) as follows

proportional to the strain rate in the case considered.
Hayes [517] and later Berndt [519] derived more complicated
equations for the vorticity jump across an unsteady 3D ﬂame in the
case of zero viscosity. In the stationary case, their results (e.g. see
Eq. (17) in Ref. [517]) reduce to Eq. (315). The analytical expression
for the vorticity jump, reported by Hayes [517], was widely invoked
to simulate premixed turbulent combustion by tracking the motion
of the instantaneous ﬂame front [520–524], or by using the random
vortex method [525].

(317)
If the baroclinic torque term on the RHS of Eq. (317) vanishes, then,
u
!=r is conserved along a ﬂow line, i.e. a premixed ﬂame dampens
vorticity in this simplest case.36 Therefore, baroclinic torque plays
a vital role in the generation of vorticity by a premixed ﬂame. Since
baroclinic torque depends substantially on the misalignment of the
pressure and density gradients, the magnitude of ﬂame-generated
vorticity (FGV) should be very sensitive to ﬂame geometry [526].
Depending on the angle between the vectors Vr and Vp, a laminar
ﬂame can either strengthen incident vorticity or dampen it and
generate the counter-rotating vortexes in the burned mixture.
Various scenarios of the behavior of vorticity in the vicinity of
a laminar premixed ﬂame were observed when investigating the
interaction between a ﬂame and a vortex pair or a single vortex.
Ashurst and McMurtry [525] simulated the interaction between
a laminar ﬂame and an isolated vortex by utilizing the random
vortex method in a 2D case. The creation of circulation of both signs
was documented in the simulations, ‘‘but the predominant
production’’ was ‘‘circulation with rotation opposite to the initial
value’’ and ‘‘was primarily in the ﬂame-tip region.’’
FGV was also observed in 2D DNS of a laminar ﬂame interacting
either with a single vortex [527] or with a counter-rotating vortex
pair [528]. Rutland and Ferziger [527] reported also that the
magnitude of the FGV was increased by the density ratio, and the
ﬂame affected the incident vortex in reactants.
Driscoll et al. [529,530] experimentally investigated the inter-
action between a single toroidal vortex and a laminar ﬂame that
moved downwards. No FGV was documented in reactants [529],
but the ﬂame reduced the incident circulation and produced
counter-rotating FGV in the products in the case of a weak (the ratio
Uq/SL of the maximum rotational velocity Uq to the laminar ﬂame
speed was equal to 1.4) incident vortex [530]. A strong (Uq/SL ¼ 10.1)
incident vortex was weakly affected by the ﬂame. In an interme-
diate case (Uq/SL ¼ 3.6), a part of the incident vorticity survived and
the counter-rotating FGV was lower than the incident vorticity.
The attenuation of weak and intermediate incident vortexes was
shown [530] to be controlled by dilatation [see term 3 on the RHS of
Eq. (13) or r–1 on the LHS of Eq. (317)], whereas the counter-rotating
FGV was mainly caused by the contribution of gravity to baroclinic
torque [see term 4 on the RHS of Eq. (13) or the RHS of Eq. (317)]. The
counter-rotating FGV was claimed to stabilize the ﬂame [530]. It is
worth noting that the stabilizing effect of FGV on a weakly perturbed
laminar ﬂame was predicted by Darrieus [64] and Landau [65].
Indeed, Eq. (40) shows that the potential ({ub1, vb1}) and rotational
({ub2, vb2}) components of the ﬂow velocity vector at the mean
ﬂame surface (x ¼ 0) have opposite signs, with the magnitude of the
potential component being larger. Accordingly, rotational ﬂow
reduces the destabilizing effect of the potential ﬂow.
The experimental results obtained by Mueller et al. [530] using
a weak incident vortex were qualitatively reproduced by Louch and
Bray [426,531] in 2D DNS of the interaction between a laminar ﬂame
and a vortex ring. These simulations clearly show a substantial
contribution of gravity to FGV. For a ﬂame that moves upwards,
baroclinic torque strengthens the incident vorticity in some sections
of the ﬂame, but destroys the incident vorticity and produces the
counter-rotating FGV in other sections (‘‘particularly the tip regions
protruding into the burned gas’’ [426]). The FGV is strengthened by
an increase in the density ratio and by a decrease in ﬂame thickness,
because baroclinic torque depends on the density ratio and ﬂame
thickness more strongly than dilatation [531].
Hasegawa et al. [532] performed 2D DNS of a laminar ﬂame
interacting with a counter-rotating vortex pair. For the weakest (Uq/
SL ¼ 4.7) vortex pair investigated, the counter-rotating FGV was
documented in products, but the total circulation retained the same
sign throughout the vortex-ﬂame interaction in a qualitative agree-
ment with the data reported by Mueller et al. [530] for Uq/SL ¼ 3.6. For
stronger vortex pairs (Uq/SL ¼ 21.8 or 54.7), no counter-rotating FGV
was reported. In these two cases, the ﬂame strengthened the incident
vorticity in reactants, it reduced the distance between two vortexes
forming a pair, and it accelerated the vortex pair motion towards the
ﬂame.
Pan et al. [533] simulated the interaction between a laminar
ﬂame and a weak (Uq/SL ¼ 1/8 or 2) counter-rotating vortex pair by
tracking the ﬂame in a 2D case. In line with the previous studies
36 Based on Eq. (317), we might claim that a planar 1D laminar ﬂame dampens
vorticity, rather than generates it. We may note, however, that such a ﬂame could
exist only in the irrotational ﬂow of an unburned mixture, i.e. this ﬂame neither
dampens nor generates vorticity, because ub ¼ uu ¼ 0.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102
91


--- Page 92 ---

[426,529–531], the ﬂame destroyed the incident vorticity and
produced the counter-rotating FGV for the heat release factor
s ¼ 4. However, for s ¼ 1, the incident vorticity survived when
passing the ﬂame, even in the case of the weakest (Uq/SL ¼ 1/8)
vortex pair.
The above brief review of research into the inﬂuence of
a laminar ﬂame on a single vortex or a vortex pair clearly shows that
even this simplest model problem is not simple and different
scenarios are possible depending on vortex strength, density ratio,
etc. Baroclinic torque may either destroy or strengthen incident
vorticity depending on the angle between the pressure and density
gradients. The signs of the incident vorticity and the vorticity
behind the ﬂame may be either the same or the opposite,
depending on vortex strength and density ratio. The incident
vorticity may either be destroyed by a ﬂame or survive, depending
on vortex strength and on the ratio of the dilatation and baroclinic
torque terms in Eq. (13).
The behavior of vorticity within a premixed turbulent ﬂame has
been examined in a few papers. As noted by Louch and Bray [531],
the vorticity contours obtained in 2D DNS with s  3 ‘‘show weak
vortical structures in the burnt gas compared to the unburnt gas’’
(see Fig. 5d in Ref. [534], Figs. 6a and 7a in Ref. [268], Fig. 5 in
Ref. [272], Fig. 6 in Ref. [535], and Figs. 5–7 in Ref. [131]). However,
such 2D DNS do not allow for the vorticity production due to vortex
stretching [see terms 1 and 10 on the RHSs of Eqs. (13) and (15),
respectively], which plays an important role in turbulent ﬂows.
Moreover, baroclinic torque is likely to play a more important role
in ﬂames characterized by a larger density ratio [531].
Experimental data obtained by Filatyev et al. [433] from stoi-
chiometric methane–air Bunsen ﬂames indicate that the instanta-
neous ﬂame front destroyed almost all eddies characterized by
juzj > 2000 s–1 (see Figs. 5 and 6 in the cited paper) under the
conditions of the measurements (u0/SL ¼ 4.5). This effect was
associated with spreading ‘‘the rotational angular momentum over
a large region in space’’ [433] due to gas expansion [see Eq. (317)
with zero RHS].
Steinberg et al. [202] exploited the same Bunsen burner in order
to experimentally investigate the dynamics of an instantaneous
ﬂame front by measuring a three-component velocity ﬁeld with
high temporal and spatial resolution using cinema stereoscopic PIV.
Lean (F ¼ 0.6, u0/SL ¼ 3.8 and F ¼ 0.7, u0/SL ¼ 2.3) and rich (F ¼ 1.35,
u0/SL ¼ 2.3) methane–air ﬂames were studied. Vorticity contours
plotted in Figs. 7–12 in the cited paper show time histories of
typical ﬂame-eddy interactions. In particular, the attenuation of the
incident eddies by the ﬂame front, followed by the production of
the counter-rotating eddies was identiﬁed in the vicinity of the
negatively curved ﬂame-wrinkle tips (e.g. see Fig. 58 here or Fig. 7
in Ref. [202]). At the same time, extensive regions characterized by
a low ju
!j are clearly seen in products in Figs. 7, 8, 11, and 12 in the
cited paper (see also the upper left and lower right regions in
Fig. 58). Therefore, the reported experimental data do not allow us
to assess the sign (strengthening or attenuation) of the integral
effect of premixed combustion on vorticity.
Experimental data obtained by Filatyev et al. [536] from highly
turbulent, swirl-stabilized, stoichiometric methane–air ﬂames do
not indicate any signiﬁcant effect of the instantaneous ﬂame front
on vorticity (see Figs. 5 and 6 in the cited paper).
Certainly, the few examples of experimental data brieﬂy
reviewed above are not sufﬁcient to solidly conclude about the
transformation of vorticity in premixed turbulent ﬂames. Never-
theless, these data cast doubt on the validity of the concept of
ﬂame-generated turbulence.
First, while the attenuation of turbulent eddies in products has
been documented both in DNS [131,268,272,534,535] and in
experiments [433], no study indicates strengthening of the vortical
structures in an extended spatial region behind the instantaneous
ﬂame front. Although FGV has been observed by Steinberg et al.
[202] in localized spatial regions, the vorticity averaged over the
burned mixture does not seem to be stronger than the vorticity
averaged over the unburned mixture in their experiments (see Figs.
7–12 in the cited paper). The above DNS and experimental data
imply that even if the mean vortex-stretching term 100 and the

200 on the RHS of the Reynolds-averaged Eq. (316), the mean

in a typical premixed turbulent ﬂame.
Second, even if we highlight the FGV observed locally by
Steinberg et al. [202], Driscoll [52] has pointed out that this
vorticity ‘‘is a stabilizing factor that tends to decrease the amplitude
of the wrinkles’’ (e.g. see white arrows, which show the direction of
the rotational ﬂow in the FGV region in Fig. 58). Therefore, this FGV
neither increases the ﬂame-front area nor the turbulent burning
velocity.
6. Conclusions
The present review may very brieﬂy be summarized as follows.
Premixed ﬂames can substantially affect a ﬂuctuating velocity ﬁeld
and both the magnitude and direction of turbulent scalar ﬂuxes, but
we are not yet capable of predicting such effects in a general case,
especially if the upstream rms turbulence velocity u0 and the
laminar ﬂame speed SL are of the same order.
The hypothesis that turbulence generated by combustion
substantially accelerates ﬂame propagation does not seem to be
supported by the experimental and DNS data reviewed in Section 3.
Nevertheless, such a mechanism might be of importance in certain
unstable large-scale ﬂames, e.g. in self-accelerating unconﬁned
spherical ﬂame kernels.
More speciﬁc conclusions are reported in Sections 3.4 and 4.6,
whereas certain important unresolved basic issues are highlighted
in Section 5.
7. Addendum: a physical appraisal
With great care, we have critically reviewed the large amount of
literature dealing with the problem of the inﬂuence of heat release
on turbulence and premixed ﬂame propagation, posed by Scurlock
et al. [53,54] and Karlovitz et al. [55] more than a half century ago.
We have arrived at the conclusion that while the problem is still
very intricate and far from the ﬁnal solution, there is no convincing
evidence that turbulence generated by combustion can substan-
tially accelerate burning in a moderately or highly turbulent pre-
mixed ﬂame, the size of which is of the order of the integral length
scale of the upstream turbulence. To gain some ground for further
studies and to support the above conclusion with physical argu-
ments, we have decided to close the paper with an unusual attempt
to describe the mechanism of ﬂame-turbulence interaction in
physical terms by discussing an elementary case.
For this purpose, let us consider a premixed ﬂame front in a ﬂow
where turbulence is represented by a single shear wave u0
KcosðKyÞ of
the Fourier decomposition of a ﬂuctuating velocity [8], as shown in
Fig. 59. If the amplitude u0
K of the wave is much less than the laminar
ﬂame speed, the problem is described by the DL solution [64–66], see
Eq. (40), Accordingly, the effect of the heat release on the ﬂuctuating
ﬂow velocity is strong and causes the hydrodynamic instability of the
ﬂame. What is the maximum magnitude of the effect?
To answer this question, let us invoke the classical result
obtained by Zel’dovich [67,537] when studying an asymptotic
state37 of the hydrodynamic instability of a bounded laminar
ﬂame. Following the seminal proposal by Petersen and Emmons
[305] and by Shchelkin [538], Zel’dovich [67,537] highlighted the
stabilizing role played by cusps (see points Qi in Fig. 59) formed
on a curved ﬂame surface according to the Huygens’ wave-
interaction effect, and showed that ‘‘the damping rate for the
perturbation amplitude owing to the greater velocity of the
cusps’’ [67] should scale as K2SLA2, where A is the amplitude of
the perturbation of the ﬂame surface. By assuming that the per-
turbed ﬂame front has a parabolic shape and considering the
limit case of a small angle between the ﬂame front and the y-axis
on the cusps (i.e. angle 4 in Fig. 59 is slightly smaller than p/2),
the coefﬁcient in this scaling law was calculated to be equal to 2/
p2 [67]. Then, Zel’dovich [67,537] evaluated the asymptotic
amplitude AN of the ﬂame perturbation by setting dA/dt ¼ 0 in
the following equation

Fig. 59. An elementary turbulent ﬂame generated by a single shear wave that represents turbulence. Straight streamlines B0B and C0C cut out a representative ﬂame segment.
37 It is worth stressing that we are considering a single ﬂame perturbation with
a single wavelength in the present addendum. Such a model problem is relevant to
ﬂames of a statistically stationary size. If ﬂame size grows (e.g. large, freely prop-
agating, expanding spherical ﬂames [507–510]), then, larger and larger perturba-
tions wrinkle the ﬂame surface. Therefore, even if an asymptotic state of ﬂame
instability is reached for a particular perturbation, the ﬂame will still be unstable
and will self-accelerate due to the expansion of the spectrum of perturbations to
larger wavelengths.
A.N. Lipatnikov, J. Chomiak / Progress in Energy and Combustion Science 36 (2010) 1–102

--- Page 94 ---

Let us consider the limit case of s[1 for the sake of simplicity,
and discuss the ﬂow in the vicinity of the curved ﬂame shown in
Fig. 59. To do so, it is worth using an analogy between the
asymptotic state of the hydrodynamic instability of a bounded
laminar ﬂame and the asymptotic state of the propagation of
a laminar ﬂame in a channel, as drawn by Uberoi [539] ﬁfty years
ago. If we consider a segment of the ﬂame, cut out by straight
streamlines BB0 and CC0, the propagation of this segment, which is
isolated from the inﬂuence of neighboring segments by the straight
streamlines, is representative for the whole ﬂame and is in many
aspects like the propagation of a laminar ﬂame in a channel.
There have been many attempts [539–545] to develop a theory
of a laminar ﬂame in a channel, but without assuming the shape or,
at least, the area of the ﬂame, the problem remains unresolved.
Nevertheless, the basic physics of the problem appears to be quite
clear.
First of all, we know that a laminar ﬂame develops towards
a stationary shape. Then, the ﬂame as a whole moves substantially
faster than the unperturbed laminar ﬂame. For instance, Emmons
[540] has calculated the asymptotically stationary velocity of an
entire ﬂame (s ¼ 4.5) to be equal to 2.5SL.
Second, the stationarity of the ﬂame shape requires the leading
point O to move equally fast despite the fact that the ﬂame is locally
convex towards the unburned mixture and, therefore, the local
speed of the leading point with respect to the mixture is typically
lower than SL (for the Lewis number Le  1). Then, the only possi-
bility for the fast movement of the leading point is a local reduction
in the ﬂow velocity before the leading point, caused by the diver-
gence of the local streamlines (see Fig. 59). The ﬂow divergence is
associated with a high pressure zone developed in the vicinity of
point O. The high pressure zone that pushes the leading point
forward results from the collision of product streamlines generated
by the curved side ﬂames that follow the leading point. The pres-
sure level achieved in the discussed zone depends on the density
ratio, the ﬂame surface area, and the curvature of the side ﬂames.
All these quantities evolve until an equilibrium is reached between
the movement of the leading point and the ﬂame size and shape.
Third, heat release generates a local pressure gradient normal to
the ﬂame front and an axial gradient of the pressure averaged across
the segment. The latter (mean) gradient accelerates both the
unburned and burned mixture, with the cold fresh gas being
accelerated less than the hot products due to the higher density of
the unburned mixture. This physical mechanism causes counter-
gradient scalar transport, as far as quantities averaged across
a cross-section of the segment are concerned. Indeed, for any line
x ¼ xf, where xO < xf < xQ 1 (see Fig. 59), the burned mixture is
accelerated for the longest time on the axis of symmetry, whereas
the shortest acceleration time is associated with points, where the
dividing streamlines BB0 and CC0 cross the ﬂame. Therefore,
throughout the whole combustion zone, the product ﬂow velocity is
the lowest at points Qi, where the axial ﬂow acceleration by the local
pressure gradient across the ﬂame front is the lowest for geomet-
rical reasons, and the axial product ﬂow acceleration by the mean
pressure gradient vanishes. To the contrary, as far as the unburned
mixture is concerned, at these points, the axial velocity is the
highest due to the long time of the mean pressure-driven acceler-
ation. Since the tangential (to the local ﬂame front) ﬂow velocity
does not change when crossing the front, the highest axial velocity
in the unburned mixture is approximately equal (if angle 4  1 in
Fig. 59) to the lowest axial velocity in the products, and the two
velocities are observed at the trailing edge of the whole ﬂame.
Therefore, if we consider the axial velocity averaged along the y-axis
(see Fig. 59) to draw an analogy with the behavior of mean quan-
tities in a premixed turbulent ﬂame, then ub > uu at any point x
within the averaged ﬂame, i.e. the pressure-driven scalar ﬂux ru00c00
is countergradient by virtue of Eq. (47). It is worth remembering that
we discuss the limit case of s[1 and u0=SL  1.
Fourth, vorticity is generated by the ﬂame owing to baroclinic
torque, see term 4 on the RHS of Eq. (13), as the pressure gradient is
not parallel to the density gradient due to the axial mean pressure
gradient discussed above.
Fifth, the uneven distribution of the axial velocity in the prod-
ucts, due to the dependence of the pressure-driven acceleration
time on y, creates a shear layer behind the ﬂame, as described by
Scurlock et al. [53,54,56]. The instability of the shear layer, in turn,
creates turbulence in a complex process of the formation of two-
dimensional Tollmien-Schlichting waves [423], which become
nonlinear in the second stage, then strongly curved in the third
stage, followed by a fourth stage when they start to interact with
each other (pairing) and to form larger entities. Finally, in the last
stage, secondary instabilities develop and breakdown of coherent
structures into turbulence occurs. From many experimental and
numerical simulations, the breakdown of the shear layer is known
to take a long time.
Turbulence generated in the products in this process may,
without question, be called ‘‘ﬂame-generated turbulence.’’ The
turbulence intensity is controlled by the velocity difference in the
products and, thus, is a function of the total pressure increase along
the axis of symmetry. However, this turbulence does not seem to be
able to diffuse ahead of the ﬂame, because the generation of
turbulence is accompanied by the acceleration of the burned gas
and, therefore, the downstream convection of the turbulence by the
high speed ﬂow of the products appears to overwhelm the diffusion
of the turbulence toward the leading point. Accordingly, the dis-
cussed ﬂame-generated turbulence cannot affect ﬂame propaga-
tion in the same way as the incoming turbulence.
Thus, the elementary case discussed above involves all the key
phenomena (ﬂame-generated turbulence and vorticity, counter-
gradient scalar transport, enhanced ﬂame speed), commonly
associated with the inﬂuence of combustion on turbulence. From
the above discussion it is also clear that all these phenomena are
driven by the same physical mechanism, i.e. the uneven pressure
ﬁeld induced by the curved ﬂame front and, hence, the vorticity
generation by the ﬂame.

speed enhancement (SL

The above discussion shows that the inﬂuence of heat release on
ﬂow can accelerate a ﬂame due to pressure forces generated by
vorticity, but the magnitude of the effect is limited by the velocity
OðSL

mechanisms discussed in Section 5.3, i.e. damping of ﬂamelet
instabilities by turbulent stretching provided by larger-scale eddies
and a low rate of the growth of unstable perturbations. All in all, the
ﬂame-generated hydrodynamic effects, particularly the increase in
